<HI>UPARICARAVASU.
<NI>{%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u in the following order:
<>Atri--Candra--Budha--Puru1ravas--A1yus--Nahus2a [Page809-a+ 65]
<>-Yaya1ti--Pu1ru--Janamejaya--Pra1cinva1n--Pravi1ra
<>Namasyu--Vi1tabhaya--S4un2d2u--Bahuvidha--Sam3ya1ti
<>-Rahova1di1--Raudra1s4va--Matina1ra--Santurodha
<>-Dus2yanta--Bharata--Suhotra--Suhota1--Gala
<>-Garda--Suketu--Br2hatks2etra--Hasti--Ajami1d2ha--R2ks2a-
<>Sam3varan2a-Kuru-Sudhanva1-Sugotra-Cyavana-Kr2ti-
<>Uparicaravasu.
<NI>2) {%How he got the name Uparicaravasu.%} Once Vasu built
<>a hermitage and began to perform penance in accord-
<>ance with the advice of Indra. When the penance
<>became too severe Indra appeared and gave him an
<>aerial chariot. Since then Vasu used to travel through
<>air in the chariot. So he got the name ‘Uparicaravasu’
<>(Vasu, who travels above). (Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva,
<>Chapter 63).
<NI>3). {%Vasu and Indra festival.%} Indra gave Vasu a garland
<>(Vaijayanti1ma1la1) which would never fade, and gave a
<>boon that, so long as he wore that garland, he would
<>not receive any wound. He was given a bamboo stick
<>(Ven2udan2d2a) and was instructed to fix that rod on
<>the earth and celebrate the festival of Indra. Vasu
<>celebrated the festival of Indra and from that day on-
<>wards the festival of Indra came to be celebrated in the
<>world. (See under Indrotsava).
<NI>4). {%The sons of Vasu.%} Five sons named Br2hadratha,
<>Kus4a1mba (Man2iva1hana), Mavella, Yadu and Ra1janya
<>were born to him.
<NI>5). {%Kicking and splitting Kola1hala.%} The river S4ukti-
<>mati1 flowed through the capital city of Vasu. Once the
<>great hill Kola1hala grew amorous of S4uktimati1 and
<>caught hold of her. Uparicaravasu got angry at this
<>and kicked the mountain which was split into two and
<>S4uktimati1 flowed out of the gap. Still two children
<>were born to the great hill by S4uktimati1. The river
<>was pleased at the freedom accorded by the king and
<>placed the two children at the feet of the king. They
<>grew up. The son became general of the army and the
<>daughter Girika1 became the wife of the king. (M.B.
<>A1di Parva Chapter 63).
<NI>6). {%Vasu goes ahunting.%} One day the king decided to
<>go to hunt. On the same day his wife had her monthly
<>course. Still without changing his programme he started
<>for the forest. Even after entering the forest the thought
<>of his wife lingered in his mind. The forest was in full
<>bloom as it was Spring. Seeing this the king grew
<>amorous and sat under a tree. A mild breeze was blowing.
<>Instantly the king had seminal flow. He collected the
<>semen in the leaf of a tree and sent it to the queen
<>through an eagle. Thinking that to be some food, an-
<>other eagle got near and a quarrel arose. The semen
<>fell in the river Yamuna1. A fish named Adrika1 swall-
<>owed it. (See under Adrika1). A fisherman caught hat
<>fish. He got a male child and a female child from the
<>stomach of the fish. That girl is the famous Satyavati1
<>Matsyagandha1 who became the queen of S4antanu. (See
<>under Satyavati1). The fisherman gave the boy he got
<>from the fish to king Uparicaravasu. (M.B. A1di Parva
<>Chapter 62).

<NI>(iii). In Maha1bha1rata, S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 337,

<>entered the world of Brahma1.
<NI>(iv). Once a controversy arose between Indra and
<>the hermits, whether cow-slaughter during sacrifices,

<>to come there. As he was a man of truth, he was asked
<>to make a decision. Uparicaravasu stood on the side

<HI>UPAVA1SA. Upava1sa means going back from sin and
<>leading a good life. (Upa1 (varta) = go back and Va1sa =
<>A life). All actions which are not good, must be relin-
<>quished. Those who observe Upava1sa should abstain
<>from using flesh, Masu1ra (pulse), can2aka (a kind of
<>gram), Varaku (a kind of grain), green leaves prepared),
<>honey, rice etc. and from contact with women. He
<>should not wear flowers, ornaments, or fashionable
<>dress; should not inhale fragrant smoke, and fragrance
<>of any sort. Cleaning the teeth and using collyrium
<>also are prohibited. Instead of cleaning the teeth in
<>the morning Pan5cagavya (Milk, curd, ghee, urine and
<>dung of cow) should be taken in. Drinking water
<>several times, using betel leaves, sleeping in the day
<>time and sexual act also should be avoided. (Agni
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 175).
<HI>UPAVEN2A1. A river. This river is considered to be the
<>mother of Agni (fire). Some are of opinion that this
<>river is Kr2s2n2aven2a1 a tributary of the great river Kr2s2n2a1
<>(R. Kistna) of South India. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter
<>222, Stanza 14).
<HI>UPAVI1TA. The sacred string or Uttari1ya. The twice-
<>born (the three upper castes) wear this. When it is
<>worn over the left shoulder and under the right arm it
<>is called Upavi1ta, when it is worn over the right shoul-
<>der and under the left arm it is called Pra1ci1na1vi1ta and
<>if it is worn around the neck as a garland it is called
<>Nivi1ta. (Manusmr2ti, Chapter 2 Stanza 63). [Page810-a+ 64]
<HI>UPA1VR2TTA (M) A country in Bha1rata. (M.B. Bhi1s2ma
<>Parva, Chapter 91, Stanza 84).
<HI>UPA1YA (S). See under Caturupa1ya.
<HI>UPAYA1JA. Brother of the hermit Ya1ja. Both of them
<>lived on the banks of the Gan3ga1. Dhr2s2t2adyumna and
<>Pa1n5ca1li1 were born to the King Drupada of Pa1n5ca1la as
<>the fruit of the sacrifices performed by these hermits.
<>The story is given below:
<NI>The famous teacher Dron2a was the son of the hermit
<>Bharadva1ja, who was a friend of the King Pr2s2ata of
<>Pa1n5ca1la. So King Pr2s2ata sent his son Drupada to the
<>hermitage of Bharadva1ja for education. Thus Dron2a
<>and Drupada were fellow students.
<NI>After completing his education Drupada became King
<>of Pa1n5ca1la. At that time the teacher Dron2a once visited
<>the palace of the King. But Drupada did not duly
<>receive his former classmate. Dron2a got angry at this
<>and went to Hastina1pura and became the teacher of the
<>Pa1n2d2avas and the Kauravas. As a remuneration
<>for teaching them Dron2a demanded that Arjuna should
<>bind Drupada and bring him before him. Arjuna did
<>so. Drupada gave Dron2a a portion of his kingdom and
<>got his liberty. From that day onwards Drupada wished
<>for a son who would take revenge on Dron2a, and
<>requested the hermit Upaya1ja to perform a sacrifice for
<>getting a son. At first the hermit refused. The King
<>served the hermit for a year. The hermit was pleased
<>and asked the King to invite Ya1ja for the sacrifice. The
<>King did as he was told and Ya1ja and Upaya1ja came
<>to Pa1n5ca1la and performed the sacrifice for getting a
<>son. From the sacrificial dais Dhr2s2t2adyumna and
<>Pa1n5ca1li1 were born. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 166

<HI>U1RDHVAPUN2D2RA. A mark worn on the forehead
<>with yellow ochre. This mark should be made by earth
<>or ochre, which should be taken from the top of a
<>mountain peak, banks of rivers, seashore, S4aivite temple
<>serpent-hill or from under a holy basil. The fruits of
<>wearing the mark will vary according to the colour of
<>the ochre. Black ochre will afford peace, red soil will
<>procure docility, yellow soil will get prosperity, and
<>white ochre will give duty or righteousness. As the fruits
<>vary according to the colour, so also it will vary accord-
<>ing to the finger used to put the sign. If the mark is [Page810-b+ 65]
<>made with the thumb the man will become stout. If it
<>is done with the middle finger he will have long life. If
<>it is done with the fourth finger (ana1mika1), he will get
<>food and if it is made with the index finger (tarjani1) he
<>will get salvation. The form of the mark also could be
<>changed. The mark may be made in the form of a
<>flame, the leaf of bamboo reed, a lotus-bud, a fish, a
<>turtle or a conch. If the mark is as large as ten finger-
<>breadths it is superfine. If of nine finger-breadths, it is
<>medium super-fine; and if it is eight and a half finger-
<>breadths, it is lower superfine. In the same way if the
<>finger-breadth of the mark is seven, six or five they will
<>be super medium, medium medium and lower medium.
<>If it is four, three and two they will be of the low,
<>medium low and lowest.
<NI>It is also ordered that when one wears the U1rdhva-
<>pun2d2ra in a particular spot of the body one will have to
<>think of a particular name of Vis2n2u.
<NI>“Kes4ava in the forehead, Na1ra1yan2a in the stomach,
<>Ma1dhava in the heart, Govinda in the neck, Vis2n2u on
<>the right side of the stomach, Madhusu1dana on the
<>middle of right hand, Trivikrama on the left ear,
<>Va1mana on the left side of stomach, S4ri1dhara on the
<>left hand, Hr2s2i1kes4a on the right ear. Padmana1bha on
<>the hinder Part, Da1modara on the nape and Va1sudeva
<>on the head, should be meditated upon.” (Devi1 Bha1ga-
<>vata, Skandha 11).

<HI>URVAS4I1 I. A famous celestial damsel.
<NI>1) {%Birth of Urvas4i1.%} In days of old two hermits named Nara
<>and Na1ra1yan2a did penance to Brahma1 in the holy her-
<>mitage of Badarika1 for a thousand years. (Nara and
<>Na1ra1yan2a were the children born to Dharma, the son of
<>Brahma1). Because of the severity of their penance Indra
<>was struck with fear. Thinking that they were doing
<>this severe penance with a view to become Indra, he
<>approached the hermits and told them that they might
<>ask for any boon. Even though Indra told them several
<>times, they did not speak a word nor did they make any
<>stir. Indra's fear increased. So Indra decided to create
<>some delusions which would arouse in them fear, desire
<>etc., so that their penance might be broken. He began
<>to bring around them wild animals such as lion, elephant
<>wild snake etc. and natural phenomena such as storm, [Page811-b+ 64]
<>heavy rain, forest-fire etc. to terrify them. The efforts
<>of Indra were futile. The disappointed Indra sent for
<>Ka1madeva (Cupid) and consulted him, as a result of
<>which Ka1madeva and his wife Ratidevi1 with many
<>celestial maids came to the hermitage in the mountain
<>of Gandhama1dana with the intention of hindering the
<>penance of Narana1ra1yan2as. The season of spring was
<>created in the forest. The celestial women such as
<>Rambha1 and others came before the hermits and began
<>to sing and dance. The hearts of the hermits began to
<>swell with passion. When they opened their eyes what
<>they saw was a beautiful sight. The famous celestial
<>women, Menaka1, Rambha1, Tilottama1, Sukes4ini1, Mano-
<>rama1, Mahes4vari1, Pus2pagandha1, Pramadvara1, Ghr2ta1ci1,
<>Candraprabha1, Soma1, Vidyunma1la1, Ambuja1ks2i1, Ka1n5ca-
<>nama1la1, and others with their ten thousand and eighty
<>hand-maids stood before them. Hermit Na1ra1yan2a who
<>got terribly angry struck on his thigh with his hand
<>and instantly there arose a woman of extreme beauty.
<>Because she had originated from the U1ru (thigh) of
<>Na1ra1yan2a, that woman, who was the most beautiful
<>in the three worlds, got the name Urvas4i1. All the others
<>were struck with wonder at the sight of this new creat-
<>ion. After that so many other beautiful women also
<>were created. The hermit Na1ra1yan2a gave all of them
<>to Indra. With shame Indra accepted them and return-
<>ed to heaven along with them. Thus Urvas4i1 arrived
<>at the realm of the devas (gods). (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 4).
<NI>2) {%Position of Urvas4i1.%} It is mentioned in Maha1bha1rata
<>that Urvas4i1 had the eleventh place among the singers.
<>The expert dancers were Anu1ca1na1, Adrika1, Somakes4i1,
<>Mis4ra1, Alambus2a1, Mari1ci, S4ucika1, Vidyutparn2a1, Tilot-
<>tama1, Ambika1, Ks2ema1, Rambha1, Suba1hu, Asita1,
<>Supriya1, Pun2d2ari1ka1, Sugandha1, Surasa1, Prama1thini1,
<>Ka1mya1 and S4a1radvati1. Urvas4i1 got the first place among
<>the celestial maids in beauty. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter
<>123).
<NI>3) {%Urvas4i1 and Puru1ravas.%} Puru1ravas was the son of Budha
<>born of Ila1. He grew up and became a king of great
<>renown. His fame reached even the realm of the gods.
<>One day during that period Brahma1 cursed Urvas4i1. “Go
<>and be born on the earth.” (It is stated in the Devi1
<>Bha1gavata that it was Brahma1 who cursed Urvas4i1 and in
<>the Bha1gavata that it was the Mitra1varun2as who cursed
<>Urvas4i1). Urvas4i1 had heard about the fame of Puru1ravas
<>and had felt tender love for him. The celestial maid
<>reached the earth. She went to the palace of Puru1ravas
<>and saw him. Because of the perfection of their figures,
<>both loved mutually. The king asked her to become
<>his wife. She agreed. But she laid down three conditions.
<NI>(i) I have with me two lambs which I bring up as my
<>sons. You must take care of them. No harm should
<>befall them.
<NI>(ii) I take in only ghee. On no account should you
<>compel me to eat any other food.
<NI>(iii) Don't come near me in nudity except at the time
<>of coition.
<NI>The king agreed to all these conditions. From that day
<>onwards Urvas4i1 lived in the palace of the king as his
<>wife. They lived happily for a long while without
<>separating from each other.
<NI>Urvas4i1 became conspicuous by her absence in the realm
<>of the gods. She was the most expert actress in heaven. [Page812-a+ 65]
<>Indra missed her. So a search was made and they found
<>out that she was in the palace of Puru1ravas. Indra
<>asked the Gandharvas to bring that celestial woman to
<>heaven somehow. Vis4va1vasu and some others of the
<>Gandharvas reached the palace of king Puru1ravas and
<>waited for an opportunity. One midnight they stole
<>the lambs and took them away through the sky. At
<>that time the king was with Urvas4i1 in the harem.
<>Urvas4i1 heard the cry of the lambs and she was flurried.
<>She reviled the king who was not capable of taking care
<>of two lambs. Hearing her harsh words the king took
<>his bow and arrow and following the cry of the lambs
<>he was about to chase the thieves. Taking this oppor-
<>tunity the Gandharvas caused a lightning to flash in the
<>king's harem. In the light of the lightning Urvas4i1 saw
<>the king standing nude. The Gandharvas having achi-
<>eved their task had left the lambs and departed before
<>the king had got out of the room. The king caught
<>hold of the lambs and returned to the harem, within a
<>short while. But Urvas4i1 had gone out of the palace
<>and was on her way.
<NI>The king was full of grief. He wandered all over the
<>country in search of Urvas4i1. At last he reached Kuru-
<>ks2etra and saw Urvas4i1 there. He bowed low before
<>her and implored her to return to the palace. But
<>Urvas4i1 replied thus:--
<NI>“Women are like wolves. Don't have alliance with them
<>Oh King! Kings should not put faith in women and
<>thieves.”
<NI>Saying thus, Urvas4i1 vanished. (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 1).
<NI>4) {%The reason why Puru1ravas had to remain separate from Urvas4i1.%}
<>Puru1ravas had once gone to heaven, in accordance with
<>the invitation of Indra to help the Gods in the battle
<>with the asuras. Indra had killed Ma1ya1dhara, a noble
<>Asura, in that battle and had celebrated a festival. In
<>that festival Rambha1 was dancing before Tumburu, the
<>sage-priest, and detecting some flaw in her dance Puru1-
<>ravas teased her. Rambha1 retorted by asking the King
<>what he knew about dance, and the king replied that he
<>had learned from Urvasi1 more dance than Tumburu the
<>teacher of Rambha1. Tumburu got angry at this, and
<>cursed the king Puru1ravas to suffer from the pangs of
<>separation from Urvas4i1. Stricken with grief, Puru1ravas
<>returned to his palace. It was after this that the Gan-
<>dharvas took away Urvas4i1. Puru1ravas went to Badari-
<>ka1s4rama and performed penance, meditating on God
<>Vis2n2u for the nullification of the curse. Urvas4i1, aggri-
<>eved by separation from her husband, sat motionless as
<>a picture, in the custody of the Gandharvas. Lord Vis2n2u
<>was pleased with the penance of Puru1ravas. The Gan-
<>dharvas brought her back to the King. Thus it became
<>possible for the king to meet Urvas4i1 at least once a year.
<>(Katha1saritsa1gara, La1va1n2akalambaka, Taran3ga 1).
<NI>5) {%The sons born to Puru1ravas by Urvas4i1.%} The king became
<>very sad when Urvas4i1 was about to depart from him at
<>Kuruks2etra. Seeing his grief Urvas4i1 told him. “Oh
<>King, if you want so much to live with me, you have to
<>worship the Gandharvas. They will be pleased and
<>will give me to you. Don't be sorry. Now I am preg-
<>nant. Come to this place at the end of a year. We
<>can spend that night together. Then we will get another
<>son also that night.” Pleased at what Urvas4i1 had
<>said, the king returned to his palace. On completion
<>of a year Puru1ravas went to Kuruks2etra and spent a [Page812-b+ 65]
<>night with Urvas4i1. Urvas4i1 gave Puru1ravas a very
<>beautiful child and then she vanished. The king sat
<>there and praised the Gandharvas, who gave the king
<>an Agnistha1li1 (a fire pot). Because of his derangement,
<>thinking it to be Urvas4i1 the king took the stha1li1 (pot)
<>and wandered about in the forest. At last placing the
<>pot in the forest, the king returned to his palace. On
<>that day Treta1yuga (one of the four ages of the word)
<>commenced, and the Vedas dawned in his mind as
<>three in number. He returned to the place where
<>he had left the Stha1li1 in the forest and took it. After
<>that he made two ‘aran2is’ (wood from which fire is
<>kindled by attrition) out of a banyan tree and placed his
<>body between them and made fire. That fire is called
<>‘Ja1tavedas.’ Thus Ja1tavedas became the son of
<>Puru1ravas.
<NI>Puru1ravas generated three fires from Ja1tavedas. The
<>first of them is Pran2ava. The second is called Na1ra1yan2a
<>and the third Agnivarn2a. (S4ri1mad Bha1gavata, 9th
<>S4kandha).
<NI>To Puru1ravas six sons were born from the womb of
<>Urvas4i1. They were A1yus, S4ruta1yus, Satya1yus, Raya,
<>Vijaya and Jaya. (A little difference is observed in these
<>names in the Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 75,
<>Stanzas 24 and 25).
<NI>6) To know how a Gandharva named Durmada and
<>a Vidya1dhari1 named Unmada1 played a trick on Puru1-
<>ravas and Urvas4i1, see under Unmada1.
<NI>7) {%How Agastya and Vasis2t2ha were born to Mitra1varun2as be-
<>cause of Urvas4i1.%} In days of old there was a renowned
<>king named Nimi in the family of Iks2va1ku. He decided
<>to perform a sacrifice of a long duration. He began
<>preparation and invited famous hermits such as Bhr2gu,
<>An3giras, Va1madeva, Pulastya, Pulaha. R2ci1ka and others.
<>Finally he invited Vasis2t2ha. At that time Indra had
<>begun another sacrifice in heaven. So Vasis2t2ha went to
<>heaven for that sacrifice, telling Nimi that he would
<>conduct the sacrifice on his return. The emperor Nimi
<>did not like it. Recognizing the hermit Gautama as
<>the main priest, he began performing the sacrifice.
<>The sacrifice of Indra lasted for 500 years. When Vasis2-
<>t2ha returned after that, the sacrifice of Nimi was over.
<>Vasis2t2ha cursed Nimi. “You will become devoid of
<>body.” Nimi cursed back. “Let Vasis2t2ha also become
<>thus.”
<NI>The aggrieved Vasis2t2ha went to his father Brahma1 and
<>complained about the curse. Brahma1 told Vasis2t2ha.
<>“You penetrate the brightness of Mitra1varun2as and
<>stay there. In due course you will get a birth which is
<>not of a womb.” Hearing these words, Vasis2t2ha reached
<>the hermitage of Mitra1varun2as. He left his body there
<>and fused himself into their effulgence. During this
<>period Urvas4i1 came to that hermitage. Seeing her,
<>Mitra1varun2as had seminal flow. The semen fell in a
<>pot. From the pot two bright and handsome sons were
<>born. The first of them was Agastya and the second
<>Vasis2t2ha. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Skandha 8).
<NI>8) {%Indra cursing Urvas4i1.%} Once Agastya went to the dur-
<>bar of Indra. On the occasion Indra asked Urvas4i1 to
<>dance. In the midst of the dance she saw Jayanta, the
<>son of Indra, and became amorous and her steps went
<>wrong. Na1rada who was playing his famous lute called
<>Mahati1 could not play well. Agastya got angry and
<>cursed Jayanta to become a bud. He cursed Na1rada
<>also. So his lute became the lute of the world. Urvas4i1 [Page813-a+ 65]
<>was born on the earth under the name Ma1dhavi1 due to
<>the curse.

<HI>US4I1NARA I. A famous king of the Candravam3s4a (Lunar
<>dynasty). [Page813-b+ 65]
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u in the following
<>order:-Brahma1-Atri-Candra-Budha-Puru1ravas-A1yus-
<>Nahus2a-Yaya1ti-Anudruhyu-Sabha1nara-Ka1lanara-Sr2n5-
<>jaya-Us4i1nara. Us4i1nara was the father of S4ibi and Vena.
<NI>2) {%The reason for the curse of the eight Vasus.%} Once the
<>As2t2avasus (Vasus eight in number) came to the hermitage
<>of Vasis2t2ha, with their wives. The wife of Dyau, one
<>of the As2t2avasus, saw the cow ‘Nandini1’ in the hermit-
<>age. She and the daughter of Us4i1nara were intimate
<>friends. She wanted to get Nandini1, so that she
<>might give it as a present to the daughter of Us2i1nara.
<>According to her wish the As2t2avasus caught hold of
<>the cow and took her home. At that time Vasis2t2ha had
<>been away. When he returned, he knew about the
<>theft of the cow and cursed the As2t2avasus to take birth
<>in the wombs of women on earth. It was according to
<>this curse that the As2t2avasus took birth in Gan3ga1devi1
<>the wife of S4antanu. See under Bhi1s2ma. (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 2).
<NI>3) {%Indra testing Us4i1nara.%} Us4i1nara was a just and liberal-
<>minded King. Indra decided to test the King regard-
<>ing his liberality. He got Agnideva (fire-god) for his
<>help. Indra took the form of a Kite and Agni that of
<>a dove. Both flew down to Us4i1nara as if the Kite was
<>chasing the dove. Us4i1nara agreed to protect the dove.
<>The Kite argued with the King that it was not meet
<>and right on his part to rob him of his lawful food.
<>The King agreed to give any other kind of flesh to the
<>Kite. But the Kite would have none of them. Finally
<>the Kite agreed to accept the flesh of the King instead,
<>equal to the weight of the dove. The King cut more
<>and more flesh from his body, but it could not weigh
<>equal to the dove. At last the King himself got into
<>the balance. Then the gods had pity on the King and
<>the Kite and the dove appeared in their real form and
<>blessed the King and then they returned to heaven.
<>(M.B. Aran2ya Parva, Chapter 131). In Maha1bha1rata
<>a story like this occurs about King S4ibi also.

<HI>UTSAVA. Celebrations conducted in temples from
<>olden days. There are S4a1straic (scriptural) injunctions
<>as regards conducting utsavas. Utsava is an indispens-
<>able celebration when once the deity (idol) is in-
<>stalled in the temple. Utsava should be celebrated for
<>one day, three days or seven days in the very same
<>month in which the deity was installed, because non-
<>celebration of Utsava will render the installation in-
<>effective. Utsava should be conducted either during
<>Uttara1yan2a (movement of the sun from south to North)
<>or Vis2u (when the Sun is in the centre) or at a time
<>suitable to the temple authorities who conduct the
<>utsava in S4ayana, Upavana or Gr2ha. It should com-
<>mence with auspicious ceremonies like the sowing of
<>seeds of nine varieties of foodgrains, and with dance,
<>song, instrumental music etc.

<HI>UTTAN3KA (UTAN3KA). An ideal disciple of Veda
<>who was the disciple of A1podadhaumya.
<NI>1) {%Uttan3ka and the Guru's wife.%} After entrusting manage-
<>ment of the a1s4rama to Uttan3ka, Veda once went out
<>on a tour of the country, and Uttan3ka stayed in the
<>A1s4rama carrying out the instructions of the Guru. Then
<>came the menstrual period of Veda's wife, and his other
<>wives requested Uttan3ka to do the needful, so that the
<>fertile period of their co-wife was not wasted. Uttan3ka's
<>reply to them was as follows:- “Asked by women, I
<>shall not do this improper act; and the preceptor has
<>not asked me to do such a thing though it might be
<>improper.”
<NI>His Guru, A1podadhaumya returned to the A1s4rama some
<>time after this, and was very pleased to hear about the
<>above incident. He blessed Uttan3ka. (M.B. A1di1 Parva,
<>Chapter 3).
<NI>2) {%His tuition fee.%} His education being over, it was now
<>time for Uttan3ka to give due offerings--tuition fee to his
<>guru, and Uttan3ka enquired of the guru as to what he
<>would have by way of daks2in2a1. The guru asked
<>Uttan3ka to get his wife's instruction in the matter and
<>act accordingly. Because of the aforesaid incident
<>(Uttan3ka not acting on the guru's wife during her
<>menstrual period) she had a grouse against him, and
<>therefore, asked him to bring as his tuition fee the ear-
<>rings worn by the Ks2atriya wife of King Paus2a.
<NI>Uttan3ka set out to procure the ear-rings. On the
<>way he saw an ox, and on its back a big man, who
<>called out to Uttan3ka to eat the excretion of the ox.
<>When Uttan3ka hesitated to do so, the man on the ox
<>asked him again to eat it, pointing out that his guru,
<>Veda, had done it. Then did Uttan3ka consume both
<>the excretion and urine of the ox, and did his oblat-
<>ions, in his perplexed mood, standing. Uttan3ka arrived
<>at the palace of King Paus2a and told him about the
<>object of his visit. The King agreed to it. But, when
<>Uttan3ka entered the Zenana Paus2a's Ks2atriya wife
<>could not be seen. When Uttan3ka told the King about
<>this, he remained thoughtful for some time and told
<>the former that he might surely have become impure
<>due to performance of oblations in the wrong manner,
<>and that his wife would not be visible, due to her
<>chastity, to the eyes of such impure folk. Uttan3ka
<>admitted to having, on his way, performed oblations
<>not in the proper manner, and then he sat down
<>facing the east after washing his hands, feet etc. and
<>performed oblation in the correct manner. Then did the
<>wife of King Paus2a become visible to him, and she, pleas-
<>ed at his humility, handed over her ear-rings to him.
<>She also advised him to be very careful about the ear-
<>rings as they had once been coveted by Taks2aka, King
<>of the serpents. Bidding adieu to the queen, Uttan3ka
<>saw the King and they sat together to take food. Not
<>only was the rice cold; but a hair was also found in it.
<>Uttan3ka cursed the King and rendered him blind,
<>and the King, in turn, cursed Uttan3ka not to have [Page815-b+ 64]
<>children. But, both of them became calm quite soon
<>and withdrew their curses.
<NI>On his way back to the A1s4rama with the ear-rings, he
<>had only just stepped into a pool for a wash, when a
<>naked Buddha sannya1sin, who was following him,
<>snatched and ran off with the ear-rings. It was really
<>Taks2aka disguised as the sannya1sin who thus snatched
<>the ear-rings. Uttan3ka chased him and caught him
<>when he assumed his original form of the serpent King
<>and disappeared into a cave. Uttan3ka waited at the
<>mouth of the cave stupefied, when Indra deputed his
<>Vajra1yudha to help Uttan3ka. The Vajra1yudha cut a
<>tunnel-like route to Pa1ta1la from the earth and through
<>the cave Uttan3ka reached Pa1ta1la by this route and
<>found out the palace of Taks2aka.
<NI>Though Uttan3ka praised and cajoled the Na1gas (ser-
<>pents) their King Taks2aka did not make his appear-
<>ance. Uttan3ka saw there two women, who had been
<>weaving clothes, and on one machine there were black
<>and white threads. The machine had one wheel with
<>twelve spokes and was turned by six boys. Also was
<>there a horse, and a man. When Uttan3ka praised them,
<>the horse and the man approached him. The man
<>advised Uttan3ka to blow through the anus of the horse
<>so that the snakes could be brought round. Uttan3ka
<>did so, when lo! from all the pores on the body of the
<>horse, flames of fire began sprouting out. When the
<>Na1galoka (country of serpents) got thus filled with fire
<>and flames, Taks2aka lost his nerves and came out with
<>the stolen ear-rings, which Uttan3ka received back. But,
<>that was the last day on which the rings had been
<>promised to be given to his guru's wife by Uttan3ka,
<>who remained there gloomy and sorrow-stricken at the
<>thought that he would not be able to travel all the
<>distance before sunset and give the ear-rings to the
<>guru's wife. Then the horseman gave the horse to
<>Uttan3ka, who reached the A1s4rama just in time when
<>the guru's wife was about to curse Uttan3ka for not
<>returning with the rings though it was nearly dusk time.
<>Now the guru and his wife blessed Uttan3ka, who de-
<>tailed to them his experiences since leaving the A1s4rama
<>in quest of the ear-rings. Then the guru spoke thus to
<>Uttan3ka--“The two women whom you found engaged
<>in weaving were Dha1ta1 and Vidha1ta1, the white and
<>black strings, day and night, and the six boys who
<>turned the wheel with twelve spokes were the six
<>seasons. The ox seen by you on the way was Aira1vata
<>(Indra's elephant), the horse seen in Pa1ta1la was Agni-
<>deva, and the horseman, Indra. The excretion you ate
<>on your way was nectar and that was the reason why
<>you did not die in the Na1galoka. Indra who is my
<>friend blessed you because of mercy towards you, my
<>disciple. You could bring back the ear-rings also be-
<>cause of Indra's blessings.”
<NI>Then the guru blessed Uttan3ka and the latter took
<>leave of thim. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 3).
<NI>3) {%Uttan3ka and Janamejaya's serpent yajn5a.%} Uttan3ka,
<>determined on taking vengeance on Taks2aka, went
<>away directly from the a1s4rama to Hastina1pura, where
<>he met Janamejaya and told him the fact that it was
<>Taks2aka, who had bitten to death Pari1ks2it, his (Janame-
<>jaya) father. It was thus prompted by Uttan3ka to take
<>revenge upon Taks2aka that Janamejaya performed the
<>serpent yajn5a. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 3). [Page816-a+ 65]
<NI>4) {%Uttan3ka and Dhundhuma1ra.%} See under Dhundhuma1ra.
<NI>5) {%Uttan3ka attained salvation.%} Uttan3ka was put up at a
<>place called Ujja1laka. When he became old, he went
<>about visiting many temples. During this tour one day
<>Uttan3ka saw a hunter in the forest called Gulika
<>attempting to steal the golden plates on the dome of
<>the Vis2n2u temple in Sauvi1ra. Gulika raised his sword to
<>kill Uttan3ka, when he told the former that he would
<>have to suffer the results of sin during many births
<>if he committed a sin. This admonition of Uttan3ka so
<>touched the heart of the hunter that he fell dead on the
<>spot with a repentant heart. Uttan3ka sprinkled Gan3ga1
<>water on the corpse of the hunter, who attained Vai-
<>kun2t2ha (abode of Vis2n2u). On the advice of Vis2n2u,
<>Uttan3ka went to Badari1 where he did tapas and also
<>attained Vaikun2t2ha. (Na1radi1ya Pura1n2a).
<NI>In Chapters 53-58 of As4vamedha Parva of Maha1-
<>bha1rata is related the story of one Uttan3ka muni, under
<>the caption ‘Utan3kopa1khya1na.’ In Maha1bha1rata
<>(original) he is referred to as Uttan3ka; but in its
<>Malaya1lam rendering he is called Utan3ka. There are
<>no substantial differences between the story of Uttan3ka
<>and that of Utan3ka, Veda's disciple. But, since, there
<>are differences, in certain aspects some information
<>about the Uttan3ka of the ‘Utan3kopa1khya1na’ is given
<>below--
<NI>1) {%General.%} Uttan3ka was a disciple of Gautama, the
<>husband of Ahalya1. The guru had more love for
<>Uttan3ka than for his other disciples, and therefore did
<>not permit the latter to leave the A1s4rama even after
<>the other disciples were sent away. Uttan3ka became
<>old; wrinkles attacked his body and greyness his head.
<NI>2) {%Tuition fee (Gurudaks2in2a1).%} Uttan3ka once returned
<>from the woods with firewood, in a very tired condition.
<>The daughter of the guru shed tears at the sight.
<>Gautama called Uttan3ka to him and asked him about
<>the cause of his grief and sorrow. Uttan3ka replied that
<>the hairs on his head turned grey on account of sorrow
<>that he was not permitted to leave the A1s4rama although
<>it was hundred years since he had gone there first as
<>disciple. Then Gautama permitted Uttan3ka to quit the
<>A1s4rama. Uttan3ka asked Gautama what he should
<>offer as tuition fee. Gautama consulted his wife on the
<>topic and she suggested as tuition fee the two ear-rings
<>of gems worn by the wife of King Sauda1sa, who used
<>to eat the flesh of man. Immediately Uttan3ka set out
<>to fetch the ear-rings and on his way met Sauda1sa in
<>the forest, and told the latter about his mission. Sau-
<>da1sa tried to eat Uttan3ka, who told him that he was
<>in duty bound to obtain the ear-rings for his guru's
<>wife and that, after fulfilling the mission, he would
<>return to Sauda1sa to be eaten up by him. Sauda1sa
<>agreed to the proposition. He directed Uttan3ka to his
<>wife Madayanti1, who spoke to him thus--“Devas and
<>Mahars2is covet these ear-rings. If I place them on the
<>ground, serpents will steal them; if handed over to
<>Ucchis2t2a, Yaks2as will steal them, and if the watchman
<>sleeps, Devas will snatch them off. Therefore, you
<>should bring some token for me to believe that you are
<>deputed by my husband.” Accordingly Uttan3ka went
<>back to Sauda1sa and returned to his wife with a token
<>from him, and Madayanti1 handed over her ear-rings to
<>Uttan3ka. Being told by Sauda1sa that Uttan3ka need
<>not return again to him, Uttan3ka started for Gautama's
<>a1s4rama with the ear-rings. Feeling hungry on the way, [Page816-b+ 65]
<>Uttan3ka tied to the branch of a vilva tree the ear-rings
<>bundled in deer skin and ate vilva fruits. Just then the
<>bundle of deer skin got untied and the ear-rings kept in
<>it fell on the ground. A serpent carried off the rings
<>between its teeth and concealed itself in a mole-hill.
<>Highly grieved and upset by the sight, Uttan3ka fell
<>down from the tree. For 35 days Uttan3ka dug the mole-
<>hill. Then Indra felt great sympathy for him, appear-
<>ed on the spot, and passed his Vajra1yudha through the
<>mole-hill. Then the earth burst open and Uttan3ka
<>entered Pa1ta1la through the opening, and a horse, its
<>tail white and the rest of the body black in colour
<>presented itself to him. It asked Uttan3ka to blow
<>through its anus. Uttan3ka did so and the Na1galoka
<>was filled with flames of fire. (The horse was Agnideva).
<>Then did the serpents return the ear-rings to Uttan3ka,
<>who returned with it to Gautama and presented it to
<>him (Gautama) as tuition fee.
<NI>3) {%Uttan3ka about to curse S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a.%} Uttan3ka once went
<>to Dva1raka1, and during their talk was about to curse
<>Kr2s2n2a for not attempting to bring about a compromise
<>between the Kauravas and the Pa1n2d2avas. At once
<>Kr2s2n2a exhibited his Vis4varu1pa (himself as containing
<>the whole universe). Thereupon Uttan3ka saluted Kr2s2n2a,
<>who asked him to request for any boon. He prayed for
<>the boon that water might be made available in any
<>desert he visited. Kr2s2n2a told him that whenever he
<>required water he might successfully think of him.
<NI>4) {%Indra tested Uttan3ka.%} Some time afterwards, while
<>roaming about quite thirsty and tired in a desert,
<>Uttan3ka thought about Kr2s2n2a, and at once there ap-
<>peared before him a naked Can2d2a1la, his body covered
<>with mud. Dogs encircled him. Uttan3ka hesitated to
<>drink the water given to him by this Can2d2a1la, who had
<>some water with him. The Can2d2a1la again insisted on
<>Uttan3ka drinking the water, but he again refused the
<>offer. The can2d2a1la then disappeared with the dogs.
<>Within a few minutes a person with disc, conch, club
<>etc. about him appeared, and Uttan3ka recognised him
<>as S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. Uttan3ka told Kr2s2n2a that it was not
<>proper to give water to a brahmin through a can2d2a1la.
<>Kr2s2n2a's reply to this was as follows:--“I asked Indra to
<>give you nectar instead of water, and he argued that it
<>was not proper that men should be given nectar. I
<>pleaded for you again. Indra replied to this that he
<>himself would, in the guise of a can2d2a1la give nectar to
<>you, and he should not be found fault with, if you
<>refused the nectar. I accepted that proposition of his.
<>But, you did not accept nectar thus offered to you. So,
<>you cannot be given nectar now. But, there will appear
<>water-laden clouds in deserts which you traverse, which
<>would be known as ‘Uttan3ka clouds’ and they would
<>give you sweet water.
<NI>Uttan3ka was pleased, and it is the Uttan3ka clouds,
<>which form as per the above orders of Kr2s2n2a that cause
<>rain to fall, though rarely, in deserts even today.

<HI>VA1GBHAT2A. A Sanskrit scholar who lived in the 12th
<>century A.D. He is the author of the two medical
<>books ‘As2t2a1n3ga-san3graha’ and ‘As2t2a1n3gahr2daya’. Another
<>work called ‘Neminirva1n2a’ is also written by Va1gbhat2a.
<>This work deals with the story of Nemina1tha, a Jain
<>hermit.
<NI>Not much is known about Va1gbhat2a. He was a scholar
<>in rhetorical science. There are certain stories about
<>his writing the medical books. It was a period when the
<>muslims had beaten down the Brahmins. They had
<>taken away the medical science also from them. The
<>Brahmins considered this to be a disgrace to them. They
<>decided to select an intelligent boy and send him to a
<>Muslim physician. They selected Va1gbhat2a. The Brah-
<>mins disguised Va1gbhat2a as a Muslim boy and sent him
<>to the Muslim Physician on the other side of the river.
<>The boy went to the physician and told him that he
<>was coming from far away with the intention of learning
<>medical science. The teacher put certain questions
<>and understood that the boy possessed extraordinary
<>intelligence. He began to teach the boy the science
<>of medicine. Seeing the interest of the boy the teacher
<>asked the boy to eat food from his house and to learn
<>day and night. The Brahmin boy did not like to eat
<>the food of Muslims. The boy said that he had a relative
<>on the other side of the river and that he would go
<>there and have his supper and return for the night study.
<>The teacher agreed and thenceforward Va1gbhat2a
<>began to learn day and night. Within a short time he
<>completed learning.
<NI>One day the teacher was sleeping on the seventh storey
<>of the building and his disciple Va1gbhat2a was massag-
<>ing his legs. The boy soliloquised that fate had destined
<>him to massage the legs of a Musalman. Instantly he
<>cried bitterly, and the teacher awoke and understood
<>that the boy was not a Muslim, and tried to cut his
<>head. The boy thought: “The four Vedas and the six
<>S4a1stras say that there is a god. If it is true no harm
<>will come to me.” Then he jumped out of the window.
<>In consequence of this jump, he became a little lame
<>and no other harm befell him. He swam across the
<>river and reached the other side and told the Brahmins
<>everything. The Brahmins asked him what he imagined
<>when he jumped from the seventh storey. He replied
<>“I jumped with this imagination. The four Vedas and
<>the six S4a1stras say that there is a god. If it is true no
<>harm will come to me.” As soon as the boy had finish-
<>ed the Brahmins became angry and they all got up.
<>They said “You went wrong in using the doubtful ‘if’,
<>instead of the affirmative ‘As.’ The Brahmins joined
<>together and expelled him from the society. The boy
<>thought of going away somewhere. “But there won't be
<>another chance for somebody else to go and learn
<>medical science from the Muslim physicians. So before
<>going away from here I must make the fruits of my
<>efforts available to these people.” Thinking so he lived
<>there for a little longer. He lived there without ming-
<>ling with the Brahmins, cooking his food. It is believed
<>that Va1gbhat2a wrote ‘As2t2a1n3gasan3graha’ and ‘As2t2a1n3ga-
<>hr2daya,’ during this period. [Page818-b+ 64]

<HI>VAIKUN2T2HA III. A Brahmin saint who lived in the

<NI>Vaikun2t2ha once lighted a ghee-lamp in the presence of
<>Vis2n2u in Ka1rttika and returned home. At that time
<>a rat came there and began to drink the ghee. Then
<>the lamp blazed into a flame. The rat was terrified
<>at this, and ran away. But by the grace of Vis2n2u the
<>rat got deliverance from all its sins.
<NI>That rat was killed by snake-bite. The men of Yama
<>came with ropes. Instantly the messengers of Vis2n2u
<>also came on Garud2a. Yama's men got afraid of [Page819-b+ 64]
<>Vis2n2u's messengers and humbly aksed them: “For
<>what goodness of him are you taking this great sinner
<>to Vaikun2t2ha?” They replied: “He had blazed a lamp
<>before the presence of Vis2n2u. That act has earned for
<>him a place in Vaikun2t2ha. The goodness earned by
<>lighting a lamp with devotion and love in Ka1rttika,
<>could be described only by Mukunda.” After that the
<>rat was taken to Vaikun2t2ha.

<>in the R2gveda. The differentiation of castes was not so
<>strict in those days as today. It is not known in what
<>country Dis2t2a and his people Anagas lived. The Kings
<>Karandhama, his son Aviks2it and his son Marutta
<>of this dynasty were great and valiant. Marutta had
<>performed both horse sacrifice (as4vamedha) and impe-
<>rial consecration (Ra1jasu1ya). To Tr2n2abindu, who was
<>in the tenth generation from Marutta, a son was born
<>named Vis4a1la. This Vis4a1la founded a city and lived
<>there. That city is called Vais4a1la1. Many of the scholars
<>are of opinion that this city Vais4a1la1 is the same
<>as Ujjayini1. It is stated in Maha1bha1rata that Soma-
<>datta of the seventh generation from Vis4a1la had per-
<>formed ten as4vamedhas (horse-sacrifices).

<HI>VAIS4VADEVA(M). A sacrifice. It is mentioned in
<>Devi1 Bha1gavata, Skandha 11, that a brahmin should
<>perform this sacrifice to protect himself from hurts
<>caused by oven, threshing stone, axe, cutting knife, and
<>other weapons. This sacrifice could be performed in a
<>cavity for kindling fire on the floor besmeared with
<>cowdung and mud. It should not be done in an
<>ordinary oven meant for cooking, in iron oven, in
<>earthernware or on ordinary floor. As all the deities
<>are having faces of fire, the sacrificial fire should not be
<>kindled by fanning the flame with hand, winnow, hide
<>of black antelope or cloth. By fanning the flame with
<>cloth, the sacrificer will contract disease; by winnowing
<>he would sustain loss of wealth; and death, by fanning
<>with hand. Plums, fruits, roots, curd, ghee etc. could
<>be used as burnt offerings. When these are not avail-
<>able, firewood, roots of herbs, grass etc. could be used
<>instead. Things to be offered as burnt-offerings should
<>be purified, first by sprinkling ghee on them. In the
<>absence of ghee, milk, curd and water may be used.
<>Using things which are unfit as burnt-offerings will
<>invite bad results.
<NI>In Vais4vadeva-sacrifice, half-burnt firewood used in
<>cooking, should never be used. So also salts of any
<>kind. After finishing Vais4vadeva, Gogra1sa (giving rice
<>to cow) also should be done.

<HI>VAJRA1YUDHA (Thunderbolt). The famous weapon
<>of Indra.
<NI>1) {%The making of this weapon.%} In olden days a fierce
<>asura named Vr2tra was born. The Ka1lakeyas and
<>many other asuras became his followers. They began to
<>create havoc in the world, and cause harm to the
<>Devas. At last under the leadership of Indra, the Devas
<>went to Brahma1 and informed him of their grievances
<>and requested for advice as to the way of killing Vr2tra.
<>Brahma1 told them that only with a weapon made of the
<>bone of the hermit Dadhi1ca, could Vr2tra1sura be killed.
<>The Devas went to the bank of the river S4on2a and saw
<>the hermit Dadhi1ca, who was the foremost of munificent
<>men, doing penance there. Indra told him the purpose
<>of their visit. He told the Devas to take his bone, if it
<>was useful to them. Saying this he forsook his body. The
<>Devas took the bones of the hermit and gave them to
<>Vis4vakarma1 who made a powerful weapon with them
<>and gave that to Indra. They named the weapon the
<>‘thunderbolt.’ (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter 100).
<NI>2) {%The face of Subrahman2ya was cut.%} Once Indra was
<>defeated by Naraka1sura. He went to the Hima1layas and
<>hid himself there. Unable to see their King Indra, the
<>devas went to Subrahman2ya. When Indra knew this, he
<>thought that Subrahman2ya had taken possession of the
<>world of the gods. So he came and fought with Su-
<>brahman2ya. The thunderbolt of Indra touched the face
<>of Subrahman2ya and wounded him. From the blood
<>which flowed from the face of Subrahman2ya two noble
<>men appeared who eventually became warriors of
<>Subrahman2ya. (Katha1saritsa1gara, La1va1n2akalambaka,
<>Taran3ga 6). [Page822-a+ 64]
<NI>3) {%Daily thunderbolt.%} In Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 1, Chap-
<>ter 5 and Am3s4a 2, Chapter 8, a process by which
<>thunderbolt is made daily in the evening from the water
<>particles thrown by Brahmins when they recite the spell
<>Ga1yatri1, is described.
<NI>In the evening, the fierce giants called the Mandehas
<>wish to eat the Sun. Praja1pati had given them a curse
<>that though their bodies would not perish they would
<>daily meet with death. So there is a fight between these
<>giants and the sun daily in the evening. At that time
<>the Brahmins recite the spell Ga1yatri1 with the Brahma-
<>pervading syllable ‘OM,’ and throw up water. This
<>water becomes the Vajra weapon, and burns the giant.
<NI>4) {%The thunderbolt became a tiger.%} The hermits Na1rada
<>and Parvata once went to the palace of the King
<>Sr2n5jaya. The King worshipped them and served
<>them for a long time, as a result of which a son was
<>born to him. Indra decided to lessen the superior
<>power of these hermits. Once the son of the King, who
<>was a mere boy, was playing in the forest. At the in-
<>struction of Indra, the thunderbold took the form of a
<>tiger and went to the forest and killed the boy. Sr2n5jaya
<>became very sad. Na1rada and Parvata brought the boy
<>to lite again and gave him to the King. (M.B. S4a1nti
<>Parva, Chapter 30).

<HI>VA1KPA1RUS2YA (Using harsh words). One of the
<>wrongs brought under the rule of chastisement in ancient
<>India. Without caring for the truth or falsity, a man
<>praising another with a view to tease or offend him,
<>is Va1kpa1rusya. The teasing may be aimed at some-
<>body with disabled members of the body or disabled
<>organs of sense. Besides, using heart-rending words also [Page822-b+ 65]
<>comes under this crime. In olden days kings issued
<>orders to fine anybody found guilty of this crime. Ordi-
<>narily the fine was 25 Pan2as. If the crime was committ-
<>ed against one who was below the level of the culprit
<>the fine to be paid was only half. If harsh words were
<>used against other women or people of a higher level
<>the fine was double. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 258).

<HI>VA1LMI1KI I. A hermit who was the first among poets
<>and the author of Ra1ma1yan2a.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} Knowledge about this hermit
<>who was the first among the poets of Bha1rata, is scanty.
<>So we have to depend mainly on some hearsay for the
<>life history of this sage.
<NI>It is said that Va1lmi1ki was the tenth son of Varun2a. But
<>in his younger age he fell into the hands of wicked
<>people and became a wicked man, like his friends. Then
<>his name was not Va1lmi1ki. Being a father, he had to
<>support his family. For this purpose he used to plunder
<>travellers. Once he happened to catch the Saptars2is
<>(the seven hermits) who passed by that way. The
<>hermits asked him whether his wife and children would
<>share the sins he had incurred by plundering. The
<>plunderer could not answer that unexpected question.
<>He ran home and asked his wife and children if they
<>would share the sins incurred by him. They were not
<>prepared to do so. In a moment his life underwent a
<>thorough change. He ran to the Saptars2is and knelt
<>before them. They imparted to the forester, knowledge
<>of the Vedas. The forester sat under a tree and began
<>to sing ‘Ra1ma Ra1ma.’ Days and months and years
<>passed, unknown to him. He did not know that white
<>ants had built a shelter above him. After several years
<>the Saptars2is returned by that way. They broke the [Page823-a+ 65]
<>ant-hill and took the hermit out. Because he came out
<>of Valmi1ka or white-ant-hill he came to be known as
<>Va1lmi1ki. He built his hermitage on the bank of the
<>river Tamasa1 and lived there with his disciples. It was
<>at this place that he composed the famous poem Ra1ma1-
<>yan2a. (For further details see under Ra1ma1yan2a).
<NI>2) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(1) Va1lmi1ki shines in the palace of Indra. (M.B.
<>Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 7, Stanza 16).
<NI>(ii) Mention is made in Maha1bha1rata, Udyoga Parva,
<>Chapter 83, Stanza 27, that Va1lmi1ki met S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a
<>who was going to Hastina1pura as a messenger of the
<>Pa1n2d2avas.
<NI>(iii) Sa1tyaki recited a poem composed by Va1lmi1ki,
<>after having killed Bhu1ris4ravas in the Bha1rata-battle.
<>(M.B. Dron2a Parva, Chapter 143, Stanza 57).
<NI>(iv) Va1lmi1ki told Yudhis2t2hira of the benefits of his
<>devotion to S4iva. (M.B. Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter
<>18, Stanza 8).
<HI>VA1LMI1KI II. One of the prominent sons of Garud2a.
<>(Maha1bha1rata, Udyoga Parva, Chapter 101, Stanza
<>11).
<HI>VA1MA1. An attendant of Subrahman2ya. (M.B. S4alya

<HI>VA1MADEVA. An ancient hermit.
<NI>1) {%Va1madeva and S4ala.%} Three sons named S4ala, Dala
<>and Bala were born to King Pari1ks2it by his wife Sus4o-
<>bhana1, a princess of Man2d2u1ka. In due course, King
<>Pari1ks2it anointed his eldest son S4ala as King and went
<>to the forest for penance.
<NI>Once S4ala went to the forest to hunt. While chasing
<>a deer, the King asked his charioteer to bring horses
<>capable of overtaking the deer. The charioteer told the
<>King that such horses were available at the hermitage
<>of Va1madeva. They went to the hermitage of Va1ma-
<>deva and got the horses on condition that they would
<>be returned.
<NI>After the hunting, S4ala reached his capital. Seeing the
<>beauty and the vigorous nature of the horses, the King
<>did not like to part with them. Va1madeva sent his
<>disciple to the court of the King to take the horses back.
<>But the King sent him back empty-handed. Va1madeva
<>got angry. He came in person and demanded his horses.
<>The King replied that Brahmins did not require such
<>horses. While these two were quarrelling with each
<>other, some fierce giants came there and pierced S4ala
<>with a trident and killed him. (M.B. Vana Parva,
<>Chapter 192).
<NI>2) {%Other information.%}
<NI>(i) He was a friend of Vasis2t2ha and a priest of Das4aratha.
<>(Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1laka1n2d2a. Sarga 7, Stanza 3).
<NI>(ii) Man2d2ala 4 of R2gveda was composed by Va1madeva.
<NI>(iii) Va1madeva was a hermit who had praised the
<>As4vini1devas when he was in his mother's womb.
<>(R2gveda, Man2d2ala 1, Su1kta 119).
<NI>(iv) Once Va1madeva tried to eat the flesh of a dog
<>because of hunger, with a view to save Brahmins.
<>(Manusmr2ti, Chapter 10, Stanza 106).
<NI>(v) He was a prominent member in the assembly of
<>Indra. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 7, Stanza 17).
<NI>(vi) Once Va1madeva gave advice about righteousness
<>to King Vasumanas. (M.B. S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 92).
<HI>VA1MADEVA II. A King. Arjuna defeated this King
<>during his regional conquest of the North. (M.B. Sabha1
<>Parva, Chapter 27, Stanza 11). [Page823-b+ 65]
<HI>VA1MADEVA III. One of the seven sons born to Manu
<>by his wife S4ataru1pa1. It is stated in Matsya Pura1n2a,
<>Chapter 4, that the Brahmin was born from the face,
<>Ks2atriya from the hand, Vais4ya from the calf of the leg
<>and S4u1dra from the foot, of Va1madeva, who was an
<>incarnation of S4iva. This Va1madeva who had five
<>faces and a trident in his hand, fought with Candra,
<>when Ta1ra1 the wife of Br2haspati was carried away by
<>Candra. (Matsya Pura1n2a, 4-13).
<HI>VA1MANA I. An incarnation of Maha1vis2n2u.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} There are ten main incarnations
<>of Maha1vis2n2u. Of these, the incarnation of Va1mana is
<>the fifth.
<NI>2) {%Two Va1manas.%} Two different stories occur in the
<>Pura1n2as about the incarnation of Va1mana. One story
<>is about how Maha1vis2n2u took the incarnation of Va1mana
<>and thrust Maha1bali an asura, down to the nether-
<>world. This story is more widely known. In the second
<>story the place of Maha1bali is given to the asura called
<>Dhundhu. This is the main difference. For a compara-
<>tive study, both stories are given below:
<NI>a) {%The first story of the incarnation of Va1mana.%} The devas
<>(gods) such as Indra and the others were born to Praja1-
<>pati Kas4yapa by his wife Aditi and the asuras (demons)
<>such as Maha1bali and others, by his wife Diti. The
<>Daityas (asuras) began to harm and harass the Devas
<>to a great extent. Once Aditi, the mother of the Devas,
<>complained to Kas4yapa about this. He advised her to
<>take the fast called ‘Payovrata’ fixing the image of
<>Maha1vis2n2u in her heart and that he will take birth
<>through her as Va1mana and destroy Maha1bali. Accord-
<>ing to the advice of her husband, Aditi began to take
<>Payovrata, at the end of which Maha1vis2n2u appeared
<>before her and asked her what her wish was. She told
<>Vis2n2u, of her grievances and Vis2n2u consoled her and
<>said “You have invoked me by your prayer and fast
<>for the safety of your sons. So I will enter your womb
<>through the penance of Kas4yapa and take birth as your
<>son and rescue your sons.”
<NI>Maha1vis2n2u disappeared. Aditi worshipped her husband
<>with devotion. By contemplation Kas4yapa knew every-
<>thing. He infused the energy he had acquired by
<>penance into Aditi, who gave birth to a son. The birth
<>took place on the twelfth day of the month of Bha1dra-
<>pada, in the bright lunar fortnight at the auspicious
<>moment called Abhijit, in the star of S4ra1van2a. The
<>infant had four hands. While Kas4yapa and Aditi were
<>looking on, the infant changed its form and became a
<>dwarfish Brahmin boy. The Devas brought presents
<>to the child. The Sun taught him the spell called Sa1vitri1-
<>mantra. Br2haspati gave the Brahmastring. Kas4yapa
<>gave the string worn round the waist. The earth gave
<>the hide of a black antelope. Soma the Vanaspati gave
<>a rod; Aditi gave the cloth over the privities, the sky
<>an umbrella, the seven hermits gave Kus4a-grass, Brahma1
<>gave a waterpot, Sarasvati1 gave a rosary and Kubera
<>gave a pot.
<NI>At that time, the powerful Maha1bali had brought the
<>three worlds of heaven, earth and Pa1ta1la (the nether-
<>world) under his control. That Asura King performed
<>a horse-sacrifice with the help of a Bha1rgava Brahmin.
<>Hearing about it Va1mana started for the place of sacri-
<>fice. Maha1bali was performing the sacrifice at a place
<>called Bhr2gukacchaka on the North bank of river
<>Narmada1. The priests who were performing the rites [Page824-a+ 65]
<>and ceremonies of the sacrifice saw Va1mana coming
<>slowly to the sacrificial hall. They wondered whether it
<>was the Sun, Sanatkuma1ra or Agni (Fire) coming to
<>see the sacrifice. While they were standing thus, Va1mana
<>with his umbrella, rod and water pot filled with water
<>entered the sacrificial hall. Maha1bali welcomed Va1mana
<>and asked him the purpose of his visit. Va1mana said
<>“Oh King! Your words are sweet. Noble Asura! give
<>me three feet of ground which I measure with my feet.
<>That is all what I want.”
<NI>Maha1bali agreed. The teacher-priest S4ukra felt some
<>doubts regarding the identity of Va1mana. So he secret-
<>ly told Maha1bali that the boy Va1mana was none other
<>than Maha1vis2n2u and as such, he should not give any
<>promise. But Maha1bali had already given the promise.
<>He refused to go back upon his words. The priest
<>S4ukra got angry at this and cursed Maha1bali thus.
<>“You boast that you are wise and learned. But you are
<>disobedient, slow-witted and unwise. So all your
<>prosperities will be destroyed.”
<NI>Though he was cursed, he did not deviate from the
<>path of truth. He gave the promised ground to
<>Va1mana by pouring water with the water brought
<>in golden pot by his queen Vindhya1vali1. The names,
<>As4ana1 and Kot2ara1 also are used in the Pura1n2as for
<>Vindhya1vali1 the wife of Maha1bali. She came out for
<>the purpose wearing golden ornaments. Maha1bali
<>himself washed the feet of Va1mana, who instantly
<>began to grow and became large beyond imagination.
<>Everybody on the spot was amazed at the unimagin-
<>able bigness of Va1mana. On that huge figure, Maha1bali
<>saw the priest, the performers of sacrificial rites, the sacri-
<>ficial hall, the universe, the elements, the qualities the
<>senses, the mind, the individual spirits, and at the feet
<>of the figure the world Rasa1tala. He saw the earth on
<>its feet, mountains below the knees, birds on its knees,
<>the Maruts on its thighs, evening on its clothes, the
<>Praja1patis on its privities, the noble and mighty asuras
<>on its lions, the sky on its navel, the oceans on its stomach,
<>Dharma (duty) in its heart, rightness and truth on its
<>breasts, Maha1laks2mi1 holding lotus on its chest, songs
<>of Sa1man and all other voices in its neck, all the gods
<>beginning with Indra on its hands, the points (direct-
<>ions) on its ears, the ether on its head, the clouds on
<>its hair, breaths in its nose, the sun in its eyes, fire on
<>its face, the Vedas in its words, Varun2a in its tongue,
<>day and night agreement and disagreement in its wink-
<>ing and anger on its forehead. In its touch there was
<>desire, in its radiance there was water, in its buttocks
<>Jawlessness, sacrifice in its steps, death in its shadow,
<>illusion in its laugh, medicines in its hairs, rivers in its
<>veins, stones in its nails, Brahma1 in its intelligence,
<>hermits, devas etc. in its life-breaths. Thus the figure
<>was seen by Maha1bali. Va1mana had trodden on the
<>whole of the earth, which was completely under the
<>cont ol of Maha1bali, with one foot, filled the whole of
<>the sky with his large body and the four points were filled
<>with his hands. The second step was put on Maharloka,
<>Janaloka and Tapoloka (three worlds). No spot was
<>left in the universe for a third step.
<NI>Va1mana said:--“You have given me three feet of
<>ground. I have measured two feet of ground. Show me
<>the place to measure the third step. I have measured
<>the earth with one step and the heaven with the second
<>step. You have seen it. If you cannot keep your promise [Page824-b+ 65]
<>you had better go down to Pa1ta1la.” Bali requested him
<>to place the third step on his head. Va1mana placed his
<>foot on the head of Maha1bali and thrust him down to
<>Pa1ta1la.
<NI>Va1mana brought Indra to the spot and anointed him
<>as the ruler of heaven, in the presence of all the gods
<>and hermits. Indra sent Va1mana with the Lokapa1las
<>(Indra, Agni, Yama and Varun2a) in a divine Vima1na
<>to the world of Vis2n2u. (Bha1gavata Skandha 8).
<NI>b) {%The second story of the incarnation of Va1mana.%} Dhundhu,
<>an asura boy, was born to Kas4yapa by his wife Danu.
<>That asura did penance and pleased Brahma1. He made
<>a request that he should not be killed by Indra and
<>the other gods or anybody else. Brahma1, who was
<>pleased at his penance, gave him the boon he asked.
<>The boy Dhundhu, at the beginning of the fourth
<>Kaliyuga, during the period of Hiran2yakas4ipu, went to
<>heaven and defeating Indra and the other Devas,
<>established his rule there as Indra. The mighty
<>Hiran2yakas4ipu was travelling on the mountain Mandara
<>as an official under Dhundhu.
<NI>The defeated and grief-stricken Devas went to the
<>world of Brahma1 and lived there. Dhundhu, who knew
<>this, wanted to drive them out of that place also. He
<>requested S4ukra, the priest and teacher of asuras, to
<>tell him the means of doing it.
<NI>S4ukra said. “Devendra was enabled to enter the
<>presence of Brahma1, because he had performed one
<>hundred horse-sacrifices. “Hearing this, Dhundhu, with
<>the permission of the teacher S4ukra, began to perform
<>sacrifice in the holy place called Pra1ci1na ti1rtha.
<NI>Hearing that Dhundhu had begun the horse-sacrifice,
<>Indra and the Devas trembled with fear and, leaving
<>the world of Brahma1, went to the world of Vis2n2u and
<>prayed for protection. Bhagava1n came to know of
<>everything and consoled them and sent them back.
<>After this Maha1vis2n2u took the form of a Va1mana
<>and jumped into the Devika1jala and lay afloat like a
<>piece of dry wood. Dhundhu and the hermits saw by
<>chance, Va1mana sinking and coming up in the water.
<>They took the Va1mana-Brahmin out of water and asked
<>him how he had fallen in water. Va1mana replied with
<>shiver:--“In the family of Varun2a there was a Brahmin
<>named Prabha1sa, who was a scholar. I am Gatibha1sa,
<>the younger of his two sons. After the death of father,
<>I requested my brother to divide the property of our
<>father. My brother quoted several rules and said that
<>I was not entitled to get any portion of my father's
<>wealth. I spoke against him and getting angry he
<>caught me by my hair and threw me into the river. It
<>is a year since I, being not able to swim, began to sink
<>and come up in this river. Thus you have seen me
<>here.”
<NI>The Bha1rgavas who were present there, requested
<>Dhundhu thus. “Oh! King of the asuras! Be pleased
<>to give this boy a well-furnished house, servant-maids
<>and plenty of wealth.” Hearing this Dhundhu said to
<>Va1mana. “I will provide you with wealth, servant-
<>maids, house, gold, cows, land, clothes etc.”
<NI>Va1mana humbly said to Dhundhu “Oh Lord! I do
<>not want any wealth. It is my desire for wealth that
<>has brought me to this plight. I request you to give
<>me only three feet of ground.”
<NI>As soon as the asura-King complied with his request,
<>Va1mana began to grow like the moon. He assumed the [Page825-a+ 62]
<>shape of Trivikrama to measure each of the three
<>worlds with a foot. In two steps he measured all the
<>worlds. Va1mana whose body was bigger than Maha1-
<>meru became angry when he did not see place to
<>measure the third step, and fell on Dhundhu. By this
<>heavy fall there appeared a great cavity thirty thousand
<>yojanas deep. Bhagava1n Vis2n2u took the asura Dhundhu
<>and threw him into that deep pit with a mighty force,
<>and by a shower of dust he filled the cavity.
<NI>Indra with the Devas occupied the world of Gods.
<>Leaving the asura King in the sea of dust, Bhagava1n
<>jumped into the river Ka1lindi1 and disappeared. (Padma
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 78).
<HI>VA1MANA II. One of the eight elephants supporting

<>bha1rata, Vana Parva, Chapter 84, Stanza 13, that those

<HI>VA1MANA V. A mountain in the Kraun5ca Island.
<>(Maha1bha1rata, Bhi1s2ma Parva, Chapter 12).
<HI>VA1MANABHAT2T2ABA1N2A. A great Sanskrit poet. It is
<>believed that he lived in the 15th century. He has
<>written several poems and plays. His poetic works are
<>‘Nala1bhyudaya’ and ‘Raghuna1thacarita.’ The former
<>consists of eight Ka1n2d2as and the latter, thirty Ka1n2d2as.
<NI>It is stated that Va1manabhat2t2aba1n2a was the teacher of
<>Vidya1ran2ya. Besides these two major works, he had
<>written several poems and dramas. Imitating the poem
<>‘Meghasandes4a’ of Ka1lida1sa, he had written a poetic
<>work of the same type called ‘Ham3sasandes4a’. A minor
<>Drama called S4r2n3ga1rabhu1s2an2a was written by this
<>Va1mana. It is said that this drama was acted at
<>Vijayanagara. The two plays, named--Pa1rvati1parin2aya
<>and ‘Kanakarekha1’ were also written by the same poet.

<HI>VA1NARA (MONKEY). Monkeys are given a prominent
<>place in the Pura1n2as. Considering them as born in the
<>family of Hanu1ma1n, an ardent devotee of S4ri1 Ra1ma,
<>some worship monkeys. It is mentioned in Va1lmi1ki
<>Ra1ma1yan2a that the monkeys got a prominent place
<>first among the gods and spirits. It is stated in Va1lmi1ki
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, Kis2kindha1 Ka1n2d2a, Sarga 33, that the
<>leaders of the monkeys were Ba1li, Sugri1va, An3gada,
<>Mainda, Gavaya, Dvivida, Gava1ks2a, Gaja, S4arabha,
<>Su1rya1ks2a, Hanu1ma1n, Vidyunma1li1, Vi1raba1hu, Suba1hu,
<>Nala, Kumuda, Ja1mbava1n, Ta1ra, Supa1t2ala, Sunetra,
<>Ni1la and Dadhivaktra.
<NI>The social life of the monkeys, is described as follows
<>in the Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Kis2kindha1 Ka1n2d2a, Sarga
<>33. “Mahendra mountain, the Hima1layas, the Vindhya
<>mountain, the peaks of Kaila1sa and S4veta mountain,
<>Mandara mountain etc. are the places mostly inhabit-
<>ed by monkeys. On the mountains shining like the
<>rising sun, on the east of the western ocean also monkeys
<>live. The Monkeys which inhabit black soil are blue
<>in colour. Monkeys which live in red arsenic caves
<>are yellow in colour. Those which live in Maha1 Meru
<>and Dhu1mra (smoky) mountain have the colour of the
<>rising sun and are drunkards.”

<HI>VAN3GA. An important state in ancient India. The
<>present name of this country is Bengal. Several state-
<>ments occur in the Pura1n2as about Van3ga.
<NI>The following are the statements about Van3ga given
<>in the Maha1bha1rata.
<NI>(i) Arjuna visited this country during his pilgrimage.
<>(M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 214, Stanza 9).
<NI>(ii) Bhi1masena attacked Van3ga. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva,
<>Chapter 30, Stanza 23).
<NI>(iii) The Kings of Van3ga visited Yudhis2t2hira with
<>presents. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 52, Stanza 18).
<NI>(iv) Karn2a conquered this country during his regional
<>conquest. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter 254, Stanza 8).
<NI>(v) In the Bha1rata battle the King of Van3ga confront-
<>ed Ghat2otkaca and was defeated. (M.B. Bhi1s2ma
<>Parva, Chapter 92, Stanza 6).
<NI>(vi) Once S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a conquered the country of Van3ga.
<>(M.B. Dron2a Parva, Chapter 11, Stanza 15).
<NI>(vii) Paras4ura1ma exterminated the Ks2atriyas of Van3ga.
<>(M.B. Dron2a Parva, Chapter 70, Stanza 12).
<NI>(viii) The low castes of Van3ga attacked the sacrificial
<>horse led by Arjuna who killed every one of them.
<>(M.B. As4vamedha Parva, Chapter 82, Stanza 29).
<NI>VAN3GR2DA. An asura. It is mentioned in R2gveda,
<>Man2d2ala 1, Anuva1ka 1, Su1kta 10, that the Kings Ati-
<>thigva and R2jis4va1, with their armies surrounded the
<>cities of the asuras Karan5ja, Parn2aya and Van3gr2da and
<>that Indra helped the two Kings.

<HI>VARA1HA III. (Boar). One of the ten incarnations of
<>Maha1vis2n2u.
<NI>1) {%Need for this incarnation.%} Jaya and Vijaya, two door-
<>keepers of Maha1vis2n2u showed disrespect towards
<>the famous hermits Sanaka and others who went to
<>visit Maha1vis2n2u. The angry hermits cursed them to take

<>were born from Praja1pati Kas4yapa by his wife Diti.
<NI>Even their birth itself was inauspicious. Once, while
<>Kas4yapa was carrying on evening worship, his wife was
<>filled with libid. She embraced Kas4yapa. Because she
<>got pregnancy in an inauspicious moment, two asura
<>sons were born. At the time of their birth darkness
<>spread over the whole of the world, and some ill omens
<>were seen. The Devas (gods) trembled.
<NI>The elder son was called Hiran2ya1ks2a and the second
<>son was called Hiran2yakas4ipu. As Hiran2ya1ks2a grew up
<>he began to quarrel with the Devas. When the fight
<>grew fierce, he picked up the earth in his hands as a
<>ball and went under water. There was left only water.
<>The Devas went to Maha1vis2n2u and prayed to him in
<>order to get the earth back.
<NI>At this time Manu Sva1yambhuva, the son of Brahma1,
<>was living with his father looking after his welfare. The
<>father, who was pleased with the services of his son
<>said. “My dear son, you should worship Devi1, who will
<>be pleased with your devotion and will bless you. If
<>she is pleased with you, you will become a famous
<>Praja1pati.” Hearing the words of Brahma1, Sva1yam-
<>bhuva worshipped Devi1 with ardent devotion, deep
<>meditation and severe vows and penance, at which
<>Jagadamba1 was pleased. She appeared before him and
<>asked him what boon he wanted. Manu requested that
<>he should be permitted to carry on creation without
<>any obstruction. Devi1 gave him permission. Manu [Page827-a+ 65]
<>returned to his father Brahma1 and said. “Father, point
<>out to me a solitary place. I will sit there and create
<>subjects by the blessings of Devi1.” Only when he heard
<>the request of his son, did Brahma1 begin to think about
<>the exigency of providing his son with such a place. For,
<>the earth was completely under water. For a long time
<>even Brahma1 was being subjected to much incon-
<>venience to carry on creation. Only Bhagava1n A1di
<>Na1ra1yan2a (Vis2n2u) could do anything in this matter. So
<>Brahma1 with the Manus, hermits and others began to
<>meditate on Maha1vis2n2u.
<NI>2) {%Incarnation.%} Instantly the young one of a Boar
<>jumped out of the nose of Brahma1 through his breath.
<>That divine figure of Boar stood in the air and began to
<>grow. Within a few moments, it became a colossal
<>Being. It grew up as big as an elephant. Soon it be-
<>came as large as a mountain. Seeing this, Brahma1 and
<>the others stood in amazement. The Boar made a grunt
<>in a loud roaring sound. The people of Janaloka, Satya-
<>loka etc. understood that it was the sound of Maha1-
<>vis2n2u. They raised glory and praise to Bhagava1n, who
<>heard all these praises, but without condescending to
<>tell anything, looked at all of them with grace and love,
<>and with a mighty force jumped into the sea. It was
<>immensely troubled by the manes of God Almighty.
<>Varun2a prayed to him to save him from them. Bhagva1n
<>folded his mane and went down to the deep water and
<>made a search for the earth. The Boar walked smelling
<>and snorting and found out the earth. He slowly lifted
<>it on his tusks and started from there. On the way, the
<>fierce and wicked Hiran2ya1ks2a hindered him. Bhagava1n
<>Vis2n2u used his club Nandaka and killed Hiran2ya1ks2a. It
<>was besmeared with his blood; Maha1vis2n2u came up to
<>the surface of water with the earth. He set the earth
<>firm over the water. Thus Brahma1 gave Manu a place
<>in the earth which floated on the water like a lotus-leaf,
<>and empowered him to perform creation.
<NI>3) {%Incarnation of Boar again.%} The goddess Earth, the
<>deity of earth which was raised to the surface of water,
<>fell in love with Maha1vis2n2u and embraced him. He
<>embraced her in return. These mutual embraces conti-
<>nued for one complete Devavars2a (year of God) and
<>consequently the goddess Earth became tired and weak
<>and unconscious. So the earth slid down a little under
<>water. Bhagava1n took the form of a Boar again and
<>lifted the earth to its original place and returned to
