<HI>VARARUCI. An ancient Indian scholar in astronomy
<>and astrology. (Some details regarding Vararuci are
<>given under Bhartr2hari).
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} It is stated in Katha1saritsa1gara, Katha1pi1t2ha-
<>lambaka, Taran3ga 1, that Vararuci was the rebirth of
<>a Gan2ana1yaka (guard of S4iva) named Pus2padanta.
<>Once S4iva happened to tell Pa1rvati1 some previous
<>stories of the Vidya1dharas. Pus2padanta eavesdropped
<>and heard everything. Pa1rvati1 understood this and
<>cursed him to become a man. Accordingly Pus2padanta
<>was born in the city of Kaus4a1mbi1 under the name
<>Vararuci or Ka1tya1yana. (For details about the curse
<>see under Gun2a1d2hya).
<NI>2) {%Of sharp intelligence.%} Vararuci was born as the son
<>of the Brahmin Somadatta at Kaus4a1mbi1. Agni-
<>s4ikha was another name of Somadatta. Vasudatta1
<>was the mother of Vararuci. In the previous birth,
<>Vasudatta1 was a hermit maid. Due to a curse she
<>lost her hermit-maidhood and took birth as a woman
<>under the name Vasudatta1 and became the wife of
<>Somadatta.
<NI>As his father died in his childhood, Vararuci was
<>brought up by his mother. She was in great penury
<>after the death of her husband. One day two travellers
<>came to her house. While they were sitting on the
<>veranda, the sound of a conch was heard. Then the
<>mother called Vararuci and said to him. “Son, the
<>dance of Nanda, the friend of your father, is about to
<>begin.” Vararuci requested his mother to let him go
<>to the temple and see the dance so that he might learn
<>it. Hearing this, the guests were amazed. How can
<>one learn dance by seeing only once? That was the
<>doubt in the minds of the travellers. “Vararuci could
<>learn anything by hearing only once,” his mother
<>said. As a test, they recited to him a portion from the
<>Vedas. Vararuci repeated the portion to them without
<>any mistake. After this the guests took the boy and [Page828-b+ 65]
<>showed him the dance and brought him back. The boy
<>performed every item of the dance he saw, before the
<>guests. They were immensely pleased.
<NI>3) {%Getting a teacher.%} The guests had actually been
<>searching for a boy who could understand anything at
<>the first hearing. There was reason for it.
<NI>In the great city of Nandara1ja, there was a great
<>Brahmin named S4an3karasva1mi1. He had two sons
<>named Vars2a and Upavars2a. Both married and lived in
<>separate houses. By and by Vars2a became cruel and
<>wicked and poor. Upavars2a became learned and
<>wealthy. The rainy season approached. In those days
<>it was a custom for women to make a sweet food of rice
<>flour with sugar and give it to cruel Brahmins. It was
<>to prevent the extreme cold in the winter and the ex-
<>treme heat in the summer from affecting them severely.
<>The wife of Upavars2a made this sweet food and gave it
<>to Vars2a. When he brought it home, his wife scolded
<>him. Vars2a felt very sorry because of his foolishness.
<>He gave up all his desires and began to worship
<>Kuma1rasva1mi1. Kuma1rasva1mi1 was pleased with him
<>and blessed him with all kinds of knowledge. Kuma1ra-
<>sva1mi1 had ordered him to teach all of this knowledge
<>to one who could learn at first hearing. From that day
<>onwards Vars2a had been waiting for a disciple who
<>could learn at first hearing.
<NI>During this period there lived two Brahmins named
<>Devasva1mi1 and Karambhaka in the city of Vetasapura.
<>They were friends. A son was born to each of them.
<>The son of Devasva1mi1 was named Vya1li1. The name of
<>the son of Karambhaka was Indradatta. When Vya1li1
<>was a child, his father died. Seeing their grief the
<>father of Indradatta left the country. Both of the boys,
<>having nobody to look after them, went to perform
<>penance, before Subrahman2yasva1mi1. Subrahman2ya
<>ordered them in their sleep to go to Vars2a in Pa1t2alika1,
<>the city of King Nanda, and to learn every thing from
<>him. The boys immediately went to the city of King
<>Nanda and enquired about the teacher Vars2a. The
<>people told them that Vars2a was a fool. They were
<>sorry to hear it. Still they did not turn back. They
<>reached the house of Vars2a, and entered the house which
<>was the abode of rats and bats, the walls of which were
<>crumbling due to rain as it had not been thatched for
<>a long time. The courtyard was full of Cassiatora
<>plants. Inside the house, here and there on the floor,
<>Cyperus-grass was growing. The wife of Vars2a who was
<>in utter penury stood up and welcomed them. She told
<>them about her husband. “He is waiting for a disciple
<>who would learn everything at first hearing.” Vya1li1
<>and Indradatta agreed to bring such a disciple and
<>they started on a journey in search of such a boy. They
<>were the two travellers who came to the house of
<>Vararuci.
<NI>Vya1li1 said all about these things to the mother of
<>Vararuci. She was very glad and said. “Everything
<>fits well. At the birth of my son, an ethereal
<>voice said: “At first hearing he will learn every-
<>thing. He will learn everything from the teacher
<>Vars2a. He will publish works in grammar and astro-
<>nomy.” If he has interest in good things he will get the
<>name Vararuci. So I had been thinking how and where
<>to find this teacher Vars2a.”
<NI>Next morning, with the permission of his mother,
<>Vararuci started for the house of Vars2a, with Vya1li1 and [Page829-a+ 65]
<>Indradatta. They reached the house of Vars2a. Next
<>morning they smeared and purified the floor and the
<>three sincere and simple disciples sat facing the east.
<>The teacher Vars2a recited to them the divine spell
<>‘Om,’ and instantly all the Vedas and the ancillaries of
<>Vedas made their appearance. The teacher commenced
<>teaching. The three disciples learned, Vararuci
<>hearing only once, Vya1li1 hearing twice and Indradatta
<>hearing thrice. Hearing these divine recitations and
<>repetitions which were not to be heard anywhere else,
<>Brahmins crowded to the place. King Nanda of
<>Pa1t2ali1putra praised the teacher Vars2a and sent him
<>plenty of wealth and conferred on him ranks and
<>privileges.
<NI>4) {%Marriage.%} Vararuci learned everything from the
<>teacher Vars2a. He went with his friends once to see
<>the Indra-festival. There, Vararuci happened to see
<>Upakos4a1 the daughter of Upavars2a and fell in love
<>with her. That night he did not sleep. At last he slow-
<>ly closed his eyes. A divine woman clad in white
<>garments appeared before him It seemed to him that
<>the woman said to him. “You need not worry in this
<>matter. Upakos4a1 was your wife in the previous birth.
<>She will marry only you. I am Sarasvati1 who pervades
<>your body.” Vararuci woke up. Next day the elders
<>came to know of this, and gave her in marriage to
<>Vararuci.
<NI>5) {%Vararuci becoming a Minister.%} Education was finished.
<>It was time for Vya1li1 and Indradatta to give presents
<>to the teacher Vars2a. The teacher demanded a crore
<>of Vilkas (a Venetian ducat used for neck-ornament).
<>They were not having so much wealth. They went
<>with Vararuci to Nanda, the King of Ayodhya1, and the
<>Brother-in-law of Vararuci. When they reached Ayodhya1,
<>they heard that the King had just died. Indradatta
<>said “By the practice of contemplation I shall enter the
<>body of the King. Vararuci should come and beg money
<>of me. Till my return Vya1li1 must keep my body.”
<NI>“The spirit of Indradatta immediately entered the
<>King's body. The dead King rose up. People were
<>struck with wonder. They celebrated a festival. Vya1li1
<>kept the body of Indradatta within a temple. Vararuci
<>went to the presence of the King and begged for a
<>crore of Vilkas. The King called his minister S4akat2a1la
<>and told him to give Vararuci a crore of Vilkas.
<>S4akat2a1la, who was an intelligent man, felt some doubt
<>in the coming to life of the King. He decided that some
<>one might have entered the body of the King. He
<>ordered that every dead body in the city should be
<>burnt. Along with the bodies, the body of Indradatta
<>also was taken by force from Vya1li1. At this time the
<>King compelled S4akat2a1la to give the beggar Vilkas.
<>But till the dead bodies were burnt, S4akat2a1la did not
<>obey him. Because his body was burnt Indradatta had
<>to remain in the body of the King. S4akat2a1la gave Vara-
<>ruci the required amount. But of what use was the
<>money? Indradatta had become the King. The King
<>and Vya1li1 made a secret consultation, as a result of
<>which Vararuci was made the Prime Minister. S4akat2a1la
<>was charged with Brahmahatya1 and was put into a
<>dungeon with his hundred sons. They were allowed
<>fried and powdered grain and a ‘ceratu’ (a measure)
<>of water as food for each.
<NI>S4akat2a1la said to his sons. “My sons, all of us cannot
<>live with so little food. The most intelligent one among [Page829-b+ 65]
<>us should live by eating the whole food, to take revenge
<>on the Yoga Nanda King. Who will do so?”
<NI>Sons:--We don't think that we are powerful to do that.
<NI>So you must live, father!
<NI>So S4akat2a1la ate the whole food and lived while his sons,
<>one by one, died of hunger before his eyes. S4akat2a1la
<>sat in the middle of the Skeletons with the sole purpose
<>of taking revenge. Vya1li1 gave the present to the teacher
<>and went home.
<NI>Indradatta and Vararuci lived as king and minister.
<NI>6) {%Loss of ministership.%} By and by Indradatta had fallen
<>into bad ways. The subjects were beset with famine.
<>The people hated the king and the minister. They made
<>a cry to bring S4akat2a1la back. They obtained the per-
<>mission of the king and brought S4akat2a1la out of the
<>dungeon. S4akat2a1la knew that so long as Vararuci was
<>alive, he could do nothing to the King Yoga Nanda. So
<>he decided to wait for an opportunity, and accepted
<>an office under Vararuci. One day Yoga Nanda went
<>out for a walk. He saw in the Ganges the palm of a
<>hand with five fingers. He called Vararuci and asked
<>him what the sight was. Vararuci showed two fingers
<>in that direction. Immediately the palm of the hand
<>disappeared. The King was amazed at this and asked
<>him for its meaning. He said “The meaning of showing
<>fingers was that if five men unite together, they could
<>accomplish anything. I showed two fingers, to mean that
<>if two men unite together they also could achieve any-
<>thing.” At this reply the king was much pleased and
<>S4akat2a1la felt miserable at the intelligence of Vararuci.
<NI>On another occasion the King saw his wife looking at a
<>Brahmin guest through the window. He got angry and
<>ordered that Brahmin to be killed. When that Brahmin
<>was being taken to the scaffold, a dead fish, placed for
<>sale, laughed loud. The king asked Vararuci for its

<>svati1. Devi1 appeared and told him. “If you climb up to

<>“The wives of the kings are not chaste. In all harems
<>men live in the guise of women. Without stopping this
<>the king was going to kill an innocent Brahmin. That
<>is why the dead fish laughed.”
<NI>Vararuci, who heard this conversation, got down when
<>the giantess was gone. He went to the king and told

<>search in the harem. He found out some men in the

<>The picture was beautiful. Still, considering the appear-

<>picture. When Vararuci had gone the king entered the [Page830-a+ 65]
<>room and saw the mole in the picture. The kingasked his

<>S4akat2a1la to him and told him to kill Vararuci secretly.
<>S4akat2a1la felt pity on Vararuci and hid him in a place.
<>Vararuci told S4akat2a1la “No body can kill me. My
<>friend is a giant. He will come to me the moment

<>the giant. Vararuci thought of him and the giant

<>Vararuci made him disappear. S4akat2a1la asked him how
<>he got the friendship of the giant. Vararuci replied.
<NI>“While you were in the dungeon, a city chief was disap-
<>pearing daily from this city. The king asked me to find
<>out the reason. One night, while I was walking through
<>the city, I saw this giant. He asked me. “Who is the
<>most beautiful woman in this city?” I replied “Whoever
<>falls in love with a woman, to him, that woman is the
<>most beautiful. You who do not know this principle,
<>are a fool.” The giant had no answer. He said “You
<>alone have defeated me. I am glad of it. The moment
<>you think of me I will come and help you.” Saying
<>this the giant disappeared. Thenceforward we became
<>friends.”
<NI>S4akat2a1la felt great respect for Vararuci. Hiran2yagupta,
<>the son of Yoga Nanda, went for hunting. His horse ran

<>The lion asked the bear to push that man down. The

<>to push the bear down. Thinking that he could save

<>gupta. “May you become mad.” The king was very

<NI>S4akat2a1la went to the presence of the king and said that
<>Vararuci was still alive. In accordance with the order

<>Immediately the prince was cured of his madness. Yoga

<>gence can understand anything by symptoms and in-

<>the mole of the queen. When the king heard this he
<>bowed down his head.
<NI>After this Vararuci left the palace. He did not stop to
<>accept the favours of the king. Nor did he pay any heed

<>house. Everybody who saw Vararuci began to cry. Not

<>to fire and his mother died of broken heart. The grief-

<NI>After this S4akat2a1la waited for an opportunity to bring
<>Indradatta (Yoga Nanda) down. Once he had been
<>walking out side the city when he saw a Brahmin dig-

<NI>8) {%The end.%} Vararuci began to perform penance in the
<>Vindhya mountain. At that time a brahmin came to

<HI>VARATANU. A King of the Kurus. Because of his sin-
<>ful deeds in the previous birth he was denied the happi-

<>his name was S4ri1dhara. He lived with his wife Prabha1-
<>vati1 for a long time, but did not get children. When

<>tanu. Your wife was called S4an3kari1. When both of you

<>caste child falling into water. Without paying any heed
<>to it, you went on your way. The child was drowned.
<>Though both of you have taken births in royal families
<>due to the several good deeds of yours, you have become
<>childless because of that one sinful act.”
<NI>According to the instruction of Vya1sa, he gave Brah-

<NI>Once there lived an ‘apsaras’ (celestial maid) named

<HI>VASIS2T2HA. A hermit who was the son of Brahma1.
<NI>1) {%The three births of Vasis2t2ha.%} Vasis2t2ha with the radiance
<>of Brahma1, is very famous in the Pura1n2as. He had three
<>births.
<NI>i) {%First birth.%}
<NI>a) {%Birth.%} Vasis2t2ha is one of the mental sons of Brahma1.
<>This is the first birth of Vasis2t2ha.
<NI>“Na1rada was born from the lap, Daks2a was born from
<>the right thumb, Vasis2t2ha from the breath (pra1n2a)
<>Bhr2gu from the skin, Kratu from the hand of Brahma1.”
<NI>In this birth, Arundhati1 was the wife of Vasis2t2ha.
<>Arundhati1 was known by the name U1rja1 also. There is
<>a Pura1n2ic story behind the marriage of Vasis2t2ha and
<>Arundhati1.
<NI>In her previous birth the name of Arundhati1 was
<>Sandhya1. She was born from the passion of Brahma1.
<>As soon as she was born she grew up. Seeing her exqui-
<>site beauty the Praja1patis were filled with excitement.
<>Even Brahma1, her father, was some-what excited at her
<>sight. S4iva, who understood this, teased Brahma1.
<>Sandhya1 was ashamed. Even after S4iva, Brahma1, Pra-
<>ja1patis and the others had gone their ways, she remained
<>there blaming her birth. She said to herself: “The mo-
<>ment I was born, I grew up to be a young woman. My
<>father and my brothers looked at me with desire. I
<>also looked at them excitedly. As an atonement for
<>this sin, I will burn my body in fire. I do not want a
<>body desired by my father and brothers. With this
<>decision she went to the mountain Candrabha1ga, which
<>is the source of the river Candrabha1ga1, and began to
<>perform penance. Brahma1 who came to know of this, [Page834-b+ 65]
<>was filled with pity and sent Vasis2t2ha to her. He went
<>there in the form of a Brahmin boy and taught her how
<>to perform penance. After the return of Vasis2t2ha she con-
<>tinued the severe penance to Vis2n2u. Her penance became
<>the subject of talk and wonder everywhere. After a short
<>while Vis2n2u appeared before her sitting on his convey-
<>ance Garud2a. She praised Vis2n2u. Vis2n2u was pleased
<>with her and asked what her desire was. She said:
<>“Lord, creatures should not be sexually excited at their
<>birth. They should have sexual desire only when they
<>are grown up. The three worlds must know that I am
<>chaste. In all my births I should not look at anybody
<>except my husband with sexual desire. The man who
<>looks at me with sexual desire should lose his manliness
<>and become a hermaphrodite.”
<NI>Maha1vis2n2u agreed to her request. It was from that day
<>onwards that sexual desire appeared in creatures in
<>their grown-up stage only, after they have passed the
<>stages of infancy and childhood. Maha1vis2n2u told her.
<>“It is preordained that this body of yours will be burnt
<>in fire. Medha1tithi, (Megha1tithi), the son of Priyavrata
<>is performing the great sacrifice called Jyotis2t2oma which
<>is to be completed in twelve years. It is coming to a
<>close. There is none equal to him because of this sacri-
<>fice. That great hermit's sacrifice is being performed in
<>the basin of this river Candrabha1ga1. Unseen by the
<>hermits, you should go there and get into the fire. Be-
<>cause of my blessing you will be born from that fire as
<>the daughter of Medha1tithi. You should go into the fire
<>thinking of him whom you want to be your husband in
<>this birth.”
<NI>Maha1vis2n2u touched Sandhya1 with the tip of his finger.
<>Sandhya1 walked to the sacrificial hall. Seen by none,
<>she got down into the fire. She was burnt in the blaz-
<>ing fire. By the order of Vis2n2u; the God fire placed
<>Sandhya1 who was burnt in the sacrificial fire, in the
<>Solar region. The sun divided that body into two and
<>placed them in his chariot. The upper portion became
<>the pra1tassandhya1 (dawn) between the day and the
<>night and the lower portion became the Sa1yantana
<>Sandhya1 (the evening twilight). At the end of the
<>sacrifice, from the centre of the sacrificial fire a girl,
<>like the flame of fire rose up. Medha1tithi took that
<>child in his hands and called her Arundhati1. The child
<>became famous throughout the three worlds, as the
<>name stood for the principle that on no reason what-so-
<>ever will righteousness be obstructed. (Rodha means
<>obstruction. So Arundhati1 is one who cannot be obstruct-
<>ed). In some other Pura1n2as Arundhati1 is given as
<>the daughter of Praja1pati Kardama and Devahu1ti.
<NI>b) {%Marriage.%} Arundhati1 grew up in the hermitage
<>of Medha1tithi. Candrabha1ga1 holy bath which was the
<>playfield of Arundhati1, is even today, known as Arun-
<>dhati1ti1rtha. Arundhati1 became five years of age. Brahma1
<>saw her when she was playing on the ground after hav-
<>ing taken her bath. At the instruction of Brahma1,
<>Arundhati1 was entrusted to Sa1vitri1 and Bahula1 for
<>education. Sa1vitri1 is the wife of the Sun. Arundhati1
<>lived in Ma1nasa lake with Sa1vitri1, Ga1yatri1, Bahula1,
<>Sarasvati1, Drupada1 and such other celestial women
<>and completed her education. One day Arundhati1
<>happened to see Vasis2t2ha who was shining with radi-
<>ance. They fell in love with each other. Parents and
<>relatives and the elders came to know of this. Sa1vitri
<>patting Arundhati1 blessed her to become the wife of [Page835-a+ 64]
<>Vasis2t2ha. The marriage of Arundhati1 with Vasis2t2ha was
<>conducted in the presence of the Gods.
<NI>c) {%Sons.%} Seven sons, named Citraketu, Purocis, Viraca,
<>Mitra, Ulban2a, Vasubhr2dya1na and Dyuma1n were born
<>to Vasis2t2ha by Arundhati1. All the seven of them were
<>Brahmars2is.
<NI>{%d) The meaning of the names Vasis2t2ha and Arundhati1.%} The
<>meaning of the name Vasis2t2ha is given by Vasis2t2ha
<>himself as follows:--
<NI>I am known as Vasis2t2ha for two reasons. I am ‘Vasu-
<>ma1n’. The S4rutis say that Air, Earth etc. are Vasus.
<>I have become Vasis2t2ha because I have brought under
<>control the Vasus beginning with An2ima1 (the power
<>of reducing the body to an atom). See what Arun-
<>dhati1 herself says, about the meaning of the name
<>Arundhati1.
<NI>“I consider mountains, earth and heaven as elements
<>in which I live, only after considering my hus-
<>band as the element in which I live. My husband
<>is the first and foremost element in my life. Moreover
<>I conform (anurodha) with the mind of my husband.
<>So know me as Arundhati1.”
<NI>{%e) Conjugal fidelity of Arundhati1.%} The Saptars2is (the seven
<>hermits) once left Arundhati1 alone in a forest and went
<>to the Mountain Hima1layas to gather fruits and roots.
<>In those days, due to severe summer, the earth
<>became a wilderness. So the hermits lived in the
<>Hima1layas. Arundhati1 all alone did penance in the
<>forest. On one day S4iva took the form of a Brahmin and
<>going near Arundhati1 requested her for alms. She rep-
<>lied: “There are only Ziziphus fruits here, and nothing
<>else to eat.” The Brahmin asked her to boil them. She
<>placed the pot of Ziziphus fruits on the oven and sat
<>there hearing the good stories told by the Brahmin. Thus
<>without thinking about her hunger or thirst she sat there
<>for twelve years hearing the stories narrated by the
<>Brahmin. When the hermits returned from Hima1layas,
<>S4iva assumed his original form and said, “Hermits!
<>This woman, sitting in the hermitage, has acquired
<>more penance than you have acquired in the Hima1layas.
<>Good woman! What boon do you want?” She
<>requested that that place should be known as the holy
<>place called ‘Badarapacana (cooking of Ziziphus fruits).
<>S4iva allowed it. Even today that place is considered
<>a holy place.
<NI>f) {%Death.%} When so many devas were dead at the sacri-
<>fice of Daks2a, Vasis2t2ha also died along with them.
<>Arundhati1 followed her husband. After death both of
<>them entered the firmament and became two stars.
<NI>g) {%Seeing Arundhati1.%} In India there is a custom of show-
<>ing the women, the star Arundhati1. Arundhati1 is the
<>first of the women who took only one husband in her life.
<>The meaning of this custom is this that this mentality of
<>Arundhati1 should be acquired by the married women
<>of India. Moreover, if the star stands in front of the star
<>Vasis2t2ha, it is considered to forebode calamities in the
<>world. (Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a).
<NI>ii) {%Second birth.%}
<NI>{%General information.%} Vasis2t2ha who died at the sacrifice
<>of Daks2a took birth again from the sacrificial fire of
<>Brahma1. Aks2ama1la1 was the wife of this Vasis2t2ha.
<>Aks2ama1la1 was the rebirth of Arundhati1. So, in some
<>Pura1n2as, both are shown as one. This birth of Vasis2-
<>t2ha was terminated by the curse of emperor Nimi of [Page835-b+ 65]
<>the Iks2va1ku dynasty. (For details see under Nimi).
<NI>(iii) {%Third birth.%} In the third birth Vasis2t2ha was born
<>from a pot as the son of Mitra1varun2as. In this birth
<>Vasis2t2ha was the brother of Agastya. (For detailed
<>story see under Agastya, para 2). In this birth the
<>wife of Vasis2t2ha was an Arundhati1, who was the sister
<>of Na1rada. Thus according to the Pura1n2as, it took
<>three births for Vasis2t2ha to complete his life. It is
<>difficult to ascertain, in which particular birth, a parti-
<>cular story, stated in the Pura1n2as, took place. It is
<>probable that a large number of episodes might have
<>taken place in the second and third births.
<NI>Stories connected with the life of Vasis2t2ha are given
<>below:
<NI>2) {%Vasis2t2ha and Vis4va1mitra.%} Vasis2t2ha and Vis4va1mitra
<>were two herm its who quarrelled with each other through-
<>out their lives. The quarrel between Vasis2t2ha a
<>Brahmin hermit and Vis4va1mitra a royal hermit, is
<>given below:
<NI>(i) {%Reason for the enmity.%} Vis4va1mitra was a King who
<>cared much for the welfare of his subjects. Once he
<>went with his army to hunt. He became tired by the
<>hunt and reached the hermitage of Vasis2t2ha who
<>welcomed them with hospitality. He told them that
<>food would be ready when they returned after a bath.
<>The King and his men bathed quickly and when they
<>returned, a grand feast was ready for so many thou-
<>sands. The King was in great perplexity. After the
<>meals Vis4va1mitra approached the hermit Vasis2t2ha
<>and asked him how he got such a grand feast ready.
<>He replied that the cow Ka1madhenu in his hermitage was
<>capable of granting any wish. The king desired to have
<>the cow. He asked the hermit to exchange the cow for
<>a crore of ordinary cows. Vasis2t2ha did not agree to
<>this. Vis4va1mitra said that the noble and superior
<>things of the country belonged to the King. The hermit
<>did not agree to that also. Finally Vis4va1mitra was
<>about to take away the cow by force. Thus the quarrel
<>began. Understanding the wish of Vasis2t2ha Ka1madhenu
<>held her horns and tail up and stood as a fierce
<>and cruel figure. From the various limbs of the cow so
<>many thousands of warriors jumped out and a fierce
<>battle ensued in which the hundred sons and the huge
<>army of Vis4va1mitra met with defeat. At last Vis4va1mitra
<>tried to launch a direct attack against Vasis2t2ha. But
<>the arrows of Vis4va1mitra were changed to flowers when
<>they touched the body of Vasis2t2ha. At last the King
<>admitted that the power of the penance of a Brahmin
<>hermit was the real power and he admitted defeat from
<>Vasis2t2ha. From that day onwards a deep-rooted,
<>hatred against Vasis2t2ha arose in the heart of Vis4va1mitra.
<>He left the administration of his Kingdom in the hands
<>of his relatives and went to the south where he erected
<>a hermitage and began to do severe penance. He acquir-
<>ed great powers of penance and became a royal hermit
<>of immense attainments and the quarrel between the
<>two great hermits Vasis2t2ha and Vis4va1mitra began.
<>(Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a, 5 sargas from 51).
<NI>(ii) {%Second confrontation.%} The next contest between the two
<>hermits was due to the king Tris4an3ku of the Solar
<>dynasty. Tris4an3ku wanted bodily attainment of heaven.
<>Vasis2t2ha tried to discourage the king. Vis4va1mitra took
<>up the matter. He began to perform a sacrifice to lift
<>up the king bodily to heaven, and Tris4an3ku began to
<>rise up higher and higher. But Indra did not allow him [Page836-a+ 65]
<>to enter bodily into the heaven. At last Vis4va1mitra, by
<>the power of his penance created an artificial heaven
<>midway between the heaven and the earth and placed
<>Tris4an3ku in that heaven. With this the gap between
<>the two hermits widened. (For more details see under
<>Tris4an3ku).
<NI>(iii) {%Third confrontation.%} By the blessing of Varun2a, a son
<>named Lohita was born to Haris4candra. He had promis-
<>ed Varun2a to sacrifice his son Lohita. But Lohita
<>came to know of this and he left the palace. Haris4can-
<>dra was in a difficult situation. Vasis2t2ha advised him
<>to buy a son and to sacrifice him. Accordingly a Brah-
<>min boy named S4unas4s4epha was bought. Vasis2t2ha
<>decided to sacrifice him. Vis4va1mitra said that he could
<>not be sacrificed. Haris4candra was loved by Vasis2t2ha.
<>So Vis4va1mitra tried to harm him as far as possible.
<>Haris4candra fell in the trap of Vi1s4va1mitra and lost his
<>country, city, wite, everything and had to live in funeral
<>places. (For detailed story see under Haris4candra.)
<NI>(iv) {%Fourth confrontation.%} Vasis2t2ha did not like the
<>deception practised by Vis4va1mitra, on Haris4candra.
<>Once Vis4va1mitra and Vasis2t2ha met in the forest by
<>chance. Vasis2t2ha scolded Vis4va1mitra, who had only
<>the guise of a hermit, thus: “You wicked Vis4va1mitra!
<>Why do you put the truthful Haris4candra into so much
<>misery? You are deceiving him as the stork sits in
<>meditation and deceives the fish. So you will become
<>a stork.”
<NI>Vis4va1mitra looked at the hermit Vasis2t2ha who had
<>cursed him and said. “So long as I am a stork you
<>shall be Kingfisher. Thus they cursed each other.
<NI>Both of them became birds and went to the shore of
<>Ma1nasa lake and each building a nest on the top of
<>trees began to live there. There was nothing but quar-
<>rel between the two birds daily. Both pecked and
<>scratched each other to such an extent that they were
<>covered with bleeding wounds. They looked like an
<>Erythrina tree filled with flowers. Everybody who saw
<>them was filled with sadness. Thus they lived there
<>for a long time.
<NI>Once the Kind-hearted Brahma1, the father of Vasis2t2ha,
<>saw their sorry plight. Having pity on them he came
<>there with the other gods and delivered them both
<>from the curse. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Skandha 6).
<NI>(v) {%Fifth confrontation.%} Vis4va1mitra called the river
<>Sarasvati1 and asked her to bring Vasis2t2ha along with
<>her current. Sarasvati1 did not like the idea. Still,
<>fearing the curse of Vis4va1mitra she agreed to do so. The
<>river-basin where Vasis2t2ha had been performing penance
<>was pulled down by the current of the river, and taking
<>Vasis2t2ha in the current carried him down. Sarasvati1
<>did not wish to leave Vasis2t2ha at the mercy of Vis4va1-
<>mitra. So when they reached the door of the hermitage
<>of Vis4va1mitra, she carried him further down in a swift
<>current. Vis4va1mitra got angry and cursed Sarasvati1.
<>“Let blood flow through the river.” With that the
<>water of Sarasvati1 changed to blood-colour. The gods
<>were perplexed at this. They all gathered on the banks of
<>the river. In the presence of the gods, both the hermits
<>came to a compromise. Thenceforward that place
<>became a holy ti1rtha under the name Vasis2t2ha1pava1ha.
<>(M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 42).
<NI>3) {%Other details.%}

<HI>VA1SUKI. One of the famous Na1gas (serpents).
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} The uragas and na1gas (serpents) were born
<>to Praja1pati Kas4yapa by his wife Kadru1. Va1suki, the
<>eldest son of Kadru1 spent his childhood with his
<>parents.
<NI>2) {%Difference of opinion.%} Vinata1 and Kadru1 staked on
<>the colour of the tail of Uccais4s4ravas (A horse obtained
<>at the churning of the sea of Milk. To win the stake,
<>Kadru1 asked her sons to hang on the tail of Uccais4s4-
<>ravas as black hairs. But the truthful Va1suki was not
<>prepared to do so. Kadru1 cursed Va1suki and the others
<>who sided with him. From that day onwards Va1suki
<>and those who were cursed, separated from their mother
<>and formed into a serpent group. (M.B. A1di Parva,
<>Chapters 37 and 48)
<NI>3) {%The length of Va1suki.%} The Devas and the asuras
<>decided to get Amr2ta (Ambrosia--the celestial honey of
<>immortalily) by churning the sea of milk. The Devas
<>went to bring Mandara-mountain, to be used as the
<>churning rod. Their attempt was futile. The asuras
<>made a trial with the same result. The Bhu1tagan2as
<>(Guards) of S4iva also made a vain attempt. On the
<>instruction of Vis2n2u, Garud2a went and brought the
<>mountain as easily as an eagle takes away a frog. Now
<>Va1suki should be brought. The Devas and Gandharvas
<>failed in that attempt also. Garud2a who was haughty
<>of his strength and speed, went to the city of the na1gas
<>(serpents) and requested Va1suki to come to the sea of
<>Milk. Va1suki replied that if the matter was so urgent
<>he had no objection for being carried to that place. He
<>took the middle part of Va1suki in his beak and flew up
<>higher and higher and reached beyond the horizon.
<>Still the lower half of Va1suki was lying on the ground.
<>So he took Va1suki in his beak as folded in two. Still
<>the result was the same. Garud2a became aware of the
<>impossibility of carrying Va1suki and returned, ashamed
<>and disappointed. Vis2n2u rebuked him for his arrogance.
<>After this, S4iva stretched his hand to Pa1ta1la. Va1suki
<>became a small bangle on that hand. Thus Va1suki was
<>brought to the shore of the sea of Milk. (Kamba
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, Yuddha Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>4) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(i) For the safety and protection of the Na1gas (ser-
<>pents) Va1suki gave his sister Jaratka1ru as wife to the
<>hermit Jaratka1ru. (See under Jaratka1ru).

<>A1di Parva, Da1ks2in2a1tyapa1t2ha, Chapter 36).
<NI>(iii) When Va1suki saw the serpents, born of his family,
<>falling into the sacrificial fire of Janamejaya and dying [Page839-a+ 65]
<>in large numbers, he sent his nephew A1sti1ka to find
<>out a way to rescue the perishing na1gas. (M.B. A1di

<NI>(iv) Va1suki participated in the birth festival of Arjuna.
<>(M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 122, Stanza 71).
<NI>(v) Va1suki advised the Na1gas to give the divine juice
<>to Bhi1masena, in accordance with the request of A1ryaka.
<>(See under Bhi1masena).
<NI>(vi) Va1suki stays in the palace of Varun2a worshipping
<>him. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 9, Stanza 8).

<NI>(xii) Va1suki is one of the seven na1gas which hold up

<NI>(xiv) Once Va1suki and Va1yu tried to find out who

<HI>VA1YU I. One of the eight guardians of the world.
<>(Dikpa1lakas).
<NI>1) {%General information.%} Va1yu was born from the breath
<>of Vis4vapurus2a. (R2gveda 10, 90). Tvas2t2a1 was the son
<>in-law of Va1yu. The eight dikpa1lakas are Indra, Vahni
<>(Fire), Yama, Nirr2ti, Varun2a, Va1yu, Kubera and S4iva.
<>Va1yu is the guardian of the North West zone. The
<>palace of Bhagava1n Va1yu is known as Gandhavati1.
<NI>2) {%Confrontation with Va1suki.%} Once Va1yu and Va1suki
<>tried to find out who of the two was mightier, in con-
<>sequence of which, Triku1t2aparvata, a peak of Mount [Page841-a+ 64]
<>Hima1laya, was broken from its bed and thrown into
<>the southern ocean near Lan3ka1. Later, the city of
<>Lan3ka1 was built on this mountain. (For details see
<>under Triku1t2a).
<NI>3) {%Children.%} Bhi1masena and Hanu1ma1n are said to be
<>the prominent sons of Bhagava1n Va1yu. It is mentioned
<>in R2gveda, Man2d2ala 1, Anuva1ka 7, Su1kta 112, that
<>Agni was the son of Va1yu. To make fire from wood by
<>attrition, the hands obtain strength by the help of the
<>life breath called Vya1na (a breath or va1yu). It is from
<>this, that the idea that fire is the son of Va1yu, came in-
<>to existence. To know how Va1yu obtained the paternity
<>of Bhi1ma and Hanu1ma1n see under Bhi1ma and Hanu1ma1n.
<>It is stated in Brahma Pura1n2a that a group of celestial
<>maids called ‘Muda1’ owes its origin to Va1yu.
<NI>4) {%Love of Va1yu.%} Once Bhagava1n Va1yu happened to see
<>the six beautiful daughters of Kus4ana1bha, a king, and
<>he was excited sexually. As the princesses refused to
<>satisfy his desire, Va1yu changed the damsels into hunch-
<>backs, by a curse. (For detailed story see under Kus4a-
<>na1bha).
<NI>5) {%The Va1yus (breaths) in the body.%} (See under Na1d2i1cakra).
<NI>6) {%Curbing the arrogance of Silk-cotton tree.%} Long ago a
<>silk-cotton tree grew up on the top of the Hima1layas.
<>It grew up to be a big tree spreading its branches in
<>all directions. Birds built their nests on the branches of
<>that big tree and hermits and animals found shelter
<>under its shade. The silk-cotton tree became arrogant.
<>Once Na1rada came there and praised the tree:--“How
<>gigantic, this silk-cotton tree is! Even in a great storm
<>its branches do not move.” The praise of Na1rada made
<>the tree more haughty. It told Na1rada that the storm
<>etc. were its servants. Na1rada told Va1yu, what the
<>silk-cotton tree said to him. Va1yu got angry and com-
<>ing to the tree, said thus:- “You, wicked silk-cotton
<>tree, in days of yore, when Brahma1 was carrying on
<>creation he took rest on you for a while. That is why I
<>keep motionless when I come to you. It is not because
<>I am afraid of you. I knew that you belittled me before
<>Na1rada. If you are bold enough, come and fight with
<>me.”
<NI>The silk-cotton tree accepted the challenge. Next day
<>Bhagava1n Va1yu changed into a storm and blew against
<>the tree. The tree lost leaves, flowers and fruits and
<>stood bare. Thus within a very short time the arrogance
<>of the tree was curbed.
<NI>This story was told to Yudhis2t2hira by Bhi1s2ma, to show
<>that rendering help to a foe, will only make him
<>haughty. (M.B. S4a1nti Parva, 3 Chapters from 154).

<HI>VEDA. The sun of the hermit Ayodhadhaumya. (For
<>further details see under Ayodhadhaumya).
<HI>VEDA (S).
<NI>1) {%Introduction.%} The root ‘Vid’ in Sanskrit means ‘to
<>know’. The books composed of the knowledge of the [Page842-a+ 65]
<>A1ryans, collected and compiled were called the Vedas.
<NI>2) {%The origin and importance of the Vedas.%} It is said that
<>Brahma1 was the creator of the Vedas. Many say that,
<>for the performance of sacrifices Brahma1 created from
<>Agni (fire), Va1yu (Wind) and Ravi (the Sun), the
<>three Vedas R2g, Yajus and Sa1man which are eternal and
<>in accordance with regulations.
<NI>The Vedas have two sides, the spiritual and the mun-
<>dane. Moreover all the explanations of the Vedas
<>from the Bra1hman2as to the Bha1s2ya of Sa1yan2a, pertain
<>to sacrifices. The literary meaning of Veda is mundane.
<>Considering these two points and the facts that they are
<>religious books and that they reveal the mental and
<>social outlooks of the early A1ryans, and as the origin of
<>all the thoughts of Indians, all the Vedas, especially
<>R2gveda, hold a lofty place.
<NI>3) {%Parts of the Vedas.%} Maha1vis2n2u incarnated as Vya1sa
<>in Dva1para Yuga and divided the Vedas. The first
<>Veda consisted of four pa1das and a hundred thousand
<>granthas. Vya1sa divided it into four parts known as,
<>R2gveda, Yajurveda, Sa1maveda and Atharvaveda.
<>The sage and seer Vya1sa thus created by R2g, “Hautra”
<>(a collection of lyrics in praise of different Gods, to be
<>recited by the priest styled the ‘hotr2’), by Yajus
<>“A1dhvaryava”, (a book of sacrificial prayer, of prose
<>formulas to be uttered by the ‘Adhvaryu’ priest, who
<>performed the manual work involved in a sacrifice), by
<>Sa1man, “Audga1tra”, (Songs meant to be sung at the
<>Soma sacrifice by a special class of priests ‘Udga1tr2’) and
<>by Atharva “Brahmatva”, (a collection of songs, spells
<>and incantations, for the healing of disease, the restora-
<>tion of harmony, the exorcism of evil spirits and to
<>celebrate the power and omniscience of God) and
<>established them separately. Paila, a scholar in R2g-
<>veda, and a disciple of Vya1sa, gave the original Veda
<>to Indrapramiti and its Sam3hita1 (collections) to Ba1s2kala
<>as gifts. Ba1s2kala divided the Sam3hita1 into four, and
<>gave each of them to Baudhya and others. Yajurveda
<>was divided into twentyfour branches. They were com-
<>posed by Vais4ampa1yana of extraordinary intelligence,
<>a disciple of Vya1sa. Ka1n2d2as (Chapters) such as Va1ja-
<>saneya etc. were composed later by hermits such as
<>Ya1jn5avalkya and others in the form of Smr2tis (tradition,
<>as handing down only the tradition derived from an-
<>cient sages, to whom the Vedas were ‘revealed’).
<>Jaimini the disciple of Vya1sa, divided Sa1maveda into
<>different branches. Sumantu and Sukarma1 compiled
<>separate Sam3hita1s (collections) from them. Sukarma1
<>composed thousand Sam3hita1s. Sumantu the disciple
<>of Vya1sa took the Atharvaveda and taught it to
<>thousands of Paippala1da disciples. By the blessings of
<>Vya1sa, Su1ta made the Pura1n2a Sam3hita1 (the collection
<>of Pura1n2as). (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 150).
<NI>4) {%Branches af the Vedas.%} It has already been mentioned
<>that the Vedas are four in number called R2g, Yajus,
<>Sa1ma and Atharva. Each of these Vedas has branches
<>called Sam3hita1s (collections) and Bra1hman2as (treatises
<>relating to prayer and sacrificial ceremony). A1ran2yakas
<>are appendices to the Bra1hman2as. Upanis2ads (secret
<>or esoteric doctrines) are appendices of the A1ran2yakas.
<>Thus each of the Vedas has Sam3hita1s, Bra1hman2as,
<>A1ran2yakas and Upanis2ads. All these are, in a way, ex-
<>positions of the Vedas. In all these expositions there
<>are numberless Su1ktas. All these taken together are
<>called Vedic literature. [Page842-b+ 65]
<>In the Sam3hita1s there are lyrics in praise of different gods.
<>All these are spells and incantations (mantras) in the
<>form of songs. The under-currents of all these spells are
<>Vedas and stories from the Vedas. Generally speaking
<>the Bra1hman2as contain prose texts giving practical
<>observations on sacrifice which are mentioned in the
<>songs of praise. Here and there stories from Pura1n2as
<>and epics occur. The A1ran2yakas (forest-texts) got that
<>name, because they are books of instruction to be given
<>in the forest or writings meant for wood-dwelling her-
<>mits. As they contained esoteric spells and incanta-
<>tions which might cause injury even to those who were
<>not concerned with them instruction in them was not
<>given in towns or villages. The contents of the A1ran2y-
<>akas are the allegorical signification of the rites and
<>sacrifices and the mystic meaning of the Vedas.
<NI>It has been mentioned that the Upanis2ads are append-
<>ices of the A1ran2yakas. Still there is not much differ-
<>ence between the two and hence they cannot be separat-
<>ed from one another so easily. The Upanis2ads are
<>called Veda1ntas, (The aim and completion of the
<>Vedas), because they are the end of the Vedas. The
<>Veda1ntas belong to the later period of the Vedic age.
<>Instruction in Veda1ntas was given only after completing
<>the study of the mantras (Vedas) and the Bra1hman2as.
<>The Upanis2ads contain philosophical speculations about
<>the conception of Brahman and the Vedas. The word
<>Veda includes the Veda1n3gas also. Veda1n3gas (Ancillaries
<>of the Vedas) are S4iks2a1 (phonetics), Vya1karan2a
<>(grammar), Chandas (metrics), Nirukta (etymology),
<>Joytis2a (astronomy), and Kalpa (ritual). At first
<>instruction is given in the study of Brahman and the
<>study of Vedas. When more discussions and expositions
<>on them are needed, the study of Bra1hman2as and
<>A1ran2yakas are resorted to. In course of time the
<>Bra1hman2as and A1ran2yakas, which contain discussions
<>and expositions of the Vedas became independent
<>branches of study under these names. That is why they
<>contain mainly prose texts.
<NI>There are one lakh of mantras or spells and incanta-
<>tions in all the four Vedas taken together. They are
<>for blessing everybody and to make the four objects of
<>life easily attainable. Sa1n3khya1yana and A1s4vala1yana
<>sorted and grouped the mantras. Accordingly there
<>are two thousand one hundred and ten mantras known
<>as Bra1hman2as. Dvaipa1yana and other hermits have
<>stated the number of granthas (verses) in R2gveda. It
<>is said that there are one thousand nine hundred and
<>ninety nine mantras in Yajurveda. There are one
<>thousand eightysix branches also. The branches in
<>Yajus are known by the names Ka1n2vi1, Ma1dhyandini1,
<>Kat2hi1, Madhyakat2hi1, Maitra1yan2i1, Taittiri1ya, Vais4am-
<>pa1yanika1 and so on.
<NI>In Sa1ma Veda there are branches such as Kauthumi1,
<>A1tharvan2a1yini1 and so on; and songs such as A1ran2yakam,
<>Uktham, U1ham and son on. The number of Sa1mavedic
<>verses are nine thousand four hundred and twenty-
<>five.
<NI>Sumantu, Jan5jali, S4loka1yani, S4aunaka, Pippala1da,
<>Mun5jakes4a and such others were responsible for the
<>sorting and grouping of the mantras in the Atharva-
<>veda. Altogether there are one thousand six hundred
<>Upanis2ads. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 271).
<NI>5) {%Expositions of the Vedas.%} The religion of the A1ryans
<>became an established one when Vya1sa had created the [Page843-a+ 65]
<>Vedasam3hita1s. The aim of Vya1sa was to bring about
<>uniformity in the religious observances by performing
<>rites and rituals, religious ceremonies such as sacrifices
<>etc. without any flaw. Following this a very wide
<>Vedic literature grew up. The study of the Vedas,
<>critical review of meaning, the connection between
<>mantras and tantras (Chants and rituals), Grammar,
<>etymology, all these became inevitable. As the Vedas
<>could be looked at from different points of view, from
<>very early days, various types of expositions of the
<>Vedas came out. Seven types of commentaries,
<>Nairukta, Ya1jn5ika, Vaiya1karan2a Jyautis2a, Sa1mprada1yika
<>A1dhya1tmika, Aitiha1sika (pertaining to etymology,
<>sacrifice, grammar astronomy, customs of a tribe,
<>Bra1hma or Spiritual legends and ancient lore) were
<>the prominent among them. When the westerners
<>began research work in the Vedas, another branch of
<>literature also took form, known as the A1gamikas
<>(the historical). Even in the time of Ya1ska, who
<>was an authority on Nirukta (etymology) the Vedas
<>were expounded on the basis of legends and ancient
<>lore. Hints to this effect are seen in Ya1ska's works.
<>Mention is made about other types of expositions also,
<>in them. The last book of importance in the Sa1mpra-
<>da1yika type of exposition, was Veda1rthapraka1s4a, of
<>Sa1yan2a. But the commentary of Skandasva1mi1, the
<>books R2garthadipika1 and Udgi1thabha1s2ya of Ma1dhava1-
<>ca1rya etc. have spread the sa1mprada1yika type of
<>speculations in India. There is another cult in India
<>which believes that everything seen in the Vedas is
<>spiritual and that the spells and incantations are
<>esoteric.
<NI>6) {%The gods of the Vedas.%} All the gods known today
<>are not found in the Vedas. Even those who are found
<>do not have the prominence that is given to them now.
<>For example, the deity Vis2n2u, worshipped as one of
<>the three godheads today, is not as omnipotent as the
<>Indra of the Vedas. Though Vis2n2u is praised in five
<>spells in R2gveda, when compared with other deities,
<>he was not of much prominence. In the Vedas the
<>incarnation of Va1mana is not given much importance.
<>Even though Rudra is a recognized deity and mention
<>occurs about Kapardi1, in the Vedas, there is not a song
<>of praise of S4iva in the R2gveda. There is no mention
<>at all, about the worship of the Phallus, Devi1 and so
<>on in the Vedas. The Vedas celebrate the Omniscience
<>of Varun2a, Indra, Agni, Mitra, the Maruts and so on.
<>The lustre of Indra was gradually dimmed with the
<>efflux of time and he became a mere libertine in the
<>Pura1n2as. The importance of many deities such as
<>Varun2a, Agni, Mitra, As4vin and so on was greatly dimi-
<>nished in the Pura1n2as. Thirtythree deities pertaining
<>to Earth, Ether and Sky are praised in the Vedas.
<NI>7) {%The rites concerning instruction in Vedas.%} Manu has
<>given certain instructions as to how the teaching of
<>Vedas should be conducted.
<NI>8) {%The teachers of Veda.%} See under Guruparampara1.
<NI>9) {%The period of the Vedas.%} The Indians believe that the
<>spells and incantations and the Bra1hman2as are not man-
<>made, but are revelations by God. So they are con-
<>sidered to be beginningless and endless. The hermits
<>are considered to be seers of the spells. It is mentioned
<>“Mantradras2t2a1rah2 na tu Karta1rah2”. (They are seers
<>of Mantra, not makers). In the opinion of one party,
<>according to this maxim, it is not necessarry to search [Page843-b+ 64]
<>for the creators or the period of creation, of mantras.
<>They hold that only the Su1tras (thread, clue, guide,
<>rule, aphorism) are made by man.
<NI>The westerners and the modern thinkers of India do
<>not agree completely with this view. They believe
<>that the early A1ryans who entered India, first settled
<>down in the Punjab and that the local civilization which
<>they had assumed, broke out as songs of praise. In
<>course of time, these songs of Praise took the form of
<>R2gveda mantras (saying, song, formula). In the begin-
<>ning there was only one Veda. Many believe that this
<>state continued up to B.C. 1500. It is a fact universally
<>acknowledged, that this Vedic literature is the most
<>ancient literature of the world. There is difference of
<>opinion as to the period of origin of this Vedic literature.
<>Prof. Macdonell and Prof. Jacobi are foremost among
<>those who have made their opinions. In the opinion
<>of Prof. Macdonell, the Vedic literature originated in
<>the period between B.C. 1500 and 1200. But Jacobi's
<>opinion is that all the Vedas were made before B.C.
<>4000.

<HI>VENA I. An ancient King who was notorious for his
<>bad rule.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u in the following
<>order: Brahma1-Sva1yambhuva Manu-Utta1napa1da-
<>Dhruva-S4is2t2i-Ripu-Ca1ks2us2a Manu-Kuru-An3ga-Vena.
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Ten sons including Kuru, were born to
<>Manu Ca1ks2us2a by his wife Nad2vala1. A1gneyi1, the wife
<>of Kuru gave birth to six sons An3ga and others. An3ga
<>married Suni1tha1. The son Vena was born by Suni1tha1.
<>It is mentioned in Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 1, Chapter
<>13, that Suni1tha1, the mother of Vena was the eldest
<>daughter of Yama, and in Va1mana Pura1n2a Chap-
<>ter 47, that this Suni1tha1 was the daughter born to Ka1la [Page844-b+ 64]
<>and Mr2tyu. Vena who was born as the son of the
<>daughter of Yama was born a wicked man because of
<>the badness of his grandfather.
<NI>Vena grew up as a great sinner. A story occurs in
<>Padma Pura1n2a about an incident which caused Vena
<>to become a great sinner.
<NI>The story says that Suni1tha1 was the mother of Vena. In
<>her play she used to imitate her father's job which was
<>hurting others. One day when she went to the forest
<>for play with her maids she saw Sus4an3kha the son of
<>a Gandharva named Gi1takola1hala. That youth who
<>was handsome in every part of his body, was doing
<>penance meditating on the goddess Sarasvati1 for the
<>attainment of the art of music. Suni1tha1 began to annoy
<>him daily. Sus4an3kha bore this annoyance patiently.
<>He said only this-“Go away, go away.” But she did
<>not pay heed to his appeals. Once she became angry
<>and struck him. Getting angry at this, Sus4an3kha called
<>out, “You wicked girl! Why do you annoy me? You
<>have beaten me, who am engaged in penance, for no
<>cause.” That righteous Gandharva youth curbed his
<>anger considering that she was a woman.
<NI>She told him “My father will beat everybody in the
<>three worlds. He will harm the wicked, and will not
<>do any harm to the good. So he rules the country
<>righteously.” Suni1tha1 told all about the story to her
<>father. Though Yama, the incarnation of righteousness,
<>heard all that his daughter said he did not give any
<>reply.
<NI>She again went to the forest and struck him with a
<>whip. Sus4an3ka rose up, shivering with pain and cursed
<>her thus:
<NI>“You wicked girl! When you become a house-holder
<>and join your husband you will get a son who will be a
<>scoffer of Devas and Brahmins and an all-round sinner.”
<>Thus cursing her he continued his penance. Vena was
<>born according to this curse.
<NI>3) {%The wicked rule of Vena.%} The hermits anointed Vena
<>as the King. He became lord of the whole earth. He
<>made a proclamation thus:--“Sacrifice is prohibited.
<>Giving alms, is prohibited. Offerings of no kind should
<>be made. Who else, other than I, can be the Yajn5a-
<>purus2a? (the deity who eats the food of sacrifice)? I
<>am the only lord and consumer of sacrifices.”
<NI>Hearing this proclamation, the hermits approached
<>him and told him that the Vedas were the basis of
<>righteousness, that sacrifices were ordained in them, and
<>that if no sacrifice was performed the Devas would not
<>be pleased and so on. But this did not make any change
<>in his behaviour.
<NI>The hermits became angry. They all gathered together
<>and recited spells and struck him with Darbha (poa)
<>grass and killed him. As there was no King the world
<>fell into darkness. Because of the troubles caused by
<>plunderers, people gathered round the hermits. Hermits
<>joined together and churned the left hand of Vena,
<>from which a man of short stature came out, to whom
<>the hermits said, ‘Nis2i1da’ (sit). Because the hermits
<>said Nis2i1da’, Nis2a1da came into existence from that
<>dwarf born of the sins of Vena. Then the hermits
<>churned the right hand of Vena. At this churning a
<>person as big as a mountain with divine signs came out.
<>He had bow and arrows and in his palm there were
<>marks of a wheel and flag. Seeing this the Devas [Page845-a+ 65]
<>anointed him the King of the earth. He ruled righte-
<>ously and brought the goddess earth (Pr2thvi1) who had
<>estranged herself from his father Vena close to him.
<>Because he had conciliated Pr2thvi1 he became famous
<>as a King. (Va1mana Pura1n2a, Chapter 47).

<HI>VIDHAVA1 A woman whose husband is dead. In
<>ancient India, it was ordained how a widow should
<>live. It was allowed for a widow to get a son by her
<>younger brother-in law to continue the family line in
<>case the death of her husband occurred before the
<>couple had children. The procedure about this is
<>given in Manusmr2ti, Chapter 9.
<NI>“He who goes to accept the widow with the permission
<>of great people, should besmear his body with ghee and
<>go to her bed in the night in a dark room. She should
<>have only one son in this manner. After she has become
<>pregnant, they should behave to each other as a
<>teacher and a younger brother-in-law.”

<NI>Vidula1 was a brave woman, who lived in ancient India.

<HI>VIDURA I.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} Vidura was a superhuman
<>being, very famous in the story of Maha1bha1rata, as a
<>brother of Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, as a man of colossal intelligence
<>who had been closely watching the goings and comings
<>of the Kauravas and the Pa1n2d2avas, as the adviser of
<>Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, and as a man of immense learning and
<>wisdom.
<NI>2) {%Incarnation af Dharmadeva.%} There is a story in Maha1-
<>Bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 107, which describes
<>Vidura as born from a portion of Dharmadeva. The
<>story is given below.
<NI>Long ago there was a hermit called Ma1n2d2avya in India.
<>As he was standing in deep meditation near his hermit-
<>age, the men of the King chased some thieves and came
<>to the place where the hermit stood. The robbers
<>placed the stolen property near the hermit and ran
<>away. The king's men caught the hermit, and the
<>thieves. The King ordered them to be placed on a
<>trident. The thieves died on the trident. But Ma1n2d2avya
<>was not dead. The King sawed the trident and got
<>Ma1n2d2avya down. The hermit went to Dharmadeva and
<>asked him what his blame was for suffering the punish-
<>ment of the trident on him. Dharmadeva replied that
<>the punishment was inflicted for a cruel deed he had
<>done in his childhood. He had caught some flies and
<>made a bunch of them by piercing them with the rib of
<>a coconut-palm leaf. But Ma1n2d2avya argued that
<>Dharmadeva was not right in punishing him because
<>the S4a1stras and rules of righteousness said that mistakes
<>committed by boys below the age of twelve could not
<>be considered to be sins. Further he cursed Dharmadeva
<>that he would take birth on the earth from the womb
<>of a S4u1dra1. Accordingly Dharmadeva took birth from
<>the womb of the servant of Ambika1 and Amba1lika1.
<NI>3) {%Birth.%} Vidura was born as the brother of Dhr2ta-
<>ra1s2t2ra and Pa1n2d2u. (For detailed story see under Dhr2ta-
<>ra1s2t2ra I, para 2).
<NI>4) {%Up to marriage.%} Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, Pa1n2d2u and Vidura
<>spent their younger days in Hastina1pura as inseparable
<>brothers. Their teacher was Bhi1s2ma. Vidura learned
<>the Vedas, S4a1stras, Pura1n2as, Itiha1sas etc. also, along
<>with the education given to a prince such as archery,
<>club-fight, sword-fight, wrestling, controlling elephants
<>etc. He understood that to be righteous was far better [Page848-b+ 64]
<>than fighting. It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva,
<>Chapter 108, that Vidura got the sense of righteousness,
<>and education in fighting, at the same time.
<NI>Childhood ended. As Vidura was born to a Brahmin
<>by a S4u1dra1 woman he had no right to become King.
<>When he grew up, the duty to find a wife for him fell
<>on Bhi1s2ma. At that time a damsel born to a Brahmin
<>by a S4u1dra1 woman was being brought up in the palace
<>of King Devaka. With the permission of Devaka, Bhi1s2ma
<>brought the girl and gave her in marriage to Vidura.
<>It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 114,
<>that sons and daughters were born to the couple.
<NI>5) {%Partiality towards the Pa1n2d2avas.%} Vidura was the most
<>intelligent and wisest man of his time, and he always
<>favoured righteousness. Though he viewed the Kauravas
<>and the Pa1n2d2avas with equal favour, in his heart he
<>felt some partiality towards the Pa1n2d2avas, because they
<>were virtuous, whereas the Kauravas were becoming
<>more and more wicked. As this partiality arose from
<>his sense of righteousness, nobody could blame Vidura
<>for this. His aim was the prosperity of the Lunar
<>dynasty of Kings.
<NI>Many ill omens were seen at the time of the birth
<>of Duryodhana. Vidura understood that if that infant
<>grew up he would be a comet to the Lunar dynasty. It
<>is mentioned in Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 115,
<>that Vidura advised Dhr2tara1s2t2ra that it would be better
<>for him to throw away that infant.
<NI>The death of Pa1n2d2u drew Vidura closer to the Pa1n2d2avas.
<>Vidura took the lead in performing the funeral rites
<>and other ceremonies which followed. The Pa1n2d2avas.
<>were very sad and miserable at the death of their father.
<>It was at this time that Duryodhana poisoned Bhi1ma-
<>sena, tied him with a rope and threw him into the
<>river Ganges. Bhi1masena was carried to the world of
<>na1gas (serpents). Kunti1 felt grieved at the loss of her
<>son Bhi1ma, but Vidura consoled her.
<NI>It was due to the wisdom of Vidura that the Pa1n2d2avas
<>escaped from the disaster in the lac-house. As soon as
<>Duryodhana had completed the lac-house, Vidura
<>understood the deception lying hidden under it.
<>and he informed the Pa1n2d2avas of everything about it.
<>Moreover he sent a man named Khanaka and made
<>an underground passage from the lac-house. When the
<>lac-house was burnt down, the Pa1n2d2avas escaped by
<>the under-ground passage and reached the banks of
<>the Ganges. Vidura had sent a ferryman secretly to
<>take them to the other side of the Ganges. When
<>Bhi1s2ma got the news that the Pa1n2d2avas had been burnt
<>to death in the lac-house he became very sad. It is
<>mentioned in Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter
<>149, that Vidura informed Bhi1s2ma secretly that the
<>Pa1n2d2avas were not dead.
<NI>After this event the Pa1n2d2avas come into the scene only
<>at the Svayam3vara (marriage) of Pa1n5ca1li1. Bhi1s2ma and
<>Dron2a proposed that the Pa1n2d2avas should be brought
<>back and be given half of the kingdom. Vidura, by his
<>arugments convinced Dhr2tara1s2t2ra that the proposal of
<>Bhi1s2ma and Dron2a was correct. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra asked
<>Vidura to bring the Pa1n2d2avas back. Vidura went to
<>the city of Drupada and brought the Pa1n2d2avas back
<>and consoled their mother Kunti1. After this Yudhis2t2hira
<>performed Ra1jasu1ya (sacrifice of royal consecration).
<>Vidura took part in it and took the charge of financial [Page849-a+ 65]
<>part of the sacrifice. It was after this that Duryodhana
<>challenged Yudhis2t2hira for a game of dice. Vidura saw
<>beforehand that this move on the part of Duryodhana
<>was dangerous. So he talked forcibly against this, and
<>gave warning to all concerned. As Duryodhana did not
<>agree with Vidura, he was scolded. But Duryodhana
<>was firm and the game was conducted. Pa1n5ca1li1 was
<>harassed by means of stripping and the Pa1n2d2avas went
<>to the forest. It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, Vana Parva,
<>Chapter 3, that Vidura had been witnessing all these
<>scenes with wet eyes.
<NI>6) {%Separated from the Pa1n2d2avas.%} When the Pa1n2d2avas
<>were driven to the forest, Dhr2tara1s2t2ra felt sorry. He
<>became more afraid of the people than he was sorry
<>for the Pa1n2d2avas. He understood that his subjects
<>would unite and rise against his sons. He called
<>Vidura and asked him for a remedy. Vidura who was
<>full of impatience, made a speech against the wicked-
<>ness of the sons of Dhr2tara1s2t2ra and advised him to
<>forsake his own sons and to bring the Pa1n2d2avas back
<>and give them the kingdom. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra did not like
<>this approach. He said that Vidura was partial to the
<>Pa1n2d2avas and asked him to go away from the palace.
<>Vidura became grieved at this. He followed the
<>Pa1n2d2avas, and walking a long distance, reached the
<>forest Ka1myaka and met the Pa1n2d2avas.
<NI>When Vidura had gone Dhr2tara1s2t2ra felt miserable.
<>He sent for Vidura and when he returned Dhr2tara1s2t2ra
<>begged for pardon. Vidura again became the adviser
<>of Dhr2tara1s2t2ra. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter 6).
<NI>7) {%Adviser of Dhr2tara1s2t2ra.%} The advice of Vidura is
<>famous in Maha1bha1rata. The main duty of Vidura was
<>to console Dhr2tara1s2t2ra by speaking about righteousness
<>when he became troubled in mind because of the const-
<>ant quarrels between his sons and the Pa1n2d2avas.
<>Vidura fulfilled his duty well. Duryodhana was firm on
<>the point that not a dot of land would be given to the
<>Pa1n2d2avas. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a came to Hastina1pura as mediator.
<>Duryodhana showed disrespect to him. Vidura said
<>in strong words that it was wrong on the part of
<>Duryodhana to have done so and compelled Dhr2ta-
<>ra1s2t2ra to show due respect and hospitality to S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a.
<>Accordingly Dhr2tara1s2t2ra welcomed S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and
<>showed respect and hospitality. Knowing this, Duryo-
<>dhana and his brothers tried to make S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a a
<>captive. Vidura harshly scolded them for this attempt.
<>Seeing all these impudent actions on the part of his
<>sons, Dhr2tara1s2t2ra became distressed. Vidura consoled
<>him by good exhortations. He told Dhr2tara1s2t2ra about
<>the transience of life and the importance of the soul.
<>When the battle was fiercely going on in the battle-
<>ground of Kuruks2etra, Vidura re ained with Dhrta-
<>ra1s2t2ra consoling him and giving him good advice. The
<>death of Bhi1s2ma was an unbearable grief to Vidura. He
<>took part in the funeral of Bhi1s2ma. He himself placed
<>the body on the funeral pyre. (M.B. Anus4a1sana Parva,
<>Chapter 168, Stanza 11).
<NI>8) {%Pilgrimage.%} When Vidura failed in his attempt to
<>ward off a pitched battle between the Kauravas and
<>the Pa1n2d2avas he felt extremely miserable. Without
<>taking part in the battle, he started on a pilgrimage.
<>Getting the news at Prabha1saksetra about the end of
<>the battle, he went to the basin of river Yamuna1. On
<>the way he heard the news of the passing away of S4ri1
<>Kr2s2n2a, from Uddhava. Before death S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a had [Page849-b+ 64]
<>revealed that Vidura had heard Uddhavagi1ta1 from
<>Maitreya. This book which is in the form of a conver-
<>sation between Vidura and Maitreya contains the talk
<>between Kapila and Devahu1ti. Description of the line
<>of Manus, sacrifice of Daks2a, story of Dhruva, story
<>of Pr2thu, story of Puran5jana etc. were the subjects of the
<>talk. (Bha1gavata, 3--4).
<NI>9) {%End.%} The Bha1rata-battle came to an end. The
<>Kauravas were exterminated. Efforts were begun to
<>establish law and order. In all these efforts Vidura was
<>a help to the Pa1n2d2avas. Still he spent most of his time
<>with the old Dhr2tara1s2t2ra. Yudhis2t2hira came to Dhr2ta-
<>ra1s2t2ra and both embraced each other. Seeing this
<>Vidura cried aloud. Vidura advised Yudhis2t2hira how
<>to carry on the administration of the new government.
<>After this he decided to go to the forest to spend his
<>last days. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, Ga1ndha1ri1, Kunti1, Vidura and
<>S4akuni went to the forest. The Pa1n2d2avas tried in vain
<>to prevent them from going. When Dhr2tara1s2t2ra,
<>Ga1ndha1ri1, Kunti1, Vidura, San5jaya and others started
<>for the forest, even Bhi1masena cried aloud. Pa1n2d2avas
<>and the people of the city went along with them up to
<>the river Ganges. On the bank of the Ganges near the
<>hermitage of S4atayu1pa, a hermitage was erected and
<>Vidura and the others lived there.
<NI>They lived there for nearly six years. The Pa1n2d2avas be-
<>came unable to bear the separation from their elders.
<>Once Dharmaputra dreamt about his mother. Next day
<>the Pa1n2d2avas went to the banks of the Ganges. Pa1n5ca1li1,
<>Subhadra1, Uttara1 and many people of the city followed
<>them. They went to the S4atayu1pa-hermitage and saw
<>Dhr2tara1s2t2ra and the others. But the great Vidura was
<>not there. When asked about it he got the reply that
<>having become abstemious and having no more desires
<>he was wandering about. Yudhis2t2hira was greatly
<>troubled.
<NI>Next day at dawn when Yudhis2t2hira went to bathe in
<>the Ganges, on the way he saw Vidura sitting in contem-
<>plation with a stone in his mouth. Yudhis2t2hira stood
<>with joined palms before the lean and weak form of
<>that sage and said “Look, Dharmaputra bows before
<>you.” He repeated this several times. But there was
<>no change in Vidura. Yudhis2t2hira's disappointment
<>did not last long. For, in a short while Dharmaputra
<>saw that a divine radiance emanated from the body
<>of Vidura and passed on to his body and that the body
<>of Vidura fell lifeless on the ground. This union took
<>place because both Vidura and Yudhis2t2hira were port-
<>ions of Dharmadeva. After this Dharmaputra made
<>preparations to burn the body of Vidura. Then an ethe-
<>real voice said “Vidura is abstemious. His body should
<>not be burned.” Dharmaputra went to the hermitage
<>and informed all, about the death of Vidura. (M.B.
<>A1s4ramava1sika Parva, Chapters 26 to 28).
<NI>Mention is made in Maha1bha1rata, Svarga1rohan2a Parva,
<>Chapter 5, Stanza 22, that Vidura entered Svarga
<>(heaven) and stays there in the form of Dharmadeva.
<HI>VIDURA II. A Ks2atriya who lived in Pa1n5ca1la. The
<>story of this Vidura, who had killed a Brahmin because
<>of his want, is given in Padma Pura1n2a, Bhu1mikhand2a,
<>Chapter 91. The story is given below:
<NI>Long ago there lived a Ks2atriya, named Vidura, in
<>Pa1n5ca1la. Because of penury he killed a Brahmin. After
<>that the Ks2atriya discarded his lock of hair and Brahma- [Page850-a+ 64]
<>string and went to every house saying “Look, here am
<>I, a slayer of Brahmin. Please, give alms to the drunk-
<>ard and killer of a Brahmin.” Saying thus he walked
<>from house to house and took alms. But he did not get
<>remission of Brahmahatya1 (killing of a Brahmin).
<NI>Filled with grief and sorrow and mental worry the
<>sinner Vidura sat in the shade of a tree. At that time
<>Candras4arma1, a Brahmin of Magadha came there. He
<>was a wicked man who had killed his teacher because
<>of inordinate lust in consequence of which he had been
<>forsaken by his own people. Vidura asked Candras4arma1
<>who wore no sign of a Bra1hman2a, what he was. Candra-
<>s4arma1 told his story to Vidura who in return told him
<>his sinful acts.
<NI>At this time another Brahmin named Vedas4arma1 came
<>there. He also was a sinner. The three of them told each
<>other about their sinful acts. At this time Valla1la, a
<>Vais4ya came there. He was a drunkard who had killed
<>cows. These four desperate sinners travelled together
<>and visited several ti1rthas (holy baths). But they did not
<>get remission of their sins. At last they started for
<>Ka1lan5jaragiri.
<NI>While these desperate sinners were living in Ka1lan5jara
<>a poor Brahmin came there. He asked them why they
<>were so sad. They told him everything. When he heard
<>their stories he felt pity for them. The old Brahmin said
<>“You sinners should go to Praya1ga, Pus2kara, Sarvati1rtha
<>and Va1ra1n2asi1 and bathe in the Gan3ga1 on New Moon days
<>and you will become free from sin.” They obeyed the
<>old Brahmin and went to the holy places told by him.
<>Thus Vidura and his friends became sinless.

<HI>VIGRAHA III. (Image).
<NI>1) {%General information.%} God, who is not discernible to
<>the outward senses, is given embodiment and consecrat-
<>ed in places of worship by people. These figures are
<>called Vigrahas (idols or images). People worship
<>S4a1lagra1ma, (a kind of ammonite found in the river
<>Gan2d2aki1), Ba1n2alin3ga (Phallus), mystical diagrams,
<>animals, birds, trees, rivers, lakes, places of death etc.
<>and so many other things.
<NI>2) {%The different kinds of Vigrahas.%} Vigrahas are of three
<>groups. They are:-(1) Mysterious (2) Evident-mysteri-
<>ous (3) Evident. S4a1lagra1ma, Phallus etc. belong to the
<>group mysterious. The Mukhalin3ga, considered to be
<>the image of Trimu1rti (Brahma1, Vis2n2u, S4iva) in the
<>Elephanta cave, belongs to the group Evident. The
<>image of Phallus is Evident-mysterious. In the first,
<>there is no form or figure. In the second there are parts
<>of the figure. The image which shows the entire figure
<>is Evident.
<NI>Some gods and goddesses possess two appearances,
<>peaceful and fierce. Those who wish to succeed in
<>performing cruel deeds and to vanquish enemies, wor-
<>ship fierce gods whereas those who wish for peace,
<>worship peaceful Gods. Vis4varu1pa, (assuming all
<>shapes), Narasim3ha (the man-lion), Vat2apatras4a1yi1 (as
<>lying on banyan-leaf) and Paras4ura1ma are fierce forms
<>of Vis2n2u. Destroying Ka1ma (cupid) Gaja and Tripura
<>are fierce forms of S4iva worshipped by some people.
<>Generally temples with images in this form are erected
<>outside the village or city.
<NI>Owing to the differences, in the making and the mater-
<>ials used, the images are divided into three groups. [Page852-a+ 64]
<>They are portraits, Half-portraits and corrupted
<>portraits. The whole of the figure will be made in
<>(portraits) citras. In half-citras only the front view
<>will be completed. Corrupted Citras are images done
<>on the wall or rock.
<NI>3) {%Main images.%} Main images are mostly S4aivite or
<>Vais2n2avite. There will be one or two other images.
<>Worship of Devi1 (Goddess) is one of them. The S4aivi-
<>tes will worship Pa1rvati1 also after S4iva and the Vais2n2a-
<>vites Maha1laks2mi1 also after Vis2n2u In South India the
<>worshippers are S4aivites and Vais2n2avites.
<NI>4) {%S4aivites.%} In India a large number of people worship
<>S4iva. There is not much difference in the mode of wor-
<>ship of these various people. There are worships which
<>are in accordance with the Vedas and which are not.
<>Forms of worship which are Sa1ttvika (of goodness) and
<>which are not, could be seen. The S4aiva worship called
<>Paras4upada is very ancient. The phallus at Gud2imalla
<>is considered to be as old as B.C. 2nd century. From
<>this it could be inferred how old, the S4aivite worship is
<>in India.
<NI>5) {%The S4aivite images.%} Of the emblems denoting S4iva,
<>the most important is the Phallus, which is of two kinds,
<>movable and immovable. Risen by itself and or erected
<>and set firm and consecrated inside temples are immov-
<>able; that which is made of earth, metal, jewel, wood
<>or stone, is movable. There are Phalluses made for the
<>time being. They are fixed on platforms or pedestals.
<>They are of different sizes and shapes. The Phallus is
<>made of male stone and the pedestal of female stone.
<NI>6) {%The portions of S4iva.%} There are various kinds of images
<>meant to reveal various attributes of S4iva. Lin3godbhava
<>(originated from Phallus), Candras4ekhara, Raudra,
<>Uma1sam3hita (with Uma1) etc. are some of them. Ka1ma1-
<>ntaka (killing Ka1ma or Cupid), Gaja1ri (enemy of Gaja),
<>Ka1la1ri (Enemy of Yama) and Tripurada1haka (burn-
<>ing Tripura) are figures of extermination. S4iva has
<>the figures of blessing Can2d2es4a, Vis2n2u, Nandi1s4vara,
<>Vighnes4a and Arjuna also. There are a large number
<>of images in various dancing poses.
<NI>There are four other figures of Daks2in2a1mu1rti, such as the
<>explanation, knowledge, yoga (or meditation, contem-
<>plation and other ways of union with the Universal
<>Soul) and Vi1n2a1dhara (carrying a lute). A few other
<>forms are Bhiks2a1t2aka (begging alms), Kapa1ladha1ri1
<>(wearing skulls), Gan3ga1dhara (bearing the Gan3ges),
<>Ardhana1ri1s4vara (God half of whom is a woman),
<>Vr2s2abhava1hana (seated on a bull), Vis2abhaks2aka (eat-
<>ing poison) etc. The figures of Sada1s4iva, Mahes4a,
<>Eka1das4arudras (eleven Rudras), Vidyes4vara and Mu1rt-
<>yas2t2aka are seen occasionally here and there. There
<>are scriptures describing these figures.
<NI>7) {%The Devas (gods) connected with S4iva.%} Among the gods
<>connected with S4iva, first place is given to Gan2apati.
<>Worship of Gan2apati was in vogue from 6th or 7th
<>century B.C. Perhaps the images that we see today
<>were made after this period.
<NI>In South India temples dedicated to Gan2apati are not
<>scarce. The position of Gan2apati is at the entrance of
<>villages and fortresses, at the foot of banyan trees, at
<>the entrance of temples and at the south-west corner of
<>S4aivite temples.
<NI>There are images of Gan2apati in the postures of stand-
<>ing and dancing. Main images are those with the trunk [Page852-b+ 65]
<>turned to the right and to the left. The trunk is turned
<>to the right in some and to the left in others.
<NI>8) {%Subrahman2ya.%} Subrahman2ya is worshipped only in
<>South India. Subrahman2ya is known by various names
<>such as Kuma1ra, Muruka, Ka1rttikeya, Skanda, A1rumu-
<>kha, Guha, and so on. Temples dedicated to Subrah-
<>man2ya are seen in plenty in Tamil Nadu. They are all
<>situated on the tops of hills.
<NI>There is sufficient proof in the poetic works of the
<>San3gha period, to the fact that Subrahman2ya-worship
<>was prevalent in South India. On excavation at the
<>place Na1ga1rjunakon2d2a in the District of Guntur, images
<>of Subrahman2ya were found under the earth. They
<>were as old as 3rd century B.C.
<NI>Images of Subrahman2ya are seen in various postures
<>and shapes such as sitting, with six faces, with one face,
<>with two hands, with four hands, sometimes with wife,
<>in the state of having finished investiture with the
<>Brahma-string etc. There are figures sitting on the pea-
<>cock also.
<NI>9) {%Other images.%} Images of S4akti, Laks2mi1, Earth-goddess,
<>Sarasvati1, Saptama1tr2s (Seven Mothers), Jyes2t2ha1, Vis2n2u,
<>the ten incarnations, the planets such as the sun etc.
<>the deities of the weapon like the thunderbolt, S4akti
<>(lance), Nandikes4vara, Can2d2es4vara, S4a1sta1, Ks2etrapa1la,
<>Brahma1, Guardians of the zones, As4vini1devas, half gods,
<>etc. are also dedicated and worshipped in temples.

<HI>VIHUN2D2A. An asura. This asura was the son of the mighty

<NI>Vihun2d2a agreed to it. He went for the flowers. He
<>wandered over forests and mountains. Nobody had any

<>He told the hermit everything. Then S4ukra told him,
<>“Ka1moda is neither a tree nor a flower. She is goddess
<>who came up from the sea of Milk at the time of the
<>churning. She dwells at Gan3ga1dva1ra. When she laughs
<>pure white fragrant flowers fall from her lips, on the
<>water of Gan3ga1, every moment. When she is sad, the
<>flowers that fall from her lips will be red.”
<NI>Vihun2d2a started for Gan3ga1dva1ra. Na1rada knew this.
<>He came to Vihun2d2a and said, “It is difficult to reach
<>Gan3ga1dva1ra and please Ka1moda1, and obtain the flower.
<>So the easiest way is to gather the flowers that come
<>down through the water of Gan3ga1.” Vihun2d2a agreed.
<>He took his seat on the bank of Gan3ga1 and waited for
<>the flowers to come.
<NI>Na1rada decided to cause harm to Vihun2d2a. So he went
<>to Ka1moda1 and by way of telling her news, he inform-
<>ed her that the hermit Bhr2gu had cursed Maha1vis2n2u
<>and changed him into a man. On hearing this Ka1moda1
<>began to cry. Red flowers fell from her lips. They were
<>carried down in cluster by the water of Gan3ga1. Vihun2d2a
<>gathered them and went to the dwelling place of S4iva.
<>S4iva and Pa1rvati1 saw this by their inward eyes. Pa1rvati1
<>did not like the idea of offering red flowers at the feet
<>of her beloved husband. S4iva found out a remedy for
<>it. He advised Pa1rvati1 to offer pure white flowers at his
<>feet standing as a boy. Vihun2d2a came with red flowers
<>Pa1rvati1 appeared in the form of a boy. Both began to
<>vie with each other in offering flowers at the feet of
<>S4iva. Gradually it changed to a fight between the boy
<>and Vihun2d2a. In the fight Vihun2d2a was killed. (Padma

<HI>VIJAYADAS4AMI1. A festival of Indians. As this festival
<>is celebrated for nine nights from the 1st to the 9th in the
<>bright lunar fortnight of the month of Kanni (Sep-
<>tember-October) it is known as Navara1tri (Nine nights)
<>and as it continues up to Das4ami1 (the tenth night) it
<>is called Dasra. The Hindus believe that the Vijaya-
<>das4ami1 (the victorious tenth) was the day on which
<>Devi1 Durga1 killed Mahis2a1sura and got victory. So this
<>day is considered to be a suitable moment to begin the
<>learning of all the arts which would enable one to carry
<>on a successful life. Sarasvati1 is considered the trans-
<>figuration of Durga1. As people became less superstiti-
<>ous, they began to consider the story of killing Mahis2a1-
<>sura as a metaphorical saying, to mean the killing of
<>ignorance. So the day of victory of Devi1 Durga1 (the same
<>as Sarasvati1) is considered to be the auspicious moment
<>for the beginning of learning. The warrior places his
<>weapons, the man of literature his books and pen, the
<>musician his musical instruments, at the feet of Devi1 and
<>with devotion and worship receives them back from
<>Devi1 on the Vijayadas4ami1 day at an auspicious
<>moment.
<NI>This is a festival celebrated everywhere in India. In
<>several native states this festival used to be celebrated
<>under the sponsorship of the royal family as a State
<>festival. As it is believed that Mahis2a1sura had lived in
<>Mysore the celebration of Dasra in that State generally
<>is on a grand scale. In Kerala, from olden times this
<>festival was celebrated under the patronage of the Van5ci
<>Royal family.
<HI>VIJAYALAKS2MI1. One of the eight Laks2mi1s. The duty
