<HI>VR2TRA (VR2TRA1SURA). A mighty and fierce asura.
<NI>1) {%Reason for his birth.%} Vr2tra was the rebirth of emperor
<>Citraketu. Citraketu and his wife Kr2tadyuti prayed to
<>An3giras, as a result of which a son was born to them.
<>That son died in his infancy. But An3giras brought him
<>to life again. Brahma1 and Na1rada taught Citraketu
<>theosophy. Citraketu sat in contemplation for eight
<>days and changing himself to a Gandharva he flew
<>through the sky. As he was flying, he saw Pa1rvati1 sitting
<>on the thigh of S4iva and laughed aloud. Knowing this
<>Pa1rvati1 cursed him to become an asura. Vr2tra1sura was
<>the rebirth of the emperor according to this curse.
<>(Bha1gavata, Skandha 6).
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Two stories are mentioned about the birth of
<>Vr2tra1sura. One story is that Kas4yapa created him from
<>fire. The other story is that Vr2tra was the son of Tvas2t2a1.
<>Both are given below:
<NI>(i) Hiran2yakas4ipu was born to Praja1pati Kas4yapa, by
<>his wife Danu. Maha1vis2n2u killed him on being request-
<>ed by the Devas. Danu was grieved at the death of her
<>son. So Kas4yapa gave her another son. He was Vala or
<>Bala. Indra killed him with his weapon, the thunder-
<>bolt. Kas4yapa got angry and plucking a hair from his
<>matted hair, made a burnt offering of it, saying “Let a
<>son who would be the killer of Indra be born.” Imme-
<>diately a huge giant, as black as antimony with yellow
<>eyes was born from the fire. That asura, clad in the
<>hide of antelope with sword in hand, opening his mouth,
<>from which two huge tusks protruded, very wide, and
<>shining with radiance cried out in a voice of thunder,
<>“Oh! Sage! Order me, what am I to do?”
<NI>Kas4yapa ordered him to kill Indra. He named the
<>monster Vr2tra. (Padma Pura1n2a, Bhu1mi Khan2d2a, Chap-
<>ter 23).
<NI>(ii) It was Praja1pati Tvas2t2a1 who created Vr2tra to kill
<>Indra. He had sufficient reasons for it. From the very
<>beginning Indra and Tvas2t2a1 were enemies. Tvas2t2a1 be-
<>got a son named Tris4iras otherwise called Vis4varu1pa,
<>for the purpose of killing Indra. This Vis4varu1pa had
<>three heads. One was meant for drinking Sura1 (a
<>liquor), the second for drinking Soma (liquor) and the
<>third for eating food. Vis4varu1pa was a Bra1hman2a. Still,
<>as his mother was an asura, he loved the asuras and
<>mingled with them. Indra knew about the behaviour
<>of Vis4varu1pa. He concluded that it was blasphemy and
<>wickedness. Indra who was afraid of Vis4varu1pa, got
<>angry and cut off his heads. Of the heads of Vis4varu1pa,
<>that which drank Soma became a bird called Kapin5-
<>jala, that which drank Sura1 became a bird called
<>Kalapin3ga, and that which ate food became the
<>bird Tittiri (partridge). Brahmahatya1 (the sin of kill-
<>ing Brahmin) took shape and went against Indra.
<>Though Indra could have destroyed it, he joined his [Page884-a+ 65]
<>hands and received it. At the end of the year he cut it
<>into four pieces and divided them among earth, water,
<>tree and woman. The earth received it with the boon
<>that depressions will be filled. That sin is now seen as
<>salt beds. Water got it with the boon, “Will be swollen
<>when joined”. That sin is foam and bubbles. The tree
<>got it with the boon, “Will not die even if cut into
<>pieces”. That sin is the sap of the tree. Women got it with
<>the boon, “Amour will last without break”. That sin is
<>the menstruation of women.
<NI>When Tvas2t2a1 heard that his righteous son was killed
<>by Indra unreasonably, he became angry and began to
<>make burnt offerings with spells and incantations of
<>Atharvaveda. This offering continued for eight days.
<>On the eighth day at night, an extremely bright male
<>person arose from the fire pit. He rose higher and higher
<>as the flame of fire. Then that figure of power asked
<>Tvas2t2a1. “Father! what is my name? What have I to
<>do for you? What is the reason for your grief?” He
<>said that he was prepared to drink up the ocean dry or
<>smash the mountains, or prevent the sun and the moon
<>from moving or any such thing for the sake of his father.
<>The father ordered him to kill Indra. From that day
<>onwards Vr2tra got ready to kill Indra. (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 6).
<NI>3) {%The slaughter of Vr2tra1sura.%} Hearing about the pro-
<>wess, strength, and the martial radiance of Vr2tra1sura,
<>Indra grew afraid of him and began to think about
<>means and ways to kill him. Indra called the Saptars2is
<>(seven hermits) and sent them to Vr2tra. His intention
<>was to make a treaty. The seven hermits approached
<>Vr2tra and requested him to make a treaty with Indra,
<>and told him that Indra was prepared to give half of
<>the position of Indra.
<NI>Vr2tra:--Look hermits! If Indra honestly desires to be
<>in peace with me, I have no objection. But what is the
<>surety that Indra will not deceive me?
<NI>Hermits:--If Indra proves to be false and deliberately
<>deceives you, he has agreed to bear the sin of Brahma-
<>hatya1 incurred, by himself.
<NI>Vr2tra agreed to make a treaty on this condition. The
<>hermits took Vr2tra to the palace of Indra. Seeing Vr2tra1-
<>sura who was coming to be friends with him Indra rose
<>from his royal chair and offered half of it to Vr2tra.
<>Both embraced each other and vowed that they would
<>be brothers born from the same womb.
<NI>Thus having engaged Vr2tra in a treaty deceitfully,
<>Indra waited for an opportunity to kill Vr2tra. Once
<>Indra sent Rambha1 to infatuate Vr2tra. “Look, beauti-
<>ful girl! Make Vr2tra senseless somehow so that I may
<>kill him.” Hearing this, with a beautiful laugh, Rambha1
<>went with her maids to the Park Nandana and waited
<>for Vr2tra1sura. At this time Vr2tra, with some Da1nava
<>friends, came to the park Nandana for entertainment.
<>Indra walked beside watching for the opportunity to
<>kill him. Without fearing any danger from Indra, Vr2tra
<>walked through the park, seeing the celestial maids
<>singing and dancing and playing various games in the
<>garden, and they reached a corner where Rambha1 had
<>been singing sweet songs and swinging with her maids.
<>This sight made Vr2tra amorous. He approached
<>Rambha1 and prayed to her to become his wife. Under
<>that sandalwood tree Rambha1 asked him. “Oh hand-
<>some youth! I am Rambha1. I came here with my
<>maids to play. Who are you, my Lord?” [Page884-b+ 64]
<>Vr2tra: “Beautiful maid! I am the son of Kas4yapa
<>and the friend of Indra. I enjoy half the position of
<>Indra. I, Vr2tra, have brought the three worlds under
<>my control. I shall be complete in all aspects, if I
<>could marry you.”
<NI>Rambha1 agreed to the marriage on condition that
<>Vr2tra would not gainsay her in anything. They engaged
<>in amorous plays and Rambha1 made the Brahmin
<>drink liquor in large quantities and he became uncons-
<>cious. Indra took this opportunity and sent his thunder-
<>bolt which took away the life of Vr2tra. Instantly Indra
<>became affected with sins such as Brahmahatya1 etc.
<>(Padma Pura1n2a, Bhu1mikhan2d2a, Chapters 23 and 24).
<NI>4) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(i) Indra washed away the sin of Brahmahatya1 incur-
<>red by killing Vr2tra, at the place called Karu1s2a. (See
<>under An3gamalada).
<NI>(ii) It was at the time of the killing of Vr2tra by Indra,
<>that Jat2a1yu and Sampa1ti made bets and flew up to the
<>region of the Sun. (Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Kis2kindha1
<>Ka1n2d2a, Sarga 58, Stanza 4).
<NI>(iii) Mention about the conflict between Indra and
<>Vr2tra1sura occurs in various places in the R2gveda.
<>R2gveda, Man2d2ala 1, Anuva1ka 16, Su1kta 80 refers to
<>the battle between Indra and Vr2tra. In many other
<>Su1ktas songs about Indra and Vr2tra occur.
<NI>(iv) Mention is made in Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva,
<>Chapter 65, Stanza 33, that Vr2tra was the son born to
<>Praja1pati Kas4yapa by his wife Danu.
<NI>(v) It was Vr2tra1sura who took rebirth later as King
<>Man2ima1n. (M.B. A1di Parva. Chapter 67, Stanza 44).
<NI>(vi) The thunderbolt of Indra hit on the head of
<>Vr2tra1sura and was broken into ten big pieces and
<>hundred small pieces. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 169,
<>Stanza 50).
<NI>(vii) It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, Udyoga Parva,
<>Chapter 9, Stanza 48, that Vr2tra1sura was born from
<>the fire of Sorcery and witchcraft of Tvas2t2a1.
<NI>(viii) In the fight between Indra and Vr2tra, Indra was
<>swallowed by Vr2tra. But Indra contracted the members
<>of his body and became smaller and smaller and came
<>out of Vr2tra's belly. (M.B. Udyoga Parva, Chapter 9,
<>Stanza 52).
<NI>(ix) Once the teacher-priest S4ukra asked Vr2tra certain
<>questions to all of which Vr2tra gave proper answers.
<>(M.B. S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 279, Stanza 13).
<NI>(x) Vr2tra entered heaven after his death. (S4a1nti Parva,
<>Chapter 180, Stanza 57).
<NI>(xi) Everybody was terrified because the fight of
<>Indra with Vr2tra prolonged without any termination.
<>At last Vr2tra gaped and taking this opportunity, Indra
<>sent his weapon the thunderbolt into the mouth of
<>Vr2tra. Thus Vr2tra met with death. (M.B. Vana Parva,
<>Chapter 101, Stanza 15; Udyoga Parva, Chapter 10,
<>Stanza 30; S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 232, Stanza 9; S4a1nti
<>Parva, Chapter 283, Stanza 59).
<NI>(xii) In Maha1bha1rata, the words, Asura, Asuras4res2t2ha,
<>Asurendra, Daitya, Daityapati, Daityendra, Da1nava,
<>Da1navendra, Ditija, Sura1ri, Tva1s2t2ar, Vis4va1tma1 etc. are
<>used as synonyms of Vr2tra.
<HI>VR2TTA. A na1ga (serpent) born to Praja1pati Kas4yapa
<>by his wife Kadru1. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 35,
<>Stanza 10).
<HI>VYA1DHI. Vya1dhi, Jara1, S4oka1, Tr2s2n2a1 and Krodha1, were [Page885-a+ 65]
<>the daughters of Mr2tyu (Death). (Agni Pura1n2a, Chap-
<>ter 20).
<HI>VYA1GHRABHAT2A. The minister of King S4ri1datta,
<>described in Katha1saritsa1gara. Besides Vya1ghrabhat2a,
<>he had three more ministers named Maha1bala, Upendra-
<>bala and Nis2t2hu1raka.
<HI>VYA1GHRADATTA I. A King who fought on the side
<>of the Pa1n2d2avas. This King who was a great fighter on
<>the chariot, fought with the teacher Dron2a. It is men-
<>tioned in Maha1bha1rata, Dron2a Parva, Chapter 16,
<>Stanza 32, that he was killed by Dron2a.
<HI>VYA1GHRADATTA II. A prince of the country of
<>Magadha. He took the side of the Kauravas and was
<>killed in the battle of Bha1rata by Sa1tyaki. (M.B. Dron2a
<>Parva, Chapter 107, Stanza 31).
<HI>VYA1GHRAKETU. A warrior of Pa1n5ca1la, who fought
<>on the side of the Pa1n2d2avas against the Kauravas.
<>Karn2a wounded him in the battle of Bha1rata. (M.B.
<>Karn2a Parva, Chapter 56, Stanza 44).
<HI>VYA1GHRA1KS2A. A follower of Subrahman2ya. (Maha1-
<>bha1rata, S4alya Parva, Chapter 45, Stanza 59).
<HI>VYA1GHRAPA1DA. An ancient hermit. He was the father
<>of Upamanyu. (M.B. Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 14,
<>Stanza 45).
<HI>VYA1LI1MUKHA. An asura who fought against Subrah-
<>man2ya. In the fight with Subrahman2ya this asura was
<>killed. (Skanda Pura1n2a, A1sura Ka1n2d2a)
<HI>VYA1SA. The sage Vya1sa who is the author of the Maha1-
<>Bha1rata.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u in the following
<>order: Brahma1-Vasis2t2ha-S4akti-Para1s4ara-Vya1sa.
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Vya1sa was born to hermit Para1s4ara by a fisher-
<>woman named Ka1li1. His name when he was a child
<>was Kr2s2n2a. As his birth took place in an island (Dvi1pa)
<>he got the name Kr2s2n2advaipa1yana. After dividing the
<>Vedas he got the name Vedavya1sa. He is the composer
<>of Maha1bha1rata, one of the greatest books in world-
<>literature. The births of great men, generally will be
<>wonderful. Behind the birth of Vya1sa also there is a
<>wonderful story.
<NI>As has already been mentioned, Ka1li1, a fisherwoman
<>was the mother of Vya1sa. There is a story about this
<>Ka1li1 also. When king Vasu of Cedi went to the forest
<>for hunting, he saw the coition of animals and he had
<>seminal discharge. The king sent that semen to his
<>queen. But on the way it fell in the river Ka1lindi1 and
<>was eaten by a fish. This fish was a celestial maid
<>named Adrika1 transformed to fish by a curse. The fish
<>conceived and got into the net of a fisherman, who lived
<>on the banks of Ka1lindi1. When this fish was cut open
<>a male and a female infant were seen inside. The male
<>child was given to the king himself. The fisherman
<>brought up the girl naming her Ka1li1. As the girl had
<>the gandha (smell) of matsya (fish), she got the name
<>‘Matsya-gandha1’, also. This fisherman was also a ferry-
<>man. Ka1li1 used to help her father in ferrying people
<>across the river Ka1lindi1. She grew up and became a
<>young woman.
<NI>Once the hermit Parasara came by that way to go to
<>the other side of the river. At that time, the fisherman
<>who has been taking people across the river, was sitting
<>on the bank of the river and having his meals. As soon
<>as Para1s4ara came, the innocent fisherman--the foster-
<>father of Matsyagandha1--called her, who was standing
<>close by and asked her to take the hermit across the [Page885-b+ 65]
<>river. The hermit got into the boat. Matsyagandha1
<>began to row the boat. The beauty of the damsel sitting
<>in front of him and the little waves of the river, com-
<>bined together had the effect of arousing passion in the
<>hermit. He became sexually excited and sat close to her.
<>Discerning his intention she moved away from him and
<>prayed to him humbly not to violate her chastity. She
<>repeated her prayer. The hermit Para1s4ara created an
<>artificial fog around the boat. The smell of fish was
<>gone from her and the fragrance of Musk took its place.
<>The hermit created an artificial island in the middle of
<>the river. They got down on the island and acted a love
<>drama. She became pregnant. Para1s4ara said to her.
<>“Beautiful girl! Even after your confinement you shall
<>remain a virgin. A son, who will be a portion of Vis2n2u,
<>a man of purity, famous throughout the three worlds,
<>highly learned, the teacher-priest of the whole world,
<>shall be born to you. He will divide the Vedas and will
<>be exalted by the people of the world.”
<NI>After this the great hermit took his bath in Yamuna1 and
<>went away. The pregnancy of Ka1li1 was completed in-
<>stantly and she gave birth to a very handsome boy in
<>that island of Yamuna1. As soon as he was born, he grew
<>up and became a hermit radiant with devotion and as-
<>suming a vow of purity and abstinence he said to his
<>mother. “Mother! You can go anywhere, as you please.
<>You need have no worry on my account. I am about to
<>go for penance. When anything unpleasant happens to
<>you, just think of me. The moment you wish to see me,
<>I will be there by you. I wish you a happy life. I am
<>going.” Saying thus the brave boy walked away. (Devi1
<>Bha1gavata, Skandha 2; Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva,
<>Chapters 60 to 63).
<NI>3) {%Spiritual life.%}
<NI>{%i) Introduction.%} Two sides, the spiritual as well as the
<>material, are seen in the life of Vya1sa. It was after the
<>marriage of S4antanu, a king of the Lunar dynasty, with
<>his mother Ka1li1, otherwise known as Satyavati1, that
<>Vya1sa came into contact with Hastina1pura. Participating
<>in all the vicissitudes of the Pa1n2d2avas and the Kauravas
<>was the worldly side of the life of Vya1sa. But the major
<>part of his life was spent in living as a hermit in his
<>hermitage in the forest with a large group of disciples;
<>teaching them the Vedas. A small description of that
<>spiritual life is given below:
<NI>ii) {%In the forest.%} We do not see Kr2s2n2advaipa1yana, who
<>had grown up to a youth at the time of his birth, for
<>many years after his departure, bidding adieu to his
<>mother. He might have been living with hermits in the
<>forests, learning Vedas from them. After this he appears
<>on the banks of river Sarasvati1 as a teacher and Priest.
<>As he was doing penance there, he saw two sparrows,
<>legs and beaks red, without even down feathers, crying
<>for food, and the parent birds, with the utmost care and
<>tenderness feeding them. They flew about here and there
<>and gathered food and came back quickly. Because of
<>joy at the sight of their parents, the little ones opened
<>their ruby-red mouths with cries and throbbing. They
<>kissed the young ones and fed them. The young spar-
<>rows hid under the wings of their father and mother
<>and enjoyed the surroundings by thrusting out their
<>heads and looking on all sides.
<NI>{%iii) Birth of son.%} Seeing this, the paternity instinct in
<>him was aroused. He understood that love of children
<>was merely for the sake of love, that this love was pure [Page886-a+ 65]
<>and simple. Moreover there is the maxim that a man
<>without a son has no right to aspire for heaven. Sad
<>and silent, thinking of these things he walked on unwill-
<>ingly and reached the vicinity of the Hima1layas. Still,
<>he was doubtful. He began to consider about the deity,
<>before whom he had to do penance for the fulfilment of
<>his wish. He could not decide. As he was sitting in
<>thought, Na1rada came there. From the talk of Vya1sa,
<>Na1rada knew that childlessness was the cause of his
<>sorrow. Na1rada advised him that for the attainment of
<>Purus2a1rthas (objects of life) penance was to be done
<>before Devi1. Accepting that advice, Vya1sa went to a
<>place near Maha1meru to do penance.
<NI>When Vya1sa began penance, the celestial maids also
<>commenced their work of hindering the penance. It was
<>Ghr2ta1ci1 who confronted Vya1sa. She took the form of a
<>parrot of five colours and flew in front of Vya1sa. The
<>hermit was excited at the beauty of Ghr2ta1ci1 and sat
<>forgetting himself. As he sat there thinking of the infat-
<>uating beauty of the parrot, seminal discharge occurred
<>to him. He became a slave to this infatuation, when he
<>was engaged in making fire by attrition. In this
<>amorous state of mind he was quite unaware of the
<>seminal discharge or its falling on the pieces of wood
<>used for attrition. He continued attrition. Then a very
<>bright, divine person appeared from the pieces of wood.
<>At the birth of a person, without attachment to a womb,
<>all the worlds were delighted. The hide of black ante-
<>lope, water pot, hermit's rod etc. fell from the sky.
<>Birth rituals and ceremonies, according to the custom,
<>were conducted by Vya1sa. As he was born from the
<>semen discharged at the sight of the S4uka (parrot) the
<>infant was named S4uka. As soon as he was born S4uka
<>began to grow by divine power and shortly became a
<>boy of shining radiance. After investiture with the
<>Brahma-string, the boy was sent for education to the
<>hermitage of Br2haspati, the teacher of the devas. S4uka
<>completed his education with Br2haspati and having
<>performed Sama1varta and offering of gift to the teacher,
<>he returned home to his father.
<NI>{%iv) Disciples.%} S4uka commenced advanced study under
<>his father Vya1sa. Besides S4uka, Vya1sa had disciples such
<>as Vais4ampa1yana, Su1ta, Paila, Jaimini and others also,
<>living with him. The hermitage of Vya1sa soon grew up
<>to be a great educational institution, with plenty of dis-
<>ciples.
<NI>{%v) Separation of son.%} In the meanwhile S4uka married and
<>lived the life of a householder in the hermitage of his
<>father, for a time. Then forsaking his family and his
<>father, S4uka went to the peak of Kaila1sa and began to
<>do penance meditating on S4iva. At last he became a
<>divine person who had obtained complete attainments,
<>and breaking the top of the peak open, he rose up into
<>the sky and shone there as a second Sun. The devas who
<>saw S4uka rising up by breaking the peak of Kaila1sa and
<>staying up in the sky, praised him.
<NI>This untimely separation of his son had undermined the
<>firmness of the mind of Vya1sa. Filled with grief, he left
<>his hermitage and wandered here and there calling out
<>his son by name. He could not find his son. At last he
<>reached the peak of Kaila1sa where his son had been
<>doing penance. Standing there he called aloud his son
<>by name cryingly. Paramas4iva appeared before the
<>lamenting father and consoled him. Thus getting a little
<>bit of peace of mind, Vya1sa returned to his hermitage [Page886-b+ 64]
<>and lived there. The sorrowing Vya1sa, was made still
<>more sorrowful by the departure of his beloved disciples,
<>Asita, Devala, Vais4ampa1yana, Sumantu, Jaimini and
<>others who had been living in the hermitage and who
<>had departed, having finished their education. All the
<>surroundings of the hermitage seemed to him filled
<>with pain. At last he thought about his mother.
<>(Devi1 Bha1gavata Skandha 1).
<NI>4) {%His terrestrial life.%}
<NI>{%i) Preface.%} Within this period many changes had
<>taken place in Hastina1pura and the bank of Yamuna1.
<>S4antanu the king of the Lunar dynasty had married
<>Gan3ga1devi1, who had disappeared after giving the king
<>a son named Devavrata (Bhi1s2ma). Bhi1s2ma grew up.
<>Once S4antanu was hunting in the forest when he was
<>attracted by the sweet smell of musk. Tracing the
<>origin of that smell, the king reached the fisherman's
<>hut on the banks of the Yamuna1. That smell proceeded
<>from Kastu1rigandha1 (Satyavati1) the mother of Vya1sa.
<>The king fell in love with her. He returned to the
<>palace, sad and silent. Learning the cause of his father's
<>sadness, Devavrata went to the fisherman's hut and took
<>S4atyavati1 to the palace to be given to his father. Deva-
<>vrata had taken a vow that the kingdom would be given
<>to the son born to Satyavati1 and that he would remain
<>unmarried, throughout his life. Because he had taken
<>so terrible a vow, Devavrata came to be called Bhi1s2ma
<>from that day onwards.
<NI>Two sons named Citra1n3gada and Vicitravi1rya, were
<>born to S4antanu. Citra1n3gada died when he was young.
<>Vicitravi1rya married Ambika1 and Amba1lika1, daughters
<>of the King of Ka1s4i1. Vicitravi1rya also died before any
<>children were born to him. It seemed as if the family
<>was about to become extinct. At this juncture Satyavati1
<>thought about her son Vya1sa.
<NI>ii) {%Vya1sa in Hastina1puri1.%} The mother thought about
<>him, and instantly he reached Hastina1puri1. Because of
<>her compulsion, two sons were born, one each to Ambi-
<>ka1 and Amba1lika1 from Vya1sa. The son of Ambika1 was
<>Dhr2tara1s2t2ra and the son of Amba1lika1 was Pa1n2d2u. Vid-
<>ura was the son born to Vya1sa by their maid.
<NI>iii) {%Vya1sa and the Kaurava-Pa1n2d2avas.%} From this time
<>onwards we see Vya1sa as the spiritual teacher of the
<>Kauravas and the Pa1n2d2avas. Behind all the movements
<>of these two families we could see the hand of Vya1sa.
<>Thus though he came to Hastina1puri1 and gave advice
<>to the members of the family frequently, his main abode
<>was his hermitage. Vya1sa's contact with Hastina1pura
<>could be seen up to the Maha1prastha1na (the great
<>departure) of the Pa1n2d2avas. In all the administrative
<>affairs up to this period, Vya1sa also had a part. The
<>situations in which Vya1sa had taken part in the life-
<>voyage of the Kauravas and the Pa1n2d2avas are given
<>below.
<NI>(i) Vya1sa gave the boon that hundred sons would be
<>born to Ga1ndha1ri1. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 114,
<>Stanza 8).
<NI>(ii) Vya1sa cut the mass of flesh given birth to by
<>Ga1ndha1ri1 into a hundred pieces and kept them in
<>hundred pots. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 114, Stanza
<>17).
<NI>(iii) Vya1sa consoled Ga1ndha1ri1 by telling her that over
<>and above hundred sons a daughter also would be born
<>to her. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 115, Stanza 16). [Page887-a+ 64]
<NI>(iv) Vya1sa consoled the Pa1n2d2avas who had been
<>living in the forest with their mother Kunti1, after the
<>death of Pa1n2d2u their father. (M.B. A1di Parva,
<>Chapter 155, Verse 5).
<NI>(v) On another occasion Vya1sa came to the Pa1n2d2avas
<>and told them the stories of the previous births of
<>Pa1n5ca1li1. (A1di Parva, Chapter 168).
<NI>(vi) Vya1sa rendered all possible help to the Pa1n2d2avas
<>to marry Pa1n5ca1li1. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 195).
<NI>(vii) Very often Vya1sa was a member of the council
<>of Dharmaputra. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 4,
<>Stanza 11).
<NI>(viii) It was Vya1sa who sent Arjuna to the north,
<>Bhi1masena to the east, Sahadeva to the south and
<>Nakula to the west for regional conquest. (M.B. Sabha1
<>Parva, Da1ks2in2a1tyapa1t2ha, Chapter 26).
<NI>(ix) Vya1sa engaged himself in making various arrange-
<>ments in the Ra1jasu1ya (sacrifice of royal consecration)
<>of Yudhis2t2hira. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 33,
<>Stanza 34).
<NI>(x) At the end of the Ra1jasu1ya, Vya1sa predicted the
<>future of Yudhis2t2hira. (Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 46,
<>Stanza 1).
<NI>(xi) When the Ra1jasu1ya ended, Vya1sa anointed
<>Yudhis2t2hira. (Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 53, Stanza 10).
<NI>(xii) Vya1sa advised Dhr2tara1s2t2ra to prevent Duryodhana
<>from doing injustice. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapters 7
<>and 8).
<NI>(xiii) When the Pa1n2d2avas were living in the Dvaita-
<>vana (forest) Vya1sa visited them and taught Yudhi-
<>s2t2hira the art of Pratismr2ti. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter
<>36, Stanza 24).
<NI>(xiv) He sent San5jaya to Dhr2tara1s2t2ra to tell him about
<>the greatness of Arjuna and S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. (M.B. Udyoga
<>Parva, Chapter 69, Stanza 11).
<NI>(xv) He gave San5jaya the power of having the eye of a
<>seer penetrating beyond time and space (Divya dr2s2t2i).
<>(M.B. Bhi1s2ma Parva, Chapter 2, Stanza 10).
<NI>(xvi) Vya1sa consoled Yudhis2t2hira who was stricken
<>with grief in the course of the battle of Bha1rata. (M.B.
<>Dron2a Parva, Chapter 71, Stanza 23).
<NI>(xvii) When Yudhis2t2hira cried over the death of
<>Ghat2otkaca in the battle of Bha1rata, Vya1sa came to
<>Yudhis2t2hira and consoled him. (M.B. Dron2a Parva,
<>Chapter 183, Stanza 58).
<NI>(xviii) He talked to As4vattha1ma1 about the greatness of
<>S4iva and S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. (M.B. Dron2a Parva, Chapter 201,
<>Stanza 56).
<NI>(xix) When Sa1tyaki was about to kill San5jaya, Vya1sa
<>turned him back from the attempt and rescued San5jaya.
<>(M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 29, Stanza 39).
<NI>(xx) Vya1sa argued and established that the act of
<>cursing As4vattha1ma1 on the part of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a was
<>correct. (M.B. Sauptika Parva, Chapter 16, Stanza 17).
<NI>(xxi) Vya1sa prevented Ga1ndha1ri1 from her intention to
<>curse the Pa1n2d2avas. (M.B. Stri1 Parva, Chapter 14,
<>Stanza 7).
<NI>(xxii) When the battle of Bha1rata was over, Vya1sa
<>advised Yudhis2t2hira about matters regarding the
<>administration of the country.
<NI>(xxiii) Yudhis2t2hira felt grieved at the death of relatives
<>and friends in the battle of Bha1rata and he decided to
<>commit suicide. But Vya1sa dissuaded him from that
<>attempt. (M.B. S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 27, Stanza 28). [Page887-b+ 65]
<NI>(xxiv) Vya1sa walked to the place where Bhi1s2ma lay on
<>the bed of arrows and visited him. (M.B. S4a1nti Parva,
<>Chapter 45, Stanza 5).
<NI>(xxv) Vya1sa advised Yudhis2t2hira to perform As4va-
<>medha (horse sacrifice). (M.B. As4vamedha Parva,
<>Chapter 3, Stanza 8).
<NI>(xxvi) Vya1sa advised the Pa1n2d2avas to go to King
<>Marutta for wealth when the battle of Bha1rata was
<>over. (As4vamedhika Parva, Chapter 3, Stanza 20).
<NI>(xxvii) Vya1sa consoled Uttara1, who was lamenting
<>over the death of her husband. (As4vamedha Parva,
<>Chapter 62, Stanza 11).
<NI>(xxviii) He consoled Arjuna who was crying over the
<>death of his son. (As4vamedha Parva, Chapter 62,
<>Stanza 14).
<NI>(xxix) Vya1sa advised Yudhis2t2hira on the various
<>arrangements which were to be made for the conducting
<>of horse-sacrifice. (As4vamedhika Parva, Chapters 62 to
<>72).
<NI>(xxx) Vya1sa went to Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, who had gone to the
<>forest after the Bha1rata-battle and pacified him. (M.B.
<>A1s4ramava1sika Parva, Chapter 28).
<NI>(xxxi) Vya1sa brought the spirits of those who died in
<>the Bha1rata-battle, to the surface of the river Ganges,
<>by the power of his penance and Dhr2tara1s2t2ra and the
<>others saw them. (See under Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, Para 7).
<NI>(xxxii) At the instruction of Vya1sa, all the Ks2atriya
<>widows immersed themselves in the river Ganges and
<>everyone of them entered the world of her husband.
<>(M.B. A1s4ramava1sika Parva, Chapter 33, Stanza 18).
<NI>(xxxiii) When the Yadu-clan was completely destroyed,
<>Arjuna went to the hermitage of Vya1sa and talked with
<>him. (Mausala Parva, Chapter 8).
<NI>(xxxiv) Vya1sa had been an adviser of King Janame-
<>jaya. (See under Janamejaya).
<NI>5) {%Saving a worm.%} Once a wicked man took rebirth as
<>a worm. This worm was crawling in haste for life in
<>front of a cart coming at great speed. He saved the
<>worm and gave it Brahminhood, and in the next birth
<>it became a Brahmin who lived in peace and comfort.
<>(M.B. Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 117).
<NI>6) {%The literary life of Vya1sa.%} Towards the close of his
<>life Vya1sa again entered the caves of Hima1layas. Vya1sa
<>who had steered through a very wide and rough sea of
<>life, was in a position to understand clearly the
<>various sides of human life. In the mind of that sage,
<>who sat in deep contemplation in the eternally silent
<>caves of the Hima1layas, the events of his past life began
<>to line up one after the other. From that inward insti-
<>gation the Pura1n2etiha1sas (the Myths and legends) took
<>form. It might have been during this period that Vya1sa
<>divided the Vedas and composed Pura1n2as and Upa-
<>pura1n2as.
<NI>One does not go wrong in saying that it was the com-
<>posing of the Maha1bha1rata that brought Vya1sa very
<>close to the later generations. The stories of the Kaura-
<>vas and the Pa1n2d2avas, flowed through his mind as a
<>river flows down crushing down the banks on either
<>side. A scribe was necessary to take them down in the
<>form of verses. Vya1sa informed Brahma1 of this need.
<>Brahma1 replied “Gan2apati is the only person capable
<>of taking down every thing that you sing.” Accordingly
<>Vya1sa thought of Gan2apati, who came to the side of
<>Vya1sa, and he informed Gan2apati of his need. Gan2apati
<>said that he was willing to do the work on condition [Page888-a+ 64]
<>that Vya1sa would go on singing unceasingly, so that he
<>might not have to stop the iron pen. Vya1sa said that
<>while he would be singing the poems without stopping,
<>Gan2apati should not take down this and that without
<>grasping the meaning. Both agreed to this condition
<>and the composing of the Maha1bha1rata commenced.
<>Within two years and a half the great poetic work was
<>finished. The great disciples of Vya1sa, such as Vais4am-
<>pa1yana, Jaimini and such others sang them and learned
<>them by heart and published them in the world. (M.B.
<>A1di Parva, Chapter 1).
<NI>7) {%Many Vya1sas.%} It is stated in the Pura1n2as that in
<>every Manu's age, a Vya1sa will be born. It is mention-
<>ed in Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 3, Chapter 3, as to who
<>were the persons who took birth as Vya1sa in a parti-
<>cular Manu's age and which were the Vedas and
<>branches of Vedas they had divided. It is given below:
<>During the age of Manu Vaivasvata, in each of the
<>past Dva1parayugas, the Veda had been divided by
<>great hermits, twentyeight times. Twentyeight Veda-
<>Vya1sas have passed, each of whom had divided the
<>Veda into four parts in each Dva1para Yuga. It was
<>Brahma1 himself who had divided the Veda into four in
<>the first Dva1parayuga. Praja1pati was the Vedavya1sa in
<>the second Dva1parayuga. In the third, Vya1sa was the
<>teacher-priest S4ukra; in the fourth Br2haspati; in the
<>fifth the Sun; and in the sixth the all powerful Dharma-
<>ra1ja. It was Indra in the seventh, Vasis2t2ha in the
<>eighth, Sa1rasvata in the ninth, and Tridha1ma1 in the
<>tenth. It was Tris4ikha in the eleventh, Bharadva1ja in
<>the twelfth, Antari1ks2a in the thirteenth, Varn2i1 in the
<>fourteenth, Trayya1run2a in the fifteenth, Dhanan5jaya in
<>the sixteenth, Kratun5jaya in the seventeenth and Jaya
<>in the eighteenth. Next Bharadva1ja comes as Vedavya1sa
<>and Gautama after Bharadva1ja. It was hermit Harya1-
<>tma1 who was the next Vya1sa, and then comes Va1jas4ra-
<>vas. The Next Vya1sa was Tr2n2abindu born in the clan
<>of Somas4us2ma. He was followed by R2ks2a. otherwise
<>called Va1lmi1ki born in the family of Bhr2gu. S4akti is
<>the Next Vya1sa. After that Para1s4ara, then Ja1tu1karn2a
<>and then Kr2s2n2advaipa1yana. They are the twentyeight
<>Vedavya1sas. Each one of these had divided the Veda
<>which had been one at the beginning of each Dva1para-
<>yuga, into four Vedas. It is As4vattha1ma1, the son of
<>Dron2a, who is going to be the Vedavya1s4a of the coming
<>Dva1parayuga.
<NI>8) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(i) Most of the scholars are of opinion that the period
<>of Vya1sa was between 1800 and 1500 B.C.
<NI>(ii) Apa1ntaratamas was the rebirth of Vya1sa. (See
<>under Apa1ntaratamas).
<NI>(iii) In Maha1bha1rata, the word Kr2s2n2a, Kr2s2n2advaipa1-
<>yana, Dvaipa1yana, Satyavati1suta, Satyavatya1tmaja,
<>Pa1ra1s4arya, Para1s4ara1tmaja, Ba1dara1yan2a, Vedavya1sa etc.
<>are used as synonyms of Vya1sa.
<NI>(iv) The word Vya1sa means he who describes elabora-
<>tely.
<NI>“He became Vya1sa because he had described all the
<>Vedas”. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapter 63, Stanza 88).
<>Vyas = describe (Differentiate the branches and divide).
<HI>VYA1SASTHALI1. An ancient holy place on the border of
<>Kuruks2etra. It is said that because of his grief due to
<>the separation of his son, Vya1sa tried to commit suicide
<>at this place. He who visits this place would get the [Page888-b+ 64]
<>fruits of giving thousand cows as alms. (M.B. Vana
<>Parva, Chapter 83, Stanza 93).
<HI>VYA1SAVANA. A forest near Kuruks2etra. It is men-
<>tioned in Maha1bha1rata, Vana Parva, Chapter 83,
<>Stanza 93, that he who bathes in the holy bath named
<>Manojavati1 ti1rtha in this forest, would get the fruits of
<>giving thousand cows as alms.
<HI>VYAS4VA. A King. It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, S4a1nti
<>Parva, Chapter 127, Stanza 3, that this King remains
<>in the palace of Yama.
<HI>VYAVAHA1RA. It is mentioned in Manusmr2ti, Chapter
<>8, that administering the law was of eighteen types in
<>ancient India.
<NI>i) Pertaining to giving and taking.
<NI>ii) Pertaining to the property entrusted to another
<>for keeping.
<NI>iii) Selling the property by anybody other than its
<>owner.
<NI>iv) Appropriating gain to oneself in a combined
<>transaction.
<NI>v) Not handing over the property which was given as
<>a gift.
<NI>vi) Non-payment of salary.
<NI>vii) Disobeying orders.
<NI>viii) Retaining and doubting the ownership after the
<>completion of a transaction of selling or buying.
<NI>ix) A law suit between the owner of the cattle and the
<>cowherd or shepherd.
<NI>x) Dispute about boundary.
<NI>xi) Striking another.
<NI>xii) Reviling others
<NI>xiii) Theft and robbery.
<NI>xiv) Violence.
<NI>xv) Stealing another's wife.
<NI>xvi) Matrimonial responsibilities.
<NI>xvii) Partition.
<NI>xviii) Gambling.
<NI>Whenever difference of opinion arises between two
<>persons on any of the matter given above, the King
<>should interfere and make a decision. For one reason
<>or another, if the King could not attend the court,
<>three learned Brahmins should enter the court and
<>conduct the trial sitting or standing, and they should
<>not conduct the trial walking to and fro. This was the
<>practice of courts in ancient India.

<HI>VYUS2ITA1S4VA. A King of the Pu1ru dynasty, who was

<>As the King led a libidinous life with this queen Bhadra1

<NI>When the King died, he had no children. Bhadra1 cried
<>for a long while embracing the dead body of the King.
<>Then the spirit of Vyus2ita1s4va, without the body, said
<>to her. “My love. Lie with me in your own bed on the
<>eighth or fourteenth day of your menstruation. I will
<>give you sons.” She acted according to the wish of the
<>King and got seven sons from the dead body. Her sons
<>were three Satvas and the four Madras. (M.B. A1di
<>Parva, Chapter 120).

<HI>YADU I. The founder of Ya1dava Vam3s4a or Yadu
<>Vam3s4a.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} From Vis2n2u were descended in the
<>following order:--Brahma1--Atri--Candra--Budha--
<>Puru1ravas--A1yus--Nahus2a--Yaya1ti--Yadu.
<NI>2) {%Yadu Vam3s4a (Ya1dava Vam3s4a).%} The origin of Yadu
<>Vam3s4a is from Atri. Candra, Durva1sas and Datta1treya
<>Muni were the sons of Atri by Anasu1ya1. Budha was
<>born to Candra, Puru1ravas was born to Budha, A1yus
<>was born to Puru1ravas, and Nahus2a was born to A1yus.
<>Two children, A1yati and Yaya1ti were born to Nahus2a.
<>Yaya1ti had two wives, S4armis2t2ha1 and Devaya1ni1. Three [Page889-b+ 65]
<>sons, Druhyu, Anudruhyu and Pu1ru were born to
<>S4armis2t2ha1. The Pu1ru vam3s4a takes its origin from Pu1ru.
<>Yaya1ti had two sons by Devaya1ni1. They were Yadu
<>and Turvasu. The descendants of Yadu are the
<>Ya1davas.
<NI>Four sons, Sahasrajit, Kros2t2a1, Nala and Ripu were
<>born to Yadu. Sahasrajit had a son, S4atajit. Three sons
<>Maha1bhaya, Ven2uhaya and Hehaya were born to
<>S4atajit. Of them Hehaya became famous under the
<>name of Ekavi1ra and founded the Hehaya vam3s4a. The
<>Hehayas and Bha1rgavas were on terms of enmity.
<>Dharma was born to Hehaya, and Kunti or Kun2i was
<>born to Dharma. Kun2i had four sons--Sada1jit, Ma1his2-
<>ma1n, Bhadrasena and Durdama. Dhanaka was the son
<>of Bhadrasena and Dhanaka had four sons--Kr2tavi1rya,
<>Kr2ta1gni, Kr2tavarma1 and Kr2taujas. Of them, Kr2tavi1rya
<>who was the eldest, was the father of Ka1rtavi1rya1rjuna.
<>From here, Yadu vam3s4a continues from Ka1rtavi1rya1rjuna.
<>He had five sons, Jayadhvaja, S4u1rasena, Vr2s2abha,
<>Madhu and U1rjjita. Vr2s2n2i was the son of Madhu, the
<>fourth of them. From this point, Yadu vam3s4a continues
<>from Vr2s2n2i. Ya1dava vam3s4a from this stage is also known
<>as Vr2s2n2i vam3s4a. Vr2s2n2i had four sons--Sumitra, Yudha1jit,
<>Vasu and Sa1rvabhauma. Yadu vam3s4a continues from
<>Yudha1jit. He had two sons, S4ini and Nimna. S4ini's son
<>was Satyaka and Satyaka's son was Sa1tyaki. Sa1tyaki
<>had another name, Yuyudha1na. Jaya was Sa1tyaki's son.
<>Kun2i was the son of Jaya, Anamitra was the son of
<>Kun2i and Pr2s4ni was the son of Anamitra. Pr2s4ni's sons
<>were Citraratha and S4vaphalka. Vid2u1ratha and Kuku1ra
<>were born to Citraratha. S4u1ra was the son of Vid2u1ratha.
<>S4ini was the son of S4u1ra, Bhoja was the son of S4ini,
<>Hr2di1ka was the son of Bhoja. Four sons, Devava1ha,
<>Gada1dhanva1, Kr2taparva1 and S4u1ra, were born to Hr2di1ka.
<>S4u1ra married Ma1ris2a1 and their children were Vasu,
<>Devabha1ga, Devas4ravas, A1naka. Sr2n5jaya, Ka1ka1ni1ka,
<>S4ya1maka, Vatsa, Kavu1ka and Vasudeva. Vasudeva
<>married Devaki1, the sister of Kam3sa. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a was
<>their son. Pradyumna was the son of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. Anirud-
<>dha was the son of Pradyumna and Vajra was the son
<>of Aniruddha. The last known link of that branch of
<>Yadu vam3s4a was Vajra.
<NI>Kam3sa also belonged to Yadu vam3s4a. Kuku1ra who was
<>the brother of Vid2u1ratha was the ancestor of Kam3sa.
<>Ugrasena who was a lineal descendant of Kuku1ra, was
<>the father of Kam3sa. The Pura1n2as mention Devaki1, the
<>mother of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a as Kam3sa's sister. But in fact she
<>was not his direct sister. Ugrasena, Kam3sa's father, had
<>a brother named Devaka. This Devaka had three sons,
<>Deva1pa, Upadeva and Sudeva. Devaki1, S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a's
<>mother was the daughter of Deva1pa. Devaki1 had six
<>sisters who were--S4rutadeva1, S4a1ntideva1, Upadeva1,
<>S4ri1deva1, Devaraks2ita1 and Sahadeva1.
<NI>Akru1ra who went to Amba1d2i also belonged to another
<>branch of Yadu vam3s4a. Pr2s4ni who was of the ninth
<>generation from Vr2s2n2i, had two sons--Citraratha and
<>S4vaphalka. Akru1ra was the son of S4vaphalka.
<NI>3) {%Other details about Yadu Vam3s4a%}
<NI>(i) Once Yaya1ti wanted his son Yadu to transfer the latter's
<>youthfulness to Yaya1ti. The son refused to oblige the
<>father who therefore pronounced a curse that Yadu's
<>descendants would not enjoy kingship. (For detailed
<>story, see under Yaya1ti).
<NI>(ii) Yadu's descendants are called Ya1davas. (M.B. A1di
<>Parva, Chapter 95, Verse 10). [Page890-a+ 65]
<NI>(iii) Yadu was the son of Devaya1ni1 and the grandson
<>of S4ukra1ca1rya. Although Yadu was a hero, he was dull-
<>witted. He did not obey his father. He used to despise
<>his father and brothers. His capital city was Hastina1-
<>pura. He became dull-witted after the loss of his king-
<>dom by the curse of his father. (M.B. Udyoga Parva,
<>Chapter 149).
<NI>4) {%The end of Yadu Vam3s4a.%} S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a was one of the most
<>important members of the Yadu Vam3s4a. That family
<>continued only for 36 years more after the end of the
<>Bha1rata Yuddha. At the end of the thirtysix years, evil
<>omens began to appear in Dva1raka1. Whirlwinds began
<>to blow furiously. Frightened birds began to fly helter-
<>skelter. Rivers began to flow upwards. The whole land
<>was enveloped in mist. Comets continued to drop
<>from heaven, scattering sparks of fire. The sun's disc was
<>eclipsed with clouds of dust.
<NI>At that time, one day, the sages Vis4va1mitra, Kan2va and
<>Na1rada happened to come to Dva1raka1. A band of
<>Ya1davas, under the leadership of Sa1mba, gathered round
<>the sages to make fun of them. They dressed Sa1mba like
<>a pregnant woman and asked the sages what child “she”
<>would bear. The enraged sages replied that the “Child”
<>would be an iron pestle which would be the cause of
<>the death of all the Ya1davas. Besides, they added that,
<>with the exception of Ra1ma, (Balabhadra Ra1ma)
<>and Kr2s2n2a, the whole of Ya1dava Vam3s4a would come
<>to ruin.
<NI>Next day Sa1mba did give birth to an iron pestle. The
<>frightened Ya1davas recalled the curse of the sages. They
<>reported the matter to their king. The king, in deep
<>gloom, had the pestle filed into dust and ordered his
<>men to throw the dust into the sea.
<NI>People recalled Ga1ndha1ri1's curse on S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a after the
<>Bha1rata Yuddha. The curse was that the family of S4ri1
<>Kr2s2n2a who was the cause of all mischief, would be wiped
<>out within a period of 36 years.
<NI>At that time, women began to be stolen from Dva1raka1
<>every night by a dark woman monster. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a's
<>weapon, Cakra slipped out of Kr2s2n2a's grip and rose up
<>to heaven, in the very presence of the Vr2s2n2is. Seeing
<>these evil omens, the alarmed Vr2s2n2is and Andhakas
<>began to leave the place on a pilgrimage. They reached
<>the shore of Prabha1sa ti1rtha. The Uddhavas parted
<>company with the Ya1davas there. Nobody prevented
<>the Uddhavas. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a who saw that the end of the
<>Ya1davas was approaching, also kept silent. The Ya1dava
<>leaders in despair, took to drinking. Intoxicated by
<>drinking they began to quarrel. The particles of the
<>iron pestle thrown into the sea, were carried and deposit-
<>ed on the shore by the waves. They began to sprout in-
<>to a kind of grass. The blades of this grass soon trans-
<>formed themselves into iron pestles. The Ya1davas pulled
<>them out and began attacking each other with them.
<>Most of them were beaten to death by their own kins-
<>folk. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a who was in a state of trance in
<>“Yoga1sana”, was hit on his toe by an arrow shot by a
<>hunter and he fell into Sama1dhi. Balabhadra Ra1ma
<>renounced his body and went to Pa1ta1la.
<NI>After that the sea advanced and submerged the whole
<>of Dva1raka1. (M.B. Mausala Parva).

<HI>YAJN5ADATTA I. A youthful sage. In Agni Pura1n2a.

<HI>YAJN5ADATTA II. A Bra1hman2a. There was a Bra1hman2a
<>village called Mahat at the foot of the Ya1muna mount-
<>ain, between the rivers Gan3ga1 and Yamuna1. This
<>Bra1hman2a was born and brought up here. There is a
<>story in Padma Pura1n2a, Chapter 92, about him, which

<>story is as follows:--
<NI>Once Yama (Ka1la) ordered his agent to bring the
<>Bra1hman2a named Yajn5adatta, who lived in Mahat village,
<>born in Vasis2t2ha gotra and was gentle, scholarly and
<>well-versed in Yajn5a practices. He was specially warned
<>that he should not make a mistake as there was another
<>Bra1hman2a with the same name who lived in the neigh-
<>bourhood and who resembled Yajn5adatta in appearance,
<>learning and in every other respect. But in spite of Yama's
<>specific warning, his agent brought the wrong person.
<NI>Yama, with his sense of justice, received him with due
<>respect and allowed him to return to the world. But
<>Yajn5adatta was not willing to return to the world.

<HI>YA1JN5AVALKYA. An ancient sage who was a profound
<>scholar.
<NI>1) {%General.%} Pura1n2as say that this sage spent the major
<>part of his life at the court of King Janaka. He was
<>also King Janaka's priest. In Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 16,
<>it is mentioned that Kalki was Ya1jn5avalkya's priest.
<>Maha1bha1rata, Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 4, Verse 51,
<>mentions that Ya1jn5avalkya was a “Brahmava1di1 son” of
<>Vis4va1mitra.
<NI>2) {%Thousand cows.%} Once King Janaka conducted a
<>Ya1ga. At that time he wished to find out the most
<>eminent among the sages. He promised to present a
<>thousand cows to the sage who was a consummate
<>scholar. A bag of gold coins was also tied to each horn
<>of the cows. Janaka announced that the sage who claim-
<>ed to be a consummate scholar might come forward and
<>take the cows with him. No one among the numerous
<>sages who were present, dared to come forward. Ya1jn5a-
<>valkya, however, ordered his disciple to take the cows
<>home with him. The other sages questioned his right to
<>claim the cows. Ya1jn5avalkya asserted his right to do so.
<>One of the sages named Arthabha1ga stood up and
<>asked him the following question:--
<NI>Arthabha1ga:--When a man who has not acquired
<>divine knowledge dies, his “Jn5a1nendriyas” (senses of
<>knowledge) are also merged with it. How is it possible
<>for him to accept another body afterwards? “Ya1jn5a-
<>valkya:--Karma (action) becomes the cause of his re-
<>birth.”
<NI>Next Kahola and many other sages put various ques-
<>tions to him. The substance of their talk is given
<>below:--
<NI>Kahola:--What have you to say about the invisible
<>and omnipresent Brahman?
<NI>Ya1jn5avalkya:--Your soul is the omni-present Brahman.
<>The soul has no hunger or thirst, fear or desire, old age
<>or death. Those who wish to know the soul, become
<>sages after transcending love of wife, children or world-
<>ly riches. The two qualities essential for sages are
<>scholarship and spiritual knowledge.
<NI>Ga1rgi1:--Who is the warp and woof of everything?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--It is Brahman. Do not ask further; if you do,
<>your head will be broken to pieces.
<NI>A1run2i:--Who is he who exists within? (Antarya1mi1).
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--He who is contained in Jagat (the universe)
<>without the knowledge of Jagat, he whose body is
<>Jagat, he who controls Jagat as antarya1mi1, he is within
<>every one. He cannot be seen, but he sees. He cannot [Page891-b+ 65]
<>be heard; but he hears. He is capable of thinking; but
<>he is beyond our thoughts. He exists as one who sees,
<>hears and thinks, in all creatures.
<NI>Ga1rgi1:--I have two questions to ask. They are as sharp
<>and pointed as two arrows. By what force is fixed that
<>which is above Heaven, below the earth and that
<>which is between them, like warp and woof?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--By the sky.
<NI>Ga1rgi1:--By what force is the sky held in position like
<>warp and woof?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--Under the command of God, the sun and
<>moon do not deviate from their orbits. The sky and the
<>earth remain in their respective places. Rivers run mak-
<>ing the fields fertile. One who lives and works without
<>the ability to know God does not obtain immortality.
<>He who dies without knowing God is a Kr2pan2a (spirit-
<>ual bankrupt). On the contrary, he who knows God
<>attains Moks2a.
<NI>S4a1kalya:--How many gods are there?
<NI>Ya1jn3a:--Thirtythree. They are--8 Vasus, 11 Rudras,
<>12 A1dityas, Indra and Praja1pati. The 8 Vasus are--
<>Agni, Pr2thvi1, Va1yu, Antari1ks2a, A1ditya, Dyau (Heaven),
<>Candra and Stars. The 11 Rudras are the combination
<>of the 10 jn5a1nendriyas and the working of the mind.
<>The ruling deities of the 12 months are known as the
<>12 A1dityas. They receive into them our lives and our
<>Karmans (actions).
<NI>Ya1jn5avalkya gave suitable answers to all other ques-
<>tions of S4a1kalya. At last, he asked S4a1kalya:--“Now I
<>will ask you a question. Whom do the Upanis2ads
<>describe? If you do not answer, your head will break.”
<>S4a1kalya could not answer it. He dropped down with a
<>broken head. He asked the other scholars who had
<>gathered there, if any of them wanted to ask him more
<>questions. But no one came forward. All remained
<>dumb. Then he continued:--“Fresh leaves sprout
<>from a tree which has been cut. If the seed is destroy-
<>ed, the tree does not grow out of it. How is it that a
<>man is born again after his death?”
<NI>No one was able to answer that question. Thus after
<>defeating all of them in argument, Ya1jn5avalkya went
<>home with the cows.
<NI>3) {%In Janaka's court again.%} On another occasion,
<>Ya1jn5avalkya went to see King Janaka. The King asked
<>him whether he had come for getting cows or to ask
<>abstruse question. The sage answered that he had come
<>for both. Their conversation continued as follows:--
<NI>Janaka:--Jita Muni has said that “Vacana” (word) is
<>Brahman or God. What is your opinion?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--It is correct. But did he say which is the body
<>and abode of God?
<NI>Janaka:--No.
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--In that case he has said only onefourth about
<>God. Vacana is god's body and sky, his abode. Brahma-
<>vidya1 depends on the word. The word is Parabrahman.
<>Janaka was highly pleased. He decided to give a thou-
<>sand bulls to the sage. But Ya1jn5avalkya said that his
<>father's principle was to give effective knowledge to the
<>pupil and not to receive any daks2in2a1 (Payment) from
<>him. Janaka again wanted Ya1jn5avalkya to explain
<>clearly what “Brahma Vidya1” means.
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--Just as a person can reach his destination by
<>walking or by travelling in a chariot or by sailing in a
<>boat, in order to secure peace for the soul, you are
<>depending on the Upanis2ads. But although one is [Page892-a+ 65]
<>learned in Vedas and A1gamas, or adored by others or
<>wealthy, or well-versed in Upanis2ads, unless one has
<>known God--unless one acquired Brahmajn5a1na--one
<>cannot be said to be gratified. Do you know where you
<>will go after renouncing your body?
<NI>Janaka:--I do not know.
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--I shall tell you. The deity of the right eye is
<>“Inda” (light). People call it “Indra”. The deity of
<>the left eye is Indra1n2i1, the wife of Indra. Indra is eater
<>and Indra1n2i1 is food. In a state of consciousness they
<>are separated. (Then the soul is called Vais4va1nara).
<>In a state of dream, the two are united. (Then the
<>soul is called Taijasa). In a state of slumber, the soul
<>is in “Prjn5a1a” state. (Since there is no activity it is
<>called Pra1jn5a). In the fourth or “Turi1ya” state, one
<>becomes capable of transcending the three other states
<>and to attain Parabrahman. It is a state of fearlessness
<>transcending birth and death.
<NI>The King who was pleased by this teaching dedicated
<>the kingdom of Videha and even himself to the sage.
<NI>4) {%In Janaka's court for the third time.%} After some years
<>Ya1jn5avalkya went again to Janaka's court. At that time
<>also there was a philosophical discussion between the
<>King and the sage in the form of questions and answers.
<NI>Janaka:--Which is the light that gives us guidance
<>during the day time?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--The Sun.
<NI>Janaka:--After sunset which is the light that guides us?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--The Moon.
<NI>Janaka:--And in the absence of the sun and moon?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--Agni.
<NI>Janaka:--Which is the light that guides us in the
<>absence of the sun, moon and Agni?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--Vacana.
<NI>Janaka:--Which is the light that shines in the absence
<>of all these luminaries?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--A1tmajyotis (soul's light).
<NI>Janaka:--Who is A1tman?
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--“Purus2a” who is formed by the union of
<>“vya1na” in pra1n2as (lives) and jyotis (light) in the
<>heart. The soul is disposed to action with the help of
<>intelligence. In the end it transcends the body, senses
<>and mind and attains Moks2a. In the state of conscious-
<>ness the soul's activities become manifest to us. When
<>in sleep, after deputing “Pra1n2a” for the protection of
<>the body, the soul remains outside the body. The senses
<>become inactive. Therefore we should never wake up
<>one who is asleep. When the soul remains in the body
<>and the senses are active, it is in a most vigilant state.
<>Thus the soul is above the states of wakefulness, dream
<>and slumber. The soul is the divine light of pure
<>energy.
<NI>Janaka who was pleased, gave the sage a thousand
<>cows more. After that Ya1jn5avalkya spoke about libera-
<>tion from worldly bonds.
<NI>Ya1jn5a:--The soul has no connection with the three
<>conditions. Just as the actions in a dream do not affect
<>the soul, the actions in a wakeful state also do not
<>affect it. Just as a fish jumps from one side of the river
<>to the other and back again, life keeps jumping from
<>the state of wakefulness to the state of slumber and vice
<>versa.
<NI>Just as a kite goes up flying and then comes back to its
<>nest, one moves from the state of wakefulness to sleep
<>and back again. In deep slumber, he merges with God, [Page892-b+ 64]
<>but he does not know that. In that state, the father or
<>the mother does not have the knowledge that they are
<>the father or mother.
<NI>After that Ya1jn5avalkya explained the nature of the soul
<>and the various aspects of its working in a state of wake-
<>fulness and of slumber. He added:--“As long as there
<>is “Ka1ma” (desire) there is “Karman” also. Life goes
<>on passing from birth to death and from death to birth.
<>One attains perfection when one is above all desires,
<>and acquires the highest divine knowledge. He is liber-
<>ated from birth and death and becomes immortal.
<NI>By jn5a1na, da1na and tapas we become self-purified and
<>thus become worthy of the highest divine grace. We
<>have to go from the world of Karman (action) to the
<>world of a1tman (soul). One who has acquired Brahma-
<>jn5a1na (knowledge of Brahman) becomes a Bra1hman2a.
<>Hearing these famous teachings of Ya1jn5avalkya, Janaka
<>became his permanent disciple.
<NI>5) {%His end.%} Ya1jn5avalkya had two wives named Kalya1n2i1
<>and Maitreyi1. After giving his all to his wives he passed
<>into immortality. (Br2hada1ran2yaka; M.B. S4a1nt2i Parva,
<>Chapters 3-9).
<NI>6) {%His works.%} Ya1jn5avalkya has written a Smr2ti on the
<>subject of law. “Mita1ks2ara1” is its famous commentary.
<>Mita1ks2ara1 is the highest authoritative treatise on Law
<>in South India.
<NI>7) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(i) Ya1jn5avalkya flourished in Yudhis2t2hira's assembly.
<>(M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 4, Verse 12).
<NI>(ii) He was also a member of Indra's assembly. (M.B.
<>Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 7, Verse 12).
<NI>(iii) Ya1jn5avalkya was the priest at the Ra1jasu1ya Yajn5a
<>of Dharmaputra. (M.B. Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 33,
<>Verse 35).
<NI>(iv) Ya1jn5avalkya had his knowledge of Vedas from

<NI>The rules for Japa, Homa etc. of Yajurveda were taught

<HI>YAMAJIHVA1. A prostitute. In Katha1saritsa1gara,
<>S4aktiyas4olambaka, 1st Taran3ga, there is a story about
<>her as follows:--
<NI>In the city of Citraku1t2a Ratnavarma1 a wealthy Vais4ya,
<>had a son named I1s4varavarma1. In order to avoid his

<>took this task for a reward of 1000 Nis2kas. (a gold coin

<NI>After completing his education on the wiles and tricks

<>and his friend Arthadatta on their way, stopped at a

<>his friend Arthadatta he was about to leave the place

<>caught in her trap and he was forced to spend the res
<>of his money also on her. When he was rendered penni-
<>less the prostitute and her mother deserted him.
<NI>I1s4varavarma1 returned home to his father, empty-hand-
<>ed. Ratnavarma1 took his son to Yamajihva1 again and

<>varma1. She brought her trained monkey named “A1la”

<NI>With this advice Yamajihva1 sent I1s4varavarma1 with the
<>monkey. He went to Ka1n5canapura in the company of

<>accepted her invitation and took up his residence there.
<>One day, he brought the monkey into the house and in
<>the presence of Sundari1 asked him to produce various
<>amounts of money for different purposes.
<NI>The monkey promptly vomited the various amounts
<>separately without making any mistake. Sundari1 and
<>her mother Makarakat2i1 were naturally amazed at the [Page894-a+ 65]
<>marvellous performance of the monkey. I1s4varavarma1

<>purchase the wonderful animal at any price. She request-

<>swallow 2,000 Nis2kas before handing it over to Sundari1

<>trade.
<NI>A1la, the monkey gave Sundari1 1,000 Nis2kas on each of
<>the first two days, but on the third day she was disillu-

<>The enraged monkey attacked both Sundari1 and

<HI>YAMUNA1. Another name for the river Ka1lindi1. (See
<>also under Ka1lindi1).
<NI>1) {%How the water in Ka1lindi1 turned Black:%}-The water in
<>the river Ka1lindi is black in colour. There is a story
<>behind the changing of the colour of this river-water
<>into black. Originally it was clear.
<NI>After the death of Sati1, Daks2a's daughter, S4iva broke
<>up Daks2a ya1ga and went about rambling round the
<>world. Ka1madeva who saw S4iva who had lost his wife
<>(Sati1) shot him with “Unma1da1stra” (an arrow causing
<>madness and excitement). S4iva, in his excitement,
<>always thinking about Sati1, wandered about, finding
<>peace and rest nowhere. In his excited and restless
<>state he plunged himself into Ka1lindi1 river. Just then,
<>the clear water of the river turned black. (Va1mana
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 6).
<NI>2) {%The greatness of Ka1lindi1 (Yamuna1).%} The Pura1n2as
<>declare that by bathing in the holy Ka1lindi1 one attains
<>heaven. There is a stroy illustrating this in Padma
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 30, as follows:--
<NI>Long ago, in Kr2tayuga, there lived in the country of [Page894-b+ 65]
<>Nis2adha, a very wealthy Vais4ya, by name Hemakun2-
<>d2ala. By doing various kinds of business, he had earned
<>eight crores of golden coins. By that time he was advanc-
<>ing in age. At this stage he began to think of the transi-
<>toriness of worldly pleasures and set apart one-sixth of
<>his entire wealth for charitable purposes. He built two
<>temples--one for Vis2n2u and the other for S4iva. He
<>offered daily worship to the gods and spent money in
<>generous hospitality. His two sons, S4ri1kun2d2ala and
<>Vikun2d2ala were growing up. He entrusted the burden
<>of domestic affairs to them and went to the forest to
<>perform austere tapas, after which he attained Vis2n2u-
<>loka.
<NI>His sons, who were intoxicated with their affluent and
<>luxurious life, deviated from the path of virtue and
<>began to indulge in all kinds of sensual pleasures. It
<>did not take long for them to be reduced to utter
<>poverty. Their relatives and dependants left them to
<>their fate and at last, they began to resort to stealing.
<>For fear of the King, the public and the law, they
<>changed their residence to the woods. One of them
<>went to a mountain and the other to a forest. One day,
<>the elder brother was caught by a tiger and the younger
<>was bit by a snake. Both of them died on the same day.
<>Yama's agents took both to Yamaloka. Dharmara1ja
<>(Yama) ordered the elder brother to be sent to Naraka
<>(Hell) and the younger one (Vikun2d2ala) to be sent to
<>Heaven.
<NI>Vikun2d2ala who knew that he did not deserve Heaven
<>by any standards, asked Yama's agent how this had
<>happened. The agent gave him this answer:--“There
<>was a Bra1hman2a well-versed in Vedic lore, who was the
<>son of Harimitra. His a1s4rama was on the southern bank
<>of Yamuna1. While you were living in the forest, you were
<>associated with him. As a result of that contact, it so
<>happened that you bathed for two months in the holy
<>river Ka1lindi1, the waters of which have the power of
<>absolving sinners. By your bath in the first month
<>you were liberated from all sins. By the second you

<>(Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 50).

<HI>YAYA1TI. An eminent king of the Lunar dynasty.
<NI>1.) {%Genealogy and Birth.%} From Vis2n2u were descended
<>in the following order--Brahma1--Atri--Candra--Budha
<>-Puru1ravas--A1yus--Nahus2a--Yaya1ti.
<NI>Sage Atri had three sons--Candra, Durva1sas and Datta1-
<>treya--by his wife Anasu1ya1. Candra's son was Budha,
<>Budha's son was Puru1ravas, Puru1ravas's son was A1yus,
<>A1yus's son was Nahus2a who had six sons--Yati, Yaya1ti,
<>Sam3ya1ti, A1ya1ti, Ayati and Dhruva. Yaya1ti had two
<>wives, Devaya1ni1 and S4armis2t2ha1. Two sons, Yadu and
<>Turvasu, were born to Devaya1ni. Of them, Yadu became
<>the founder of Yaduvam3s4a. S4armis2t2ha1 had three sons
<>who were Druhyu, Anudruhyu and Pu1ru. The Puru-
<>vam3s4a traces its origin to Puru.
<NI>2). {%Yaya1ti's marriage.%} Yaya1ti married Devaya1ni1, the
<>daughter of sage S4ukra and S4armis2t2ha1, the daughter of
<>Vr2s2aparva1.
<NI>There is a story behind this marriage, given in Maha1-
<>bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 76. [Page896-b+ 64]
<>Once there was keen rivalry between Devas and Asuras.
<>For achieving victory Devas accepted Br2haspati and
<>Asuras accepted S4ukra as their preceptors. Although
<>Devas slaughtered all Asuras they were restored to life
<>by S4ukra by the power of his mantra. Devas and Asuras
<>resumed their battle. “Mr2tasan5ji1vani1” (the art of revi-
<>ving the dead) was then unknown to the gods. Devas
<>sent the handsome youth Kaca to S4ukra, to learn
<>Mr2tasan5ji1vani1 from him. There, Kaca fell in love with
<>Devaya1ni1, S4ukra's daughter. But after acquiring the
<>knowledge about Mr2tasan5ji1vani1 he returned, without
<>marrying Devaya1ni1. At the time of his departure, Kaca
<>and Devaya1ni1 cursed each other. Devaya1ni1's curse was
<>that Kaca's newly acquired knowledge would not
<>produce the desired result, while Kaca's curse was that
<>Devaya1ni1 would not be married by any youthful r2s2i.
<NI>S4armis2t2ha1 was the daughter of Vr2s2aparva1, the Daitya
<>king. Devaya1ni1 was the daughter of S4ukra, the Daitya
<>preceptor. One day they were enjoying a bath in a
<>forest brook with their maids, after leaving their clothes
<>on the bank of the stream. At that time Devendra who
<>came that way, transformed himself into a strong wind
<>and carried all the clothes to a place and put all of
<>them in a heap. Seeing this, the women rushed out of
<>the water and in the hurried confusion in snatching
<>their garments Devaya1ni1 and S4armis2t2ha1 happened to
<>put on each other's clothes. A quarrel took place bet-
<>ween them on this mistake and the angry S4armis2t2ha1
<>pushed Devaya1ni1 into an unused well. Thinking that
<>Devaya1ni1 was dead, S4armis2t2ha1 and her maids went to
<>the palace.
<NI>At this time, Yaya1ti who was a king of the lunar dynasty
<>happened to come there exhausted after his hunting.
<>After rescuing Devaya1ni1 from the well and enquiring
<>about her identity and the circumstances in which she
<>fell into the well, he returned to his palace
<NI>Devaya1ni1 sent her maid Ghu1rn2ika1 to her father and in-
<>formed him about her mishap. The father, sage S4ukra
<>became angry towards Vr2s2aparva1, and came down from
<>Daityaloka and settled down in the forest with his dau-
<>ghter. Afraid of his preceptor's curse and anger, Vr2s2a-
<>parva1 came to S4ukra and offered profuse apologies to him
<>for his daughter's misconduct. But S4ukra answered
<>that he would be prepared to forgive only if S4armis2t2ha1
<>with 1000 maids would go and serve Devaya1ni1. Finding
<>no other way, Vr2s2aparva1 yielded to the condition. Soon
<>S4armis2t2ha1 and her 1000 maids went to the forest and
<>began to serve Devaya1ni1. S4ukra returned to Daityaloka.
<>Some time after this, Yaya1ti came to the forest one day
<>for hunting. There he met Devaya1ni1, being attended
<>by many maids. In the conversation that followed, Deva-
<>ya1ni1 was able to recognize Yaya1ti. She fell in love with
<>him. But Yaya1ti insisted that he was not prepared to
<>marry her without the consent of S4ukra1ca1rya. So she
<>sent one of her maids to her father and informed him of
<>her desire. The sage readily agreed and had their
<>marriage performed. He also presented them 1,000
<>maids. After the ceremony, Yaya1ti returned to his
<>palace with his bride. S4armis2t2ha1 and the 2,000
<>maids also accompanied them. (M.B. A1di Parva,
<>Chapters 76-81).
<NI>3) {%Yaya1ti's domestic Life.%} After his arrival in his capital
<>city, Yaya1ti made arrangements for Devaya1ni1's residence
<>in his own palace while S4armis2t2ha1 took up her residence [Page897-a+ 65]
<>in As4okavanika1. In due course, Devaya1ni1 gave birth to
<>a son. The sight of the child roused the motherly inst-
<>incts of S4armis2t2ha1 who spent a thousand years in heart-
<>burning. One day, while she was brooding alone in her
<>garden, Yaya1ti happened to come there. As they met,
<>their hearts were moved by tender feelings. S4armis2t2ha1
<>approached Yaya1ti and begged for a son. But he tried
<>to evade her by pleading that it was not proper to do
<>anything in violation of Dharma. But in the end, he
<>had to yield to her earnest supplications and philosophical
<>arguments. Thus S4armis2t2ha1 conceived a son in secret.
<NI>When the child was born and it grew up, Devaya1ni1's
<>mind was constantly vexed with the thought as to who
<>was the father. Once in an angry mood she questioned
<>S4armis2t2ha1 herself about it. She answered that the
<>child's father was a sage whose identity she could not
<>ascertain in the excitement of her union with him. Any
<>way, this answer satisfied Devaya1ni1.
<NI>Soon after, another son was born to Devaya1ni1. The
<>first boy was named Yadu and the second son, Turvasu.
<>As a result of the secret relations between Yaya1ti and
<>S4armis2t2ha1, three boys were born to her and they were
<>named Druhyu, Anudruhyu and Pu1ru, respectively.
<>These boys also grew up in As4okavanika1.
<NI>Once Devaya1ni1 was in her garden with her husband
<>and children. Just then, S4armis2t2ha1's children also
<>happened to come there. Devaya1ni1 who somehow had
<>her own suspicions, enquired of them their parentage
<>and the innocent children replied that their mother was
<>S4armis2t2ha1 and their father was Yaya1ti. Devaya1ni1 who
<>became furious, immediately went to Asuraloka and
<>informed her father S4ukra about it. On hearing it, the
<>sage who was beside himself with anger owing to his
<>paternal affection, cursed Yaya1ti so that he fell a victim
<>to the infirmities of old age. Yaya1ti entreated S4ukra to
<>withdraw the curse since he had not enjoyed his youth
<>to the full.
<NI>S4ukra lifted the curse by assuring him that one of
<>Yaya1ti's sons would accept his father's old age in ex-
<>change for his youth and the son would be crowned
<>King after Yaya1ti. (M.B. A1di Parva, Chapters 82 and
<>83).
<NI>4) {%Rejuvenation.%} Yaya1ti asked his eldest son Yadu for
<>his youthfulness. But the latter was not prepared to
<>exchange it for his father's old age. Next the father
<>approached his younger sons, Turvasu, Druhyu, and
<>Anudruhyu, with the same request, but all of them
<>in turn refused to oblige him. Even though Yaya1ti
<>promised to return the youthfulness to them after a
<>thousand years, they refused to exchange it for his old
<>age. Yaya1ti in his anger, cursed Yadu that his progeny
<>would never become Kings. Turvasu's family would be
<>completely destroyed and Druhyu would float down the
<>river, with unfulfilled desires and faded prosperity. Pu1ru
<>wholeheartedly accepted his father's old age in exchange
<>for his youth and went to the forest. From that day
<>Yaya1ti began his reign with a cheerful heart. (M.B.
<>A1di parva, Chapters 84 and 85).
<NI>In Padma Pura1n2a, there is a slightly different version
<>about the way in which Yaya1ti fell a victim to the in-
<>firmities of old age. It is as follows:--Seeing the rigorous
<>observance of Yaya1ti's virtuous life, Devendra began to
<>be alarmed at the thought that he might be dislodged
<>from his place. He called his charioteer Ma1tali and
<>ordered him to bring Yaya1ti with him to Indraloka. [Page897-b+ 65]
<>When Ma1tali failed in his mission, Devendra sent some
<>Gandharvas to enact the drama of “Va1mana1vata1ra”
<>before Yaya1ti. Enchanted by the charm and perform-
<>ance of Rati on the stage, the King became irresistibly
<>fascinated by the female sex.
<NI>Once the Ra1ks2asi1 named Jara1 and Madana got access
<>to Yaya1ti's body. While in that state, one day he went
<>to the forest for hunting. There he happened to meet
<>a beautiful girl named “As4rubindumati1”. From her
<>companion-maid, Vis4a1la1, the King came to know of her
<>history. Vis4a1la1 said to Yaya1ti--“Long ago when Ka1ma-
<>deva was burnt Rati was in deep grief. The gods were
<>moved to pity by her tears and they restored Ka1ma to
<>life. Rati who was extremely happy, began to shed
<>tears of joy. A beautiful girl was born from the tears
<>which fell from her left eye. This maid who is standing
<>before you is that girl. She is now looking for a suitable
<>person for her Svayam3vara.” On hearing this story the
<>King expressed his desire to marry her. As4rubindumati1
<>agreed to become his wife on condition that he exchang-
<>ed his old age with some young person for his youth-
<>fulness.
<NI>Yaya1ti returned to his palace and asked each of his sons
<>to give him his youth. Pu1ru alone fulfilled his father's
<>desire. After this Yaya1ti married As4rubindumati1. But he
<>was compelled to submit to another condition also, that
<>he should not maintain any contact with his other wives.
<>Devaya1ni1 and S4armis2t2ha1 naturally, became distressed
<>and furious when they saw Yaya1ti and As4rubindumati1
<>as husband and wife. So Yaya1ti asked Yadu to kill both
<>of them. But Yadu refused to obey him. Yaya1ti who
<>became angry at this, cursed his son that one of his
<>descendants would marry his uncle's daughter and
<>become heir to his mother's wealth.
<NI>After some time, on Menaka1's advice, As4rubindumati1
<>urged Yaya1ti to go on a visit to Heaven. So he entrust-
<>ed his kingdom to Pu1ru and went to Vaikun2t2ha.
<NI>5) {%Yaya1ti's reign.%} The Pura1n2as describe Yaya1ti as a
<>noble and eminent King. Some of the events which took
<>place during his reign and which deserve special praise,
<>are given below:
<NI>(i) {%Gift of cows.%} Once when King Yaya1ti was in the
<>company of his subjects, a Bra1hman2a approached him
<>with a request for Gurudaks2in2a1. At once Yaya1ti gave
<>him a gift of 1,000 cows. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter
<>195).
<NI>(ii) {%Yaya1ti and Ga1lava.%} See para 6 under Ga1lava.
<NI>(iii) {%His end.%} After a thousand years, Yaya1ti handed
<>over his kingship to Pu1ru and went to the forest. He
<>spent many years living in Bhr2gutun3ga in the company
<>of sages, and feeding on fruits, roots, etc. and in the end
<>died in the forest and attained Heaven.
<NI>(iv) {%Yaya1ti's fall from Heaven.%} Yaya1ti remained in Heaven
<>for many years. Once he insulted the Devas, Indra and
<>other gods and kings in the divine assembly. The gods
<>looked at one another, wondering who this proud fellow
<>was. They asked how this man reached Heaven without
<>renouncing his pride. In the meantime Yaya1ti lost his
<>balance and fell down from Heaven to the earth. While
<>descending, he prayed that he should fall in the midst of
<>good men.
<NI>Just at this time, four kings named Pratardana, Vasu-
<>manas, S4ibi and As2t2aka were performing a Ya1ga in
<>Naimis2a1ran2ya. Yaya1ti happened to fall head down-
<>wards into their midst. But since they caught him in [Page898-a+ 64]
<>his fall he did not crash to the ground. He explained
<>his story to them. They allowed him to accept the
<>fruit of their Yajn5a and to go to Heaven. But since he
<>was a Ks2atriya he told them he had no right to it. Just
<>then, his daughter Ma1dhavi1 arrived there. She allowed
<>him to take half of the divine grace which she had earned
<>and with the help of it Yaya1ti ascended to Heaven
<>again. (M.B. Udyoga Parva, Chapter 120).
<NI>6) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(i) Yaya1ti witnessed the battle between Kauravas and
<>Pa1n2d2avas which was fought as a result of the theft of
<>King Vira1t2a's cows. (M.B. Vira1t2a Parva, Chapter 56,
<>Verse 9).
<NI>(ii) In Maha1bha1rata, Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 8, Verse
<>8, it is stated that King Yaya1ti continues to be in
<>Yama's assembly.
<NI>(iii) Yaya1ti once gave 1,000 cows to a Bra1hman2a as
<>“Gurudaks2in2a1”. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter 195).
<NI>(iv) He came riding in Indra's chariot and witnessed
<>the battle between Arjuna and Kr2pa1ca1rya, from the
<>sky. (M.B. Vira1t2a Parva, Chapter 56, Verse 9).
<NI>(v) Maha1bha1rata, Udyoga Parva, Chapter 115, men
<>tions that Yaya1ti had conducted 1,000 Ya1gas and that
<>he was the leader of all Kings.
<HI>YAYA1TIPATANA. A holy place. Maha1bha1rata, Vana
<>Parva, Chapter 82, Verse 48, mentions that a visit to
<>this place will give the same reward as an As4vamedha
<>Ya1ga.
<HI>YA1YA1VARA(S). A particular section of Brahmins.
<>Their special feature is that they wander about here

<HI>YODHYA. A country in ancient India. In Maha1-

<HI>YOGA I. A sage who was the grandson of Sva1yambhuva
<>Manu and son of S4raddha1. Yoga was born to S4raddha1
<>by Dharma. (Bha1gavata, 4th Skandha).
<HI>YOGA II. It is Jn5a1na that reveals Brahman. Yoga is the
<>concentration on Brahman. Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 372,
<>mentions that Yoga is the perfect union of Jiva1tma1 and
<>Parama1tma1.
<NI>In fact Yoga is activity with the body as its basis. It
<>may be doubted how a mere physical karman helps
<>Parabrahmada1rs4ana. But Mahars2is assert that there
<>is an inseparable connection between matter and soul.
<NI>Matter and soul are merely the two phases of the
<>single “Astitva”. The object of Yoga is to achieve
<>union with Parabrahman; in other words, to attain
<>Moks2a. The recitation of mantras is important in many
<>disciplines connected with the practice of Yoga. The
<>letters and words of mantras are so arranged that their
<>recitation in the proper manner produces profound and
<>favourable effects in the body. The habitual recitation
<>of mantras helps the practice of Yoga.
<NI>There are two broad divisions of Yoga--Hat2ha Yoga
<>and Ra1ja Yoga. The two are interconnected and have
<>to be practised simultaneously. Hat2ha Yoga is mainly
<>concerned with disciplining the body by various kinds
<>of exercises. Standing in Pan5ca1gni with the arms held
<>aloft; standing on one leg or standing on the head are
<>some of these exercises. Ra1ja Yoga aims at arousing
<>Prajn5a1 by control of the senses. [Page898-b+ 64]
<>Ra1ja Yoga involves the practice of eight disciplines.
<>They are:--Yama, Niyama, A1sana, Pra1n2a1ya1ma,
<>Pratya1ha1ra, Dha1ran2a1, Dhya1na and Sama1dhi. An
<>account of each of these is given below:--
<NI>i) {%Yama.%} Yama means refraining from evil courses or
<>sinful things like cruelty or dishonesty. Yama demands
<>the practice of Ahim3sa1, satya, asteya, brahmacarya and
<>aparigraha. (Ahim3sa1 = non-violence or not killing any
<>creature. Satya = truth; practising truth in thoughts,
<>words and deeds. Asteya = not stealing. Brahmacarya =
<>sexual continence. Aparigraha = not coveting wealth or
<>pleasures which are not absolutely necessary).
<NI>ii) {%Niyama.%} While Yama is a negative discipline,
<>Niyama is a positive one. It requires the positive practice
<>of virtuous courses. Niyama includes five things:--
<>S4auca (cleanliness), Santos2a (contentment), Tapas
<>(penance and austerities), Sva1dhya1ya (study of the
<>Vedas and incantations or mantras) and I1s4varapran2i-
<>dha1na (prayer and meditating on God). S4auca means
<>not only cleanliness of the body, but also of the mind.
<>The mind must be purged of all ‘malas’ like ka1ma,
<>krodha and lobha. Tapas means ability to endure
<>opposites (dvandvas) like heat and cold or joy and
<>sorrow. Sva1dhya1ya is “study of philosophical works.”
<>I1s4varapran2idha1na--dedicating all actions unto God.
<NI>iii) {%A1sana.%} The way of sitting or posture. Different
<>postures have been prescribed for different actions.
<>First of all, the yogi1 must choose a suitable place.
<NI>(The place must be level, neat, free from stones, fire or
<>gravel, agreeable to the mind and not causing irritation
<>to the eyes). (S4veta1s4vatara).
<NI>Different a1sanas like Padma1sana, Svastika1sana and
<>Siddha1sana have been prescribed for the practice of
<>Yoga.
<NI>Putting the left leg on the right thigh and the right leg
<>on the left thigh, holding the right toe with the left
<>hand and the left toe with the right hand, place the
<>chin on the chest and look at the tip of the nose. This
<>is Padma1sana.
<NI>Sitting upright with the feet tucked under the hips is
<>Svastika1sana.
<NI>Placing the left leg under the genitals, put the right
<>leg on it. Insert the chin into the pit of the throat;
<>control the senses; with steady eyes look at the point
<>between the eye-brows. This is Siddha1sana which will
<>break open the doors to Moks2a.
<NI>iv) {%Pra1n2a1ya1ma.%} This is regulation of breathing. Inhale
<>air through the left nostril, retain the air in the lungs
<>for a few minutes and then exhale it through the right
<>nostril. The process may be reversed--that is inhala-
<>tion through the right nostril and exhalation through
<>the left. This is the first step in Pra1n2a1ya1ma. Exhaling
<>the breath and then not inhaling for some time is
<>called recaka; the opposite process is called pu1raka.
<>The object of pra1n2a1ya1ma is to awaken Kun2d2alini1.
<NI>v) {%Pratya1ha1ra.%} This is a process of withdrawing the
<>five senses from the outer world. A man who practises
<>Pratya1ha1ra becomes oblivious of the outer world. This
<>helps the concentration of the mind on the A1tman.
<NI>vi) {%Dha1ran2a1.%} Withdrawing the mind from outer objects
<>and concentrating it on the self.
<NI>vii) {%Dhya1na.%} Meditation, concentrating the mind on
<>several places like Bhru1madhya and na1bhi1cakra. Re-
<>peating the sacred syllable OM is very useful in dhya1na. [Page899-a+ 65]
<NI>viii) {%Sama1dhi.%} In Sama1dhi the soul and mind unite,
<>the mind merging into the soul. Sama1dhi is defined
<>thus:--
<NI>When the mind and soul unite just as salt and water
<>unite, that state is called Sama1dhi.
<HI>YOGAMA1YA1DEVI1. The form of Devi1 in the state of
<>Yoga. It was Yogama1ya1devi1 who transferred the seventh

<HI>YOGI1S4VARA(S). A community of famous yogins.

<>this community. They were the sons of the sage R2s2a-
<>bha and used to go about naked, everywhere. This

<HI>YONIDVA1RA. A sacred place on the Udaya mountain.
<>(Maha1bha1rata, Vana Parva, Chapter 84, Verse 95).

<HI>YOTIMATSAKA (POTIMATSAKA). A King in
<>ancient India. There is a passage in Maha1bha1rata,

<HI>YUDDHA (WAR). (i) In ancient times in India war
<>was considered a “Ra1jadharma”. A war declared
<>under this law was known as “Dharmayuddha”
<NI>(ii) It is forbidden to use a weapon describing it falsely
<>as another weapon. The use of arrows heated in fire, is
<>also against Dharmayuddha.
<NI>(iii) It is also against the rules of Dharmayuddha to
<>kill a person who gets down from the chariot, a eunuch,
<>one with joined palms, one who squats on the ground,
<>one who seeks refuge, one who is asleep, one who is
<>naked, an unarmed person, one who has come to wit-
<>ness the fight, one who is fighting with another, one
<>whose weapon is broken, one who is bereaved by the
<>death of a son or other relative, one who is vanquished,
<>one who flees from battle, and one who refuses to attack
<>in return etc.
<NI>(iv) If a warrior fleeing from battlefield is killed by his
<>enemy, he carries with him his master's sins.
<NI>(v) All the grace earned by the young man who flees
<>from battle, passes to his master.
<NI>(vi) The soldier himself may take all booty in the
<>battle except chariots, horses, elephants, umbrellas,
<>wealth, corn, cows, women, weapons, silver and gold.
<NI>(vii) All costly articles, seized in battle, such as gold,
<>silver, jewels etc. are to be handed over to the King,
<>according to the Vedas. (Manusmr2ti, Chapter 7).
